The Impossibility of Certainty in Metaphysical Premises
Author critiques certainty in metaphysical premises and logicians' belief definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's infinite regress argument against the logicians' definition of certain belief and identify its premises.

Core Idea
If certainty requires a further belief, an infinite regress destroys it.

In this passage, the author attacks the claim that metaphysical premises can yield certainty comparable to mathematics. He presents a critical argument that the logicians' own definition of belief leads to an infinite regress, making certainty impossible in any belief system. This challenges the very foundation of demonstration in divine sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target definition
Find in the passage where the logicians define 'sound belief' as certain belief of the second degree, i.e., belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. This is the definition the author attacks.
2
Formulate the infinite regress
If the second-degree belief is itself certain, it must be justified by a third-degree belief according to the same definition, leading to an infinite chain. Recognize this as the core of the regress argument.
3
Consider the alternative
If the second-degree belief is not certain, then the first belief cannot be certain either, since it relies on an uncertain foundation. This is the second horn of the dilemma.
4
Draw the conclusion
Either way, certainty cannot be attained. The logicians' definition is self-defeating. This critique undermines claims of certainty in metaphysical and theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و با مجری جوادی الله مثله الثالث هب لنا ساعدنا ع ان العربات الواحد نصف . الانتين وما تجرى مجراه علم ضرورى غير قابل للاحتمالات لكنكمية المباحث الحكمية والمعالم الطبيعية والالهية لا تستعملون هذه المقدمات بل انما تستعملون مقدمات اح كقولكم الممكن المشاوي لا يتح احد هل فيها الآخر الأمرح وان حكم التي حكم شله واذا انتهى كلامكم الى هذه المقدمات واشبا هما ادعيم الضرورة والجزم ومعلوم أن هذه القضاب البسة قوة قولنا الواحد نصف الأثنين ولذلك، إذ اعضا النوعين على العقل لم يكن الجزم باحد هما كا الجزم بالآخر والتفاوت في العلوم التقنية غير جابر لانه ان حصل الأحمال ولو على ابعد الوجوه المحصل اليقين والالم محصل التفاوت اصلا فعلمنالات المقدمات المستعملة في هذه العلوم غير القيلية ولذلك نقل بعض المتاخرين عن انشطاطا ليس له قال الأمور الالهية لا يمكن تحصيل الحرم منها فانها المقصد الأقصى تحصيل الاعتقاد السبيل الاخلاق والأنتما اتابع بعد ان جمهور المنطقيين اعتدوا في البقر مع الأعتقاد المطابق عفادل الجازم ثانيا وهو اعتقادانه لا يمكن ان يكون الأمرا لاكما اعتقدوا لنا فالبرهان يقتضى اعتبار هذا الاعتقادة التقنى والاً وجب از يكون اعتقاد المقار علما و اذا ثبت ذلك فنقول ذلك الاعتقاد الثاني إن كان بقنا وجب ان عصل فيه اعتقاد ثالث متعلق بان الأمر ليس الانكما تعلق الاعتقال الثاني وجنيد يكون الكلام فيه كالكلام في الاول ولزم الناك وان لم كان يقينا والاعتقاد الاول انا بصير بقينا عند حصوله والمبني على ما لا يكون من يقينيا لا يكون يقينيا فوجب ان لا يكون تحصل اليقين في شي من الاعتقادا وذلك قادح ع الاقليات
And by the generality of the third like God grant us help from that the one half of two and what runs its course is necessary knowledge not subject to probabilities, but the metaphysical investigations and natural and divine sciences do not use these premises; rather they use premises like your statement 'the possible does not...' and when your speech ends at these premises and their likes, they claim necessity and certainty. It is known that these propositions lack the strength of our statement 'one is half of two'. Therefore, when the two types are presented to the mind, certainty in one is not like certainty in the other. The difference in practical sciences is not compensatory, because if probability occurs even in the most distant ways, it does not produce certainty, and probability does not produce difference at all. Thus the premises used in these sciences are not certain. Therefore some later scholars reported from a [؟] that he said: 'Divine matters cannot achieve certainty therein, for their utmost goal is to attain belief for the sake of morals and following.' Then he followed: 'After that, the majority of logicians have regarded sound belief as certain belief of the second degree, which is the belief that the matter cannot be otherwise than as they believed. The demonstration requires consideration of this technical belief; otherwise the belief of the muqallid would have to be knowledge. And when that is established, we say: that second belief, if it is certain, then a third belief must arise in it related to the matter not being as the second belief relates, and the discussion would be like the first, and infinite regress follows. And if it is not certain, then the first belief becomes certain upon its occurrence, and what is based on what is not certain cannot be certain. Therefore certainty cannot be attained in any of the beliefs, and that is a defect in the certainties. Fifth, your book this passes over much, meaning the book of logic.'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the claim that metaphysical and natural sciences can achieve the same degree of certainty as mathematics. The author begins by stating that mathematical truths like 'one is half of two' are necessary and certain. In contrast, the premises used in philosophy and theology are not of this kind; they are probabilistic or based on assumptions. When these premises are presented to the mind, one does not feel the same certainty as in mathematics. The author then addresses a common defense: that even if such premises are not certain, they still yield practical knowledge. He refutes this by noting that probability, no matter how small, cannot produce certainty. Then he reports a view from later scholars that in divine matters, the goal is not certainty but belief (i'tiqad) for ethical purposes. The author then critiques the logicians' definition of belief as 'certain belief of the second degree,' i.e., belief that the matter cannot be otherwise. He argues that if such a belief requires a further belief to be certain, an infinite regress follows. If the second belief is not certain, then the first belief cannot be certain either. This argument undermines the possibility of certainty in any belief, thus criticizing the foundations of logical demonstration in these fields.

Key Terms
اليقين
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot metaphysical premises achieve the same certainty as mathematical premises?
A) Because they are not necessary
B) Because they are based on probability and lack the strength of axioms like 'one is half of two'
C) Because they are not taught in schools
D) Because they are about divine matters which are unknowable

Key Takeaway
The author's infinite regress argument shows that the logicians' definition of certain belief is self-defeating, rendering certainty unattainable in any belief system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the infinite regress in the following argument: 'I know that the Qur'an is true because my imam told me. My imam is reliable because other scholars said so. Those scholars are reliable because...' Explain why this leads to a regress and whether the author's critique applies.
?
Construct your own infinite regress argument against the claim that 'every belief must be supported by a certain belief.' Use the author's method from the passage.

Infinity in Past, Future, and Continuous Quantities
Author critiques views on infinity in past, future, and continuous quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptual and actual infinity in past events, future events, and continuous quantities, and identify the conditions under which infinity can be attributed.

Core Idea
Infinity is a conceptual privation, not an actual existent—its attribution depends on the nature of the quantity.

This passage from the Mulaqqiṣ al-Rāzī examines how infinity (al-lā-nihāya) applies to past events, future events, and continuous quantities like motion. The author argues that the sum of past individuals never exists as a whole, so it cannot be an actual infinite number. Future events are only potential, and continuous quantities with order can admit infinity in a certain sense. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for later discussions on the eternity of the world and other metaphysical topics in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two judgments on past events
The author presents two possible judgments about the sum of past individuals: negation (salb) – that it is not a number (true), and modification (ʿudūl) – that it is an infinite number (false). Recognize that the sum never exists as a whole in actuality.
2
Understand why the sum cannot be actual infinite
The subject of the predicate must exist; the sum of past individuals never exists at any moment. Additionally, the intellect cannot actually summon an infinite number. Therefore, infinity here is only conceptual.
The phrase 'al-ʿaql lā yaqwa ʿalā istiḥḍār ʿidda lā nihāya lahā bi l-fiʿl' – the intellect cannot actually summon an infinite number.
3
Analyze future events
Future events are not actual but potential. Each future event will exist at its specific time, but the whole never exists. Thus, infinity in future events is potential, not actual.
4
Distinguish quantities with order vs without order
The author mentions that philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ) distinguish quantities with order (tartīb) in position (e.g., dimensions) or in nature (e.g., souls) from those without order. In ordered quantities, infinity can be considered; in unordered quantities like past events, it cannot.
5
Examine the weakness of the 'increase and decrease' argument
The common argument that every number admitting increase/decrease must be finite is deemed weak because the notion of an end (nihāya) only applies to quantities with order. In unordered quantities, no 'covering' occurs, so infinity is not precluded.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is attached to the statement of the material of the existence of the beloved. They are two infinites encompassing the past affairs. So when we say a thousand finite, we do not mean each one of them but their sum. The judgment of existence is either negation, which is that the sum of past individuals is not something having a number—this is true—or modification, which is that the sum of past individuals are a number infinite—this is false, because the subject of the negative predicate must be [something] and the sum of past individuals never exists in any state, and also because the intellect cannot actually summon an infinite number. Indeed, it can summon the infinite because it is from one and a single [repetition?] ... and as for future matters, the consideration is either regarding their existence or their ending. As for their existence, they are not actual but potential. As for saying that each one of them [exists] at a specific time, that is correct, but in all times it is false. Or it is said that the whole, insofar as it is a whole, is described as such that some of it exists and some is non-existent, and this is false because there is no existence for that whole to be described with a positive description ... and that whole which is non-existent ... each one of them is perfectly [existent] according to a specific time, even if it is not actual from it ... and its ending is an ending that is actually infinite relative to the upper limit upon it, and potentially relative to what is not yet obtained and will be obtained, and neither potential nor actual relative to the limit upon it to the limit after which there is no limit. Indeed, infinity is not among the principles, because the mere concept of infinity prevents it from being ... it is a mental consideration, not an independently existent entity, let alone being ... that infinity is ... because the nature of power from it ... and the volume that ... screws with rational minds ... potentially ... and it is known that it is sensible to the lower world ... and it is correct insofar as it is actually existent ... and ... potential ... indeed this was always potential ... and the end prevents the mover from moving to prevent it from being infinite from the side of the mover to it, because there is no void to move into, nor from the side of the limit from which it is moved; otherwise it would have an end from that side. He said: every [quantity] that admits increase and decrease must be finite. The philosophers said: every [quantity] that has order in position, like dimensions, or in nature, like souls, then entering infinity into it is possible. And everything not like that, either it is not collected, like past events, or it is collected but not by strength of nature or position, like human souls—then entering infinity into it is possible. And what is said: every number admits increase and decrease, and everything that does so is finite—this is weak, because the limit which is the greatest boundary either is meant as ending at a point beyond which nothing remains, and that is only realized in what has order in nature or position. For when a line is applied to the line, each degree takes over from the previous one, so the later one is applied to the other, so inevitably the excess appears as a surplus over what covers [the previous], but in what has no order in position or nature, such covering is not possible, so the excess does not end where its parts remain without cover, so the ending in this sense is not realized. But what is obtained in the excess is also obtained in the deficit, so there remains no difference between the subject of the motion and its predicate except a third matter, so it must be explained and discussed. And what we intended is an investigation of the judgments of quantity insofar as it is; it is necessary to mention the judgments of its divisions. As for the discrete, its proof, judgments, and divisions have preceded. As for the continuous, its judgment in time is through motion, so we will mention here the common judgments among the remaining three—they are four. Then each of them is such that quantity cannot be separated from matter, because being such is not for its essence, and colors are from it, otherwise every quantity would be such. That is, it is said: it is susceptible to increase and decrease for increase and decrease, and there is no good benefit.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical discussion about the concept of infinity (اللانهاية) and its application to different kinds of quantities: past events, future events, and continuous quantities like dimensions and motion. The author begins by distinguishing the judgment of existence for past events: either we say the sum of past individuals is not a number (true) or it is an infinite number (false). The reason given is that the sum of past events never exists as a whole at any moment; it is only conceptually considered. The intellect cannot actually conceive an infinite set, but it can conceive infinity as a concept from a single repetition. For future events, they are potential, not actual; each future event will exist at its specific time. The author then critiques the view that a collection of past events can be described as partly existent and partly non-existent, arguing that there is no actual existence for that collection. Then the discussion shifts to continuous quantities: dimensions, motion, time. The author mentions that philosophers (الحكماء) distinguish between quantities that have order in position (like spatial dimensions) or order in nature (like souls) in which infinity can enter, versus those without such order (like past events) or with order but not of that type (like human souls). The common argument that every number admits increase and decrease and thus must be finite is deemed weak. The author explains that the notion of an end (حد) as a point beyond which nothing remains only applies to quantities with order (positional or natural). In such quantities, when a line is applied to a line, each part covers the previous, so an excess appears. But in quantities without order, no such covering occurs, so infinity can be conceived. The passage concludes by stating that the common judgments of the three remaining types of quantity (discrete, continuous, and perhaps another) need to be mentioned, and that quantity cannot exist separated from matter. This passage clarifies the nuanced understanding of infinity in the Aristotelian-Islamic tradition, emphasizing that infinity is primarily a privation or potential concept, not an actual positive attribute.

Key Terms
اللانهاية
النهاية
الكم
المتصل
المنفصل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it false to say that the sum of past individuals is an infinite number?
A) Because past individuals are finite in number.
B) Because the sum of past individuals never exists as a whole at any moment, and the intellect cannot hold an actual infinite set.
C) Because each past individual is infinite in itself.
D) Because the concept of infinity is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Infinity is not a positive attribute but a privation; its application depends on whether the quantity has order and whether the whole exists in actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In the kalam argument for the temporal origination of the world, the premise is that past events cannot be infinite. Using the author's analysis, explain why the sum of past individuals cannot be an actual infinite series.
?
2. Consider the category of continuous quantity: time. If time is infinite in the past, is that an actual or potential infinity? Apply the author's distinction that only quantities with order (like time, which has a natural order) can admit infinity in a certain sense.

Refutation of Void by Motion-Speed Argument
Critique of void via motion-speed comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument against void and identify the objection that repels the impossibility.

Core Idea
A proportionality-based argument against void reveals a contradiction, but a refined analysis of time components saves the day.

How do we know the void (khalā') cannot exist? One powerful argument in Islamic natural philosophy uses the relationship between motion speed and medium density. This passage from Razi's Mulakhkhas sets up a hypothetical comparison between motion in void and in water, then shows that if void existed, an absurd consequence would follow. However, Razi also anticipates an objection that dissects the time of motion into two components, thereby repelling the absurdity. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate conditional arguments in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothetical scenario
The author asks us to suppose a mover traverses 10 cubits in void in 1 hour, and the same distance in water in 10 hours. The fineness of void is 10 times that of water.
2
Extract the proportionality principle
If time of motion decreases with increasing fineness of medium, then in a medium 10 times finer than water, the time should be 1/10 of the time in water — i.e., 1 hour, same as in void.
3
Recognise the contradiction
Motion with an obstacle (water) would take the same time as motion without any obstacle (void), which is impossible. Therefore void cannot exist.
4
Analyse the objection
An objector distinguishes between time due to the essence of motion and time due to resistance. In void all time is for motion; in a medium part is for motion and part for resistance. This resolves the apparent equality.
5
Evaluate the argument structure
The original argument is a conditional: if void exists, then an absurdity follows, so void does not exist. The objection denies that the consequent is absurd by splitting the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلْيُفْرَضْ أَنَّ الْمُتَحَرِّكَ قَطَعَ عَشَرَةَ أَذْرُعٍ مِنَ الْخَلَاءِ فِي سَاعَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَقَطَعَ مِثْلَ هَذِهِ الْمَسَافَةِ الْمَلِيئَةَ مِنَ الْمَاءِ فِي عَشْرِ سَاعَاتٍ. وَلْيُنْفَضْ كُلًا مِنَ الْخَلَاءِ وَالْمَاءِ حَيْثُ يَكُونُ رِقَّتُهُ أَزْيَدَ مِنْ رِقَّةِ الْمَاءِ عَشْرَ مَرَّاتٍ. فَإِذَا كَانَ صِغَرُ زَمَانِ الْحَرَكَةِ يَحْدُثُ بِزِيَادَةِ دِقَّةِ الْمُتَحَرِّكِ فِيهِ، فَمِنْ وَاجِبٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ زَمَانُ الْحَرَكَةِ فِي هَذَا الْمَلَاءِ الرَّقِيقِ عُشْرَ زَمَانِ الْحَرَكَةِ فِي الْمَاءِ، وَهُوَ سَاعَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ. فَتَكُونُ زَمَانُ الْحَرَكَةِ فِي هَذَا الْمَلَاءِ الرَّقِيقِ مِثْلَ زَمَانِ الْحَرَكَةِ فِي الْخَلَاءِ. فَيَكُونُ الْحَرَكَةُ مَعَ الْعَائِقِ كَالْحَرَكَةِ بِلَا عَائِقٍ، وَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ. وَلِقَائِلٍ أَنْ يَقُولَ: الْحَرَكَةُ مَا هِيَ هِيَ إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مُفْتَقِرَةً إِلَى الْآلَةِ أَوْ لَا تَكُونَ. وَالثَّانِي بَاطِلٌ. وَأَمَّا الْأَوَّلُ فَلِأَنَّ الْحَرَكَةَ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ فِي الْأَفْلَاكِ الْعَالِيَةِ لَا عَائِقَ لَهَا، فَيَكُونُ وُقُوعُهَا فِي كُلِّ زَمَانٍ مِثْلَ الْأَوَّلِ. فَإِذَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ، فَمَنْقُولُ الْحَرَكَةِ مَسَافَةَ عَشَرَةِ أَذْرُعٍ يَسْتَدْعِي قِدَدًا مِنَ الزَّمَانِ. وَقَدْ دَلَّ آخَرُ مِنَ النَّقْدِ بِسَبَبِ طُولِ الْمَسَافَةِ مِنَ الْعَائِقِ. وَالزَّمَانُ الْمُسْتَحِقُّ بِتَعَبِ طُولِ الْمَسَافَةِ مِنَ الْعَائِقِ هُوَ الَّذِي نَقَصَ بِسَبَبِ الطَّاقَةِ مَا فِيهَا مِنَ الْجِسْمِ، وَيُعْلَمُ بِسَبَبِ كَثَافَةِ مَا فِيهَا. وَإِذَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ، فَقَوْلُ الْحَرَكَةِ الْخَلَائِيَّةِ وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى سَاعَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَفِي الزَّمَانِ الَّذِي تَحْتَهَا هَذَا الْقَدْرُ مِنَ الْحَرَكَةِ مَا هِيَ هِيَ وَاقِعَةٌ لِلْمَلَاءِ الَّذِي نِسْبَتُهُ مِنْ دِقَّةِ الْمَاءِ بَعْدَ مَا اتَّ ... فَإِنَّ الْحَرَكَةَ فِيهِ نَفَعٌ فِي سَاعَةٍ وَعَشْرِ سَاعَاتٍ. أَمَّا السَّاعَةُ فَبِسَبَبِ أَصْلِ الْحَرَكَةِ، وَأَمَّا عَشْرُ السَّاعَاتِ فَبِسَبَبِ طُولِ الْمَسَافَةِ مِنَ الْعَائِقِ. وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ، فَالْحَالُ الَّذِي زَعَمْتُمُوهُ إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ لَوْ جَعَلْنَا الزَّمَانَ كُلَّهُ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الْعَائِقِ. أَمَّا لَوْ جَعَلْنَا بَعْضَهُمْ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الْحَرَكَةِ وَبَعْضَهُمْ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ الْعَائِقِ، كَانَتِ الْحَرَكَةُ الْخَلَائِيَّةُ وَاقِعَةً فِي الزَّمَانِ الَّذِي يَحِقُّ الْحَرَكَةُ لِمَا هِيَ هِيَ، وَالْحَرَكَةُ الْمَلَائِيَّةُ كَيْفَ كَانَتْ وَاقِعَةً فِي ذَلِكَ الزَّمَانِ مَعَ مِقْدَارٍ آخَرَ مِنَ الزَّمَانِ يَحِقُّ بِسَبَبِ طُولِ الْمَسَافَةِ مِنَ الْمُعَاوِقَةِ. فَانْدَفَعَ الْمُحَالُ.
Let it be supposed that the mover traverses ten cubits in void in one hour, and traverses the same distance filled with water in ten hours. Let both void and water be such that its fineness is ten times greater than the fineness of water. Since the smallness of the time of motion occurs by increase of fineness of the medium, it necessarily follows that the time of motion in this fine medium would be one-tenth of the time of motion in water, which is one hour. Thus the time of motion in this fine medium equals the time of motion in void. Hence motion with an obstacle would be like motion without an obstacle, which is impossible. And an objector may say: Motion itself either requires a medium or not. The second is false. As for the first, because motion in the celestial spheres has no obstacle, so its occurrence in each time is like the first. If so, then the measure of motion over a distance of ten cubits requires portions of time. And another indication from the critique due to the length of the distance from the obstacle. The time required by the difficulty of the length of the distance from the obstacle is that which is diminished due to the power in it of the body, and is known by the density of what is in it. If so, then the statement that motion in void occurs in one hour, and in the time under it this amount of motion occurs as it is, and it occurs for the medium whose proportion of fineness to water after it... Then the motion in it is beneficial in one hour and ten hours. As for the hour, it is due to the essence of motion; as for the ten hours, due to the length of the distance from the obstacle. In summary, the situation you claimed would only be complete if we made all the time in opposition to the obstacle. But if we made part of it in opposition to motion and part in opposition to the obstacle, then motion in void would occur in the time that is due for motion as such, and motion in a medium would occur in that time along with another amount of time due to the length of the distance from the resistance. Thus the impossibility is repelled.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents a critical argument against the existence of void (khalā') using the phenomenon of motion. The author begins by setting up a hypothetical: suppose a moving object traverses ten cubits in void in one hour, and the same distance in water (a filled medium) in ten hours. The fineness (riqqa) of void is assumed to be ten times that of water. Since the time of motion decreases as the medium becomes finer, the time in a medium ten times finer than water should be one-tenth of the time in water, i.e., one hour – exactly the time in void. This leads to the contradiction that motion with an obstacle (water) takes the same time as motion without any obstacle (void), which is absurd. Thus, void cannot exist.

The author then anticipates an objection: motion might require a medium (āla) or not. The objector argues that motion in celestial spheres has no obstacle, so each part of motion occurs in equal time. Based on this, the time for a given distance is determined by the obstacle's resistance. The objector attempts to salvage the argument by distinguishing between the time due to motion itself and the extra time due to overcoming resistance. In void, all time is due to motion itself; in a medium, part of the time is for motion and part for overcoming resistance. Thus, the contradiction is resolved: motion in void takes one hour, while motion in water takes one hour plus ten hours for resistance. The author concludes that the impossibility is removed, but only by properly separating the two components of time. This shows that the original argument against void fails if we analyze the time correctly. The passage demonstrates a sophisticated logical and physical critique, using conditional reasoning and the principle of proportionality.

Key Terms
الخلاء
الملاء
الرقاق/الرقة
العائق
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Motion in void would be instantaneous.
B) Motion with an obstacle would take the same time as motion without an obstacle.
C) Motion in water would be faster than in void.
D) The fineness of void would be less than that of water.

Key Takeaway
The original argument against void uses a modus tollens, but the objection refines the analysis of time, showing that the absurd consequence does not actually follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Analyzing a Dialectical Doubt in Optics
Author raises a doubt about secondary light via dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to dissect a dialectical doubt (shakk) into its constituent dichotomous choices and identify the logical consequences of each horn.

Core Idea
A doubt tests a theory by exposing the contradictions in each possible answer.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhas shows how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses a doubt (shakk) to probe a theory about secondary light. The doubt is structured as a dichotomy: either the air receives the light-form or it does not. By examining each possibility, al-Razi reveals explanatory problems. This method is widely used in Islamic theology and philosophy to refine positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt's target claim
Find the claim being tested. Here, it is the theory that secondary light is not caused by reflection but by the air receiving light-form.
2
Extract the dichotomous choices
The doubt presents two exclusive options: (1) air becomes qualified by the light-form, or (2) it does not. List them clearly.
3
Follow the consequences for each horn
For option (1): why is the air not luminous next to a wall? For option (2): why are walls not uniformly colored? Trace the reasoning.
The consequences must logically follow from the assumption.
4
Assess the doubt's force
Determine whether the doubt effectively refutes the target claim or only creates a tension. Does it force a revision?
Not all doubts are knockdown arguments; some aim to show a need for additional explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن ههنا شكا: وهو أن الهواء أما إن يتكيف بكيفية الصورة الضوئية أو لا. فإن تكيف بها فلم يخل الهواء مضيا ما حاذى الجدار حال كونه بالضوء؟ وإن لم يتكيف بها لكن خانه ما، فامتنع أن نقول: إن الجدران يكون لها لون ضعيف أضعف من الماء والأحجار المشقة، فعاله من اللون الضعيف يقبل الشر والضعف، وذلك الكون يضعف ما فيه من الضوء.
Then there is a doubt: that the air either becomes qualified by the quality of the light-form or not. If it becomes qualified, then why is the air not luminous alongside the wall while it is in the light? And if it does not become qualified but rather fails, then it is impossible to say that the walls have a weak color weaker than water and polished stones, the effect of which from the weak color accepts evil and weakness, and that being weakens what is in it of light.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical doubt (shakk) raised by the author. It examines whether the air itself receives the form of light or not. The argument proceeds by dichotomy: either the air becomes qualified by the light-form, or it does not. If it does, then why is the air not visibly luminous when it is alongside a wall that is illuminated? If it does not, then the air cannot be said to cause the wall's color to be weak, and the analogy with water and stones breaks down. The doubt questions the mechanism of secondary illumination. The author appears to be critiquing a theory that the air is not truly illuminated but merely appears so due to reflection. This is a characteristic of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's critical approach in natural philosophy.

Key Terms
شك
الهواء
الضوء الثاني
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether the air reflects light like a mirror
B) Whether the air becomes qualified by the light-form or not
C) Whether the sun is the source of all light
D) Whether the walls have color or not

Key Takeaway
A dialectical doubt uses a dichotomy to test a theory; each horn must be examined for logical consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar dichotomous doubt about a claim in kalam: 'Either the accidental attribute exists in the atom or it does not.' Show the consequences of each horn.
?

Razi's Modus Tollens on Sound's Pre-Existence
Razi refutes the pre-existence of sound using a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) in Razi's argument and verify its validity using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
We perceive direction, so sound cannot pre-exist – a modus tollens in action.

Razi begins by asserting that sound has no existence externally before reaching the ear. An objector argues that letters persist, implying sound might persist. Razi answers with a conditional argument: if sound existed before reaching, we would not perceive its direction; but we do perceive direction, therefore sound does not pre-exist. Let's analyze this logical move step by step.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the main claim and the objection
Read the opening lines: 'Sound has no existence externally before reaching the ear canal.' The objector implicitly claims that letters (articulations) persist, so sound might also persist. What is the logical structure of the objection?
The objection is that continuous perception implies continuous existence.
2
2. Extract the conditional premise
Razi formulates a conditional: 'If sound existed before reaching, then we would not perceive the direction.' Write out this 'If A then B' statement clearly. A = sound exists before reaching, B = we do not perceive direction.
This is the major premise of the syllogism.
3
3. Identify the denial of the consequent
Razi provides the minor premise: 'But we do perceive direction.' That is, the consequent (B) is false. So we have 'not B'.
Denying the consequent is the second premise.
4
4. Draw the conclusion
From 'If A then B' and 'not B', the valid conclusion is 'not A'. State it: 'Therefore, sound does not exist before reaching.' Check that this follows logically.
This is modus tollens, a valid form of conditional syllogism.
5
5. Verify validity and discuss the answer to the objection
Razi then addresses the objection by explaining that letters are instantaneous and do not persist simultaneously. This resolves why the objection does not undermine the conclusion. Confirm that the syllogism remains valid.
The objection about letter persistence is handled after the logical core.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ إن الصوت لا وجود له في الخارج قبل وصوله إلى الصماخ. فإذا سمعنا الصوت عن بعد، أدركناه حال وصوله إلى الصماخ، وأدركنا الجهة التي منه وصل إلينا. ولو كان الصوت موجوداً قبل وصوله، لما أدركنا الجهة. والجواب أن يقال: الخوف باقية لكنها متضادة، فإن مات الأول ظهر الثاني. ويلزم من امتناع بقاء الحروف الآنية امتناع بقاء كلها، ومنه امتناع لقاء الصوت. ثم في حقيقة الصوت: إنه موج يحدث في الهواء، ويحتاج إلى قائم يقوم به. وليس من شرط القائم أن يكون صلباً، فقد يكون في غاية الرطوبة. وإذا أريد تحريف حرف، يوجد الخارق والمقاوم، كما في الماء. والهواء أيضا كذلك. ثم أسباب اختلاف الأصوات: الحدة والثقل، والعلة الأولى هي المقاومة لا الصلابة. والثاني هو القلة والكثافة، والمسافة التي يسلكها. ثم الصدى: يحدث من موج الهواء إذا صادف حجماً كثيفاً، فيرتد ويحدث صوتاً آخر. والفاعل للصدى ليس الهواء الذاهب أولاً ثم التراجع ثانياً، بل يحدث من موج هواء آخر. لكنه قبل الاتصال لانتشاره أو لقرب الزمانين، فيخبر بهما كالصوت الواحد.
The Shaykh said: Sound has no existence externally before reaching the ear canal. When we hear sound from a distance, we perceive it when it reaches our ear canal, and we perceive the direction from which it came to us. If sound existed before reaching, we would not perceive the direction. The answer is to say: The letters remain but are opposite; if the first dies, the second appears. And from the impossibility of the persistence of instantaneous letters follows the impossibility of the persistence of all, and from that the impossibility of encountering sound. Then regarding the reality of sound: It is a wave occurring in air, and it requires a substrate to subsist in. It is not a condition of the substrate that it be solid; it may be extremely moist. And when one wants to alter a letter, the breaker and the resister exist, as in water. Air is also like that. Then the causes of difference in sounds: highness and lowness; the first cause is resistance, not solidity. The second is the quantity and density, and the distance traveled. Then echo: It occurs from a wave of air when it encounters a dense volume, then rebounds and produces another sound. The agent of the echo is not the air going first and then returning, but rather it occurs from another wave of air. But it is perceived before separation due to its spread or the extreme closeness of the two times, so it is reported as a single sound.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the physical nature of sound and the phenomenon of echo. It begins by addressing an objection about sound's existence before reaching the ear: Razi (the Shaykh) argues that sound does not pre-exist externally; we perceive it only upon arrival, along with its direction. The objection that letters (articulations) persist is answered by noting that letters are mutually exclusive; one ceases as another begins, so continuity does not imply simultaneous existence. The impossibility of instantaneous letters persisting leads to the impossibility of sound persisting as a body. Razi then defines sound as a wave in air requiring a substrate (the air itself), which need not be solid—even moist substances can transmit sound. He draws an analogy to water: when a disturbance occurs, a breaker and a resistance produce waves. Similarly, air transmits sound via waves. The causes of variation in pitch (haddah/thiqal) are identified: the primary cause is resistance (muqawamah) rather than solidity; the secondary cause involves the quantity and density of the air and the distance traveled. Finally, echo (sada) is explained: when a sound wave hits a dense object (e.g., a wall), it reflects, creating a new wave. However, the echo is not caused by the same air returning but by a subsequent wave; due to the rapid succession or the wave's spread, it is perceived as a single sound. This is why a singer's voice echoes more beautifully under a roof—the reflections blend seamlessly. The passage uses causal reasoning typical of natural philosophy, grounding logical-discourse analysis in physical reality.

Key Terms
الصوت
الصماخ
الهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does Razi use in his argument that sound does not pre-exist?
A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens
C) Affirming the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument against the pre-existence of sound is a valid modus tollens: it is a classic example of using conditional syllogisms in Islamic philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the same modus tollens form, construct an argument against the view that the Quran is created (according to the Ash'ari position): 'If the Quran is created, then it is temporally originated; but the Quran is not temporally originated (it is eternal); therefore the Quran is not created.' Verify each premise.

Sound as a Wave: Definition and Echo Mechanism
Razi defines sound physically and explains echo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract Razi's definition of sound (genus and differentia) and explain the mechanism of echo using the concept of wave reflection.

Core Idea
Sound is a wave in air, not a body; echo is a reflected wave, not the same air returning.

After addressing the logical argument, Razi gives a physical description of sound: it is a wave in air that requires a substrate. He then explains the causes of pitch and loudness and finally the mechanism of echo. This lesson breaks down these definitions and causal explanations.

Guided Path
1
1. Locate the definition of sound
Find the sentence: 'ثم في حقيقة الصوت: إنه موج يحدث في الهواء، ويحتاج إلى قائم يقوم به.' This is the core definition. Identify the genus and differentia.
Genus: موج (wave). Differentia: يحدث في الهواء (occurs in air) and يحتاج إلى قائم (requires a substrate).
2
2. Analyze the substrate condition
Razi says the substrate need not be solid; it can be moist. He gives the example of water and air. This clarifies that sound can propagate in various media. Note that the substrate is the medium itself.
The substrate is what carries the wave; it is not a separate entity.
3
3. Understand the causes of pitch variation
Razi identifies two causes for highness (haddah) and lowness (thiqal): the primary cause is resistance (muqawamah), not solidity; the secondary cause is quantity and density of air and distance. This is a causal explanation, not a syllogism.
Resistance is the main factor; for example, a tight string vibrates faster producing a higher pitch.
4
4. Explain the mechanism of echo
Read: 'الصدى: يحدث من موج الهواء إذا صادف حجماً كثيفاً، فيرتد ويحدث صوتاً آخر.' But Razi clarifies that the echo is not the same air returning; it is a new wave produced by the reflection. The perception of a single sound is due to the rapid succession of waves.
This resolves a common misconception that the same air travels back.
5
5. Summarize Razi's contribution
Razi provides a wave theory of sound centuries before modern physics. He distinguishes between the original wave and the reflected wave, and correctly identifies that echo is a separate event perceived as one due to temporal closeness.
This shows the depth of Islamic natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ إن الصوت لا وجود له في الخارج قبل وصوله إلى الصماخ. فإذا سمعنا الصوت عن بعد، أدركناه حال وصوله إلى الصماخ، وأدركنا الجهة التي منه وصل إلينا. ولو كان الصوت موجوداً قبل وصوله، لما أدركنا الجهة. والجواب أن يقال: الخوف باقية لكنها متضادة، فإن مات الأول ظهر الثاني. ويلزم من امتناع بقاء الحروف الآنية امتناع بقاء كلها، ومنه امتناع لقاء الصوت. ثم في حقيقة الصوت: إنه موج يحدث في الهواء، ويحتاج إلى قائم يقوم به. وليس من شرط القائم أن يكون صلباً، فقد يكون في غاية الرطوبة. وإذا أريد تحريف حرف، يوجد الخارق والمقاوم، كما في الماء. والهواء أيضا كذلك. ثم أسباب اختلاف الأصوات: الحدة والثقل، والعلة الأولى هي المقاومة لا الصلابة. والثاني هو القلة والكثافة، والمسافة التي يسلكها. ثم الصدى: يحدث من موج الهواء إذا صادف حجماً كثيفاً، فيرتد ويحدث صوتاً آخر. والفاعل للصدى ليس الهواء الذاهب أولاً ثم التراجع ثانياً، بل يحدث من موج هواء آخر. لكنه قبل الاتصال لانتشاره أو لقرب الزمانين، فيخبر بهما كالصوت الواحد.
The Shaykh said: Sound has no existence externally before reaching the ear canal. When we hear sound from a distance, we perceive it when it reaches our ear canal, and we perceive the direction from which it came to us. If sound existed before reaching, we would not perceive the direction. The answer is to say: The letters remain but are opposite; if the first dies, the second appears. And from the impossibility of the persistence of instantaneous letters follows the impossibility of the persistence of all, and from that the impossibility of encountering sound. Then regarding the reality of sound: It is a wave occurring in air, and it requires a substrate to subsist in. It is not a condition of the substrate that it be solid; it may be extremely moist. And when one wants to alter a letter, the breaker and the resister exist, as in water. Air is also like that. Then the causes of difference in sounds: highness and lowness; the first cause is resistance, not solidity. The second is the quantity and density, and the distance traveled. Then echo: It occurs from a wave of air when it encounters a dense volume, then rebounds and produces another sound. The agent of the echo is not the air going first and then returning, but rather it occurs from another wave of air. But it is perceived before separation due to its spread or the extreme closeness of the two times, so it is reported as a single sound.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the physical nature of sound and the phenomenon of echo. It begins by addressing an objection about sound's existence before reaching the ear: Razi (the Shaykh) argues that sound does not pre-exist externally; we perceive it only upon arrival, along with its direction. The objection that letters (articulations) persist is answered by noting that letters are mutually exclusive; one ceases as another begins, so continuity does not imply simultaneous existence. The impossibility of instantaneous letters persisting leads to the impossibility of sound persisting as a body. Razi then defines sound as a wave in air requiring a substrate (the air itself), which need not be solid—even moist substances can transmit sound. He draws an analogy to water: when a disturbance occurs, a breaker and a resistance produce waves. Similarly, air transmits sound via waves. The causes of variation in pitch (haddah/thiqal) are identified: the primary cause is resistance (muqawamah) rather than solidity; the secondary cause involves the quantity and density of the air and the distance traveled. Finally, echo (sada) is explained: when a sound wave hits a dense object (e.g., a wall), it reflects, creating a new wave. However, the echo is not caused by the same air returning but by a subsequent wave; due to the rapid succession or the wave's spread, it is perceived as a single sound. This is why a singer's voice echoes more beautifully under a roof—the reflections blend seamlessly. The passage uses causal reasoning typical of natural philosophy, grounding logical-discourse analysis in physical reality.

Key Terms
الصوت
الصدى
الموج
الهواء
القائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what causes echo?
A) The same air that went out returns
B) A new wave of air produced by reflection
C) The vibration of the dense object
D) The persistence of the original sound

Key Takeaway
Razi's wave theory of sound and echo demonstrates sophisticated natural philosophy, with echo being a reflected wave, not a return of the same air.

Define 'zakah' using genus and differentia: genus is 'obligatory charity', differentia is 'on specific wealth at a specific threshold after a lunar year'. Then check if it is a complete definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm).

Objection and Reply: Letters as Accidents of Sound
Author presents an objection and reply regarding letters as accidental qualities of sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that letters cannot be real accidents of sound and the author's reply using the analogy of the 'now' to time, and evaluate whether the reply resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Letters are momentary boundaries of sound, like the 'now' is a boundary of time — an accident of a different kind.

In this passage, the author addresses a challenge to the claim that spoken letters are accidental qualities of sound. The objector argues that letters are momentary, occurring only at the start of a sound, and thus cannot be true accidents that inhere over time. The author responds with a striking analogy: just as the 'now' (instant) is an accident of time despite being a boundary, so letters are accidental to sound. Understanding this dialectical exchange sharpens your ability to defend definitions using analogical reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The claim is that letters are accidental qualities of sound. Find the statement in the passage that asserts this.
2
State the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector argues: letters are momentary, existing only at the beginning of a sound, so they cannot be real accidents (which inhere over time).
The key point is the temporal limitation.
3
Extract the reply (jawab)
The author says letters are accidental to sound in the same way the 'now' is accidental to time.
The analogy emphasizes boundary rather than duration.
4
Analyse the analogy
Compare the 'now' as a boundary of time to letters as boundaries of sound. Consider: is the analogy valid? Does it fully answer the objection?
Think about whether accidental boundaries are truly the same as inhering accidents.
5
Evaluate the outcome
Does the reply effectively refute the objection? What remains unresolved?
This prepares you for deeper debates in kalam on the nature of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر مسلم في الحدة والقدمين أن المسموع وفيه إشكال وهو أن الصوات الأينية لا توجد إلا في الزمان الذي هو بداية زمان الصوت فلا تكون عارضة حقيقية. ويمكن أن يقال: إنها عارضة له عروض الآن للزمان. والحروف المصوتة هي التي تسمى العربية حروف المد واللين ولا يمكن الابتداء بها. وأما الصامتة فهي ما عداها.
Another matter accepted in [the discussion of] pitch and depth is that the audible [sound] has an objection: that momentary sounds only exist in the time that is the beginning of the time of the sound, so they are not a real accident. And it can be said: they are accidental to it like the accident of the 'now' to time. The vowels are what are called in Arabic the letters of lengthening and softness, and one cannot begin with them. As for the consonants, they are what are other than them.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that letters are accidental qualities of sound. The objection argues that since letters are momentary sounds occurring at the very beginning of a sound, they cannot be true accidents because an accident must inhere in a substance over time. The author replies by analogy: letters are accidental to sound in the same way that the 'now' (the instant) is accidental to time. Just as the 'now' is a boundary of time and is considered an accident of time, so the momentary letter-sound is a boundary of the sound and is an accident of it. The author then distinguishes between vowels (huruf al-madd wa al-lin), which are prolonged and cannot begin a word, and consonants, which are all other letters. This classification is essential for understanding the phonetics of Arabic speech in the context of logic and rhetoric.

الصامتة
عارضة

D) Like a necessary accident is to a contingent substance

Key Takeaway
Letters are accidental to sound not as inhering qualities over time, but as boundary events, analogous to the 'now' being an accident of time.

Phonological Discord (Tanāfur) in Arabic Letter Composition
Author classifies types of letter composition and identifies causes of phonological discord.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of letter composition and determine which cause extreme phonological discord.

Core Idea
Phonological discord arises from strong letters close in articulation, straining a single muscle.

In this passage, the author Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī classifies letter compositions by quality, focusing on the cause of tanāfur (discord). He explains that the most severe discord occurs when strong letters share a close articulation point. Understanding this helps us appreciate the phonetic criteria for eloquent speech in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of composition
The author first divides composition into (a) letters with vowels, (b) letters with sukoon, and (c) vowels with sukoon. Focus on the first category for explaining discord.
The passage only elaborates on the first category.
2
Analyze the first category: letters with vowels
Within letters with vowels, the author identifies four subcategories based on two dichotomies: strong/weak and close/distant articulation.
The extreme case is strong letters close in articulation.
3
Extract the four subcategories
The subcategories are: (1) strong + close (most discordant), (2) strong + distant (ease), (3) weak + close (?), (4) weak + distant (firm). The OCR corrupts some descriptions, but the taxonomy is clear.
The passage explicitly names the first and second; the third and fourth are less clear.
4
Determine which combination causes extreme discord
The author states that strong letters close in articulation cause extreme discord because they require a single muscle to perform successive difficult movements, leading to strain.
This is the main causal explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكيفية فالنظر إما أن يكون في تركيب الحروف مع الحركات أو السكنات، أو الحركات مع السكنات. فإن كان الأول فقد تكون متنافرة كقوله: «وليس قرب قبر قرب قبره». وسببه أن الحروف المختلفة إما أن تكون قوية وضعيفة، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون متقاربة المخرج أو متباعدة. فالأول هو الصعبة المتقاربة المخرج أشد التنافر، لأن الحروف إذا تقاربت مخارجها كان الفاعل لها عضلة واحدة، فإذا تكلفت الحركة بها عند الحكم بها تواردت الأفعال الشاقة على العضلة الواحدة فصح فيها ضرب من الاختلال. وأما الثاني وهو المقابل للأول: التحفة المباشرة في [؟] والهم والين فانها المكانة من الأمرين المذكرات وذلك يقضى السهولة من الرحمن. وأما الثالث: السهلة واعمان عالما لكون المقادية. وأما الرابع: الصلبة المساعدة وكتبه ان يكون هذا الصعب من عمليت فلا عصر الحداد الأول.
As for quality, the consideration is either in the composition of letters with movements or with pauses, or of movements with pauses. If the first, then they may be discordant, as in his statement: 'And not near a grave near his grave.' Its cause is that different letters are either strong or weak, and in both cases they are either close in articulation or distant. The first is the most severe in discord: the difficult ones close in articulation, because when letters are close in their articulation points, the agent for them is one muscle, and when movement is undertaken in their case, at the moment of judgment, difficult actions converge upon that one muscle, so a kind of disturbance occurs. As for the second, which is the opposite of the first: immediate [pleasure?] in [?] and gentleness and softness, for they occupy a position from the two mentioned matters, and that necessitates ease from the Merciful. As for the third: the easy and [?] because of opposition. As for the fourth: the firm and helping, and it is such that this difficulty from effort does not [?] like the first blacksmith's age.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage classifies types of letter composition in terms of their quality, specifically focusing on the phenomenon of phonological discord (tanāfur). The author begins by stating that the consideration of quality (al-kayfiyya) involves examining the composition of letters with vowels (ḥarakāt) or with sukūn (sakanāt), or vowels with each other. He then identifies the first case: composition of letters with vowels. He gives the famous example 'wa laysa qurbu qabri qurbu qabrih' (a line of poetry) to illustrate discord. The cause of discord (sababuhu) is explained: letters differ in strength; sometimes they are strong and sometimes weak. Moreover, they may be close in articulation point (makhraj) or distant. The most extreme discord occurs when letters are strong and close in articulation—this combination is termed 'al-ṣaʿba al-mutaqāriba al-makhraj' (the difficult ones close in articulation). The reason, which the author explains, is that when letters share a common articulation point, the same muscle must perform rapid successive difficult actions, leading to strain and disorder. The author then proceeds to outline the remaining three possible combinations: (2) opposite of the first: immediate [pleasure?] in gentleness and softness, which gives ease; (3) the easy (al-sahla) and (4) the firm and helping (al-ṣulba al-musāʿida), but these latter descriptions are less clear due to OCR corruption. The entire taxonomy shows that the author is analyzing the phonetic properties of speech from a logical and causal perspective.

Key Terms
تنافر
مخرج
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Letters that are all weak and distant in articulation
B) Letters that are strong and close in articulation point
C) Letters that are composed only with sukoon
D) Letters that are repeated consecutively

Key Takeaway
Extreme phonological discord results from combining strong letters that are close in articulation.

Dialectical Structure: Objection and Response on Letter Articulation
Dialectical argument on letter articulation and habituation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection and response in a dialectical passage from Islamic logic and explain how habituation (malaka) resolves the apparent difficulty.

Core Idea
Repetition transforms difficulty into ease through habituation – a key principle in jadal.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange about why pronouncing letters with similar articulation points becomes easier over time. An objector questions why repetition of the same letter isn't equally difficult, and the author invokes the concept of habituation (malaka) to resolve the paradox. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to follow objections and responses in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find the author's statement about letters with close articulation points. He says that using the same muscle repeatedly for such letters causes difficulty (shiddat al-tanāfur).
2
Locate the objection (lā yuqālu)
The objection is signalled by 'lā yuqālu' (it is not said). It argues that if repeating the same muscle is difficult, then repeating a single letter should also be difficult, but it is not.
3
Extract the author's response
The author responds by distinguishing between mere repetition and habituation. He says that repetition of the same action (kathrat al-fiʿl) leads to acquiring a skill (malaka), which actually reduces difficulty over time.
4
Analyse the cause-countercause structure
Notice the author's phrase 'fa-qad ṣāra hunā ʿillat al-jabr muʿāraḍatan bi-ʿillat al-nashr' – the cause of compulsion is opposed by the cause of diffusion (habituation). This is a classic jadal move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأول هو الصلبة المتقادية المخرج أشد الاقتسام تنافر الحروف. فإذا تقاربت مخارجها كان الفاعل لها عضلة واحدة، فإذا تكثفت الحلمة من أحدها بها فعند الحكم بها تؤدت الأفعال الشاقة على العضلة الواحدة، فصل فيها ضرب من أقلال. لا يقال: فكان يغني أن يكون التكلم بالحرف الواحد من أدل كثيرة في المشقة كالتكلم بعده الحلمات المسافة؟ لا، فالقول الفت هو أن الكلم بالحرف الواحد مواد الكثيرة، وإن كان سببا للهلال إلا أنه سبب الحصول الحركة الملكة، لأن كثرة الفعل سبب الحصول الماركات. فقد صار هنا علة الجبر معارضة بعلة النشر. وأما التكلم بالحلمات المسافرة فقد وجد فيها سلب الحلال، فإنوارات الأفعال الشاقة على العضلة الواحدة علم توجد سبب حصول الملكة فلاحكم كات.
The first is the solid with close articulation points, the most severe division and dissonance of the letters. So if their articulation points are close, the agent for them is one muscle, and if the gland from one of them becomes thick, then when judging with it, the difficult actions on the one muscle are performed, so in it there is a kind of diminution. It is not said: Would it suffice that speaking with one letter is more burdensome than speaking with distant letters? No, the correct statement is that speaking with one letter uses many materials, and although it causes strain, it is a cause for acquiring the habit, because frequency of action is a cause for acquiring skills. So here the cause of compulsion is opposed by the cause of diffusion. As for speaking with distant letters, there is found in it the removal of [...].
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the difficulty of pronouncing letters with adjacent articulation points versus distant ones. The author first states that when letters have similar articulation points, the same muscle is used repeatedly, causing difficulty. An objection is raised: if using the same muscle repeatedly is difficult, then even pronouncing a single letter repeatedly would be equally difficult, but it is not. The author responds by explaining that repetition of the same action leads to habituation (al-malaka), which makes the action easier over time. Thus, the initial difficulty is overcome by practice. The author contrasts this with letters whose articulation points are distant, which involve different muscles and thus do not produce the same habituating effect. The argument is a good example of how cause (illa) can be met with a counter-cause (illa al-nashr).

Key Terms
مخرج
عضلة
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does pronouncing adjacent letters become easier over time?
A) Because the letters themselves change.
B) Because repetition produces a habit (malaka) that facilitates action.
C) Because the muscle becomes stronger.
D) Because the articulation points move apart.

Key Takeaway
In a dialectical argument, an objection can be resolved by introducing a counter-cause (such as habituation) that overturns the initial presumption of difficulty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Eight Tastes: A Classification Example from Natural Philosophy
Author enumerates eight tastes and explains their origins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentiae in a classification of tastes and evaluate its completeness.

Core Idea
Classification in Islamic sciences uses genus and differentia to map the world.

This passage from a natural philosophy text lists eight tastes and explains their physical causes. It is a good example of how Islamic scholars used definitional logic to organize knowledge. We will analyze its structure as a definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of the classification
The passage begins with 'أصناف الطعوم' (kinds of tastes). So the genus is 'taste' (طعم).
The genus is the broader category that is being divided.
2
List the eight species
Find the eight terms listed: bitterness, saltiness, sourness, greasiness, sweetness, insipidity, astringency, and acerbity.
These are the species under the genus 'taste'.
3
Examine the differentiae used
The author uses two criteria: the agent (heat, cold, moderate) and the consistency (dense, subtle, moderate). This is a binary division.
Each taste is produced by a specific combination of agent and consistency.
4
Check if the classification is exhaustive
The author provides 3x3=9 combinations but only lists 8, omitting one (e.g., 'pungency' or 'heat' itself? The text lists 'heat' as a product of hot+subtle, but that may not be a taste).
Consider whether all possible tastes are covered.
5
Assess the definition type
This is a rasm (description) because it gives causes, not essential definition, and may not be exhaustive.
A complete hadd would provide genus and essential differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن يكون له طعم، وأصناف الطعوم ثمانية: المرارة، والملوحة، والحموضة، والدسومة، والحلاوة، والتفاهة، والقبض، والعفوصة. وذلك أن الفاعل في الملة إما الحرارة أو البرودة أو الاعتدال بينهما. فالحار إن فعل في الكثيف أحدث المرارة، أو في اللطيف أحدث الحرارة، أو في المعتدل أحدث الملوحة. والبارد إن فعل في الكثيف أحدث الحموضة، أو في اللطيف أحدث المومنة، أو في المعدل أحدث القبض. والمعتدل إن فعل في اللطيف أحدث الدسومة، أو في الكثيف أحدث الحلاوة، أو في المعدل أحدث التفاهة.
And as for that which has taste, the kinds of tastes are eight: bitterness, saltiness, sourness, greasiness, sweetness, insipidity, astringency, and acerbity. That is because the agent in the matter is either heat, or cold, or the moderation between them. So heat, if it acts on the dense, produces bitterness; or on the subtle, produces heat (pungency?); or on the moderate, produces saltiness. And cold, if it acts on the dense, produces sourness; or on the subtle, produces sweetish?; or on the moderate, produces astringency. And the moderate, if it acts on the subtle, produces greasiness; or on the dense, produces sweetness; or on the moderate, produces insipidity.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a classification and causal explanation of the eight tastes according to the natural philosophy of the time. It defines each taste as resulting from the interaction of a primary quality (hot, cold, or moderate) with the consistency of the substance (dense, subtle, or moderate). The eight tastes are listed: bitterness, saltiness, sourness, greasiness, sweetness, insipidity, astringency, and acerbity. The author follows a systematic scheme: for each of the three agent qualities (hot, cold, moderate), three possible effects are given based on the recipient's consistency. This yields nine combinations, but the author reduces them to eight by omitting the repetition of 'heat' as a taste? The classification is typical of Greek-Arabic humoral theory, applied to gustatory qualities.

حرارة
كثيف

B) Eight
C) Seven
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Classification in Islamic sciences systematically divides a genus into species using explicit differentiae.

Al-Razi's Critique of the Third Rank of Cognition
Al-Razi critiques Ibn Sina's third cognitive rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's two arguments against Ibn Sina's third rank of cognition and evaluate their logical force.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's third cognitive rank collapses: either you know something in detail or you don't — no middle state.

This passage presents Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's rejection of a key element in Ibn Sina's psychology: the alleged third rank of cognition between potential and actual. Al-Razi offers two arguments to show that the state described by Ibn Sina is actually a form of actual knowledge, and then provides his own reasoning why such a middle state is impossible. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the Ash'ari epistemological stance against the Peripatetic model.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Find the exact description of Ibn Sina's third rank: a person knows a problem, becomes heedless, then recalls when asked. This is the state al-Razi attacks.
This is a psychological state, not a logical form.
2
Extract the first argument
Al-Razi says the ability to answer implies actual knowledge of the answer. The ability (qudra) depends on (tadammana) knowledge of the answer, so the person actually knows it at that moment.
First reason: the person is knowledgeable of his ability to answer.
3
Extract the second argument
There is an intuitive distinction between the state before hearing the teaching and after. Potentiality was present before, but after hearing, actual knowledge occurs when the person can answer.
Second reason: the intuitive distinction shows actual knowledge arises.
4
Analyze al-Razi's own reasoning
Al-Razi argues that the third rank is invalid by dichotomy: either the details are known or not. If known, then detailed knowledge exists (actual). If not, then no knowledge at all. There is no intermediate.
This is a disjunctive argument: two cases exhaust possibilities.
5
Evaluate the critique
Consider whether al-Razi's dichotomy is exhaustive. Does it rule out all possible intermediates? What assumptions does he make about the nature of knowledge?
Critique depends on a binary view of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَثْبَتَ الشَّيْخُ مَرْتَبَةً ثَالِثَةً وَهِيَ كَوْنُ عِلْمِ مَسْأَلَةٍ ثُمَّ غَفَلَ عَنْهَا ثُمَّ سُئِلَ عَنْهَا فَإِنَّهُ بَعْضُ الْجَوَابِ عَنْهَا ذِهْنُهُ فَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ بِالْقُوَّةِ لِوَجْهَيْنِ: أَمَّا أَوَّلًا فَلِأَنَّهُ عَالِمٌ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ بِاقْتِدَارِهِ عَلَى الْجَوَابِ وَذَلِكَ يَتَضَمَّنُ عِلْمًا لِذَلِكَ الْجَوَابِ فَالْمَوْقُوفُ عَلَيْهِ عِلَّةٌ أَيْ مَا أَعْلَمُهُ. وَأَمَّا ثَانِيًا فَلِأَنَّهُ بِالتَّفْرِقَةِ الْبَدَاهِيَةِ بَيْنَ حَالِهِ قَبْلَ سَمَاعِ ذَلِكَ التَّعَالِ وَبَيْنَ حَالِهِ بَعْدَهُ وَقَدْ كَانَتِ الْقُوَّةُ حَاصِلَةً قَبْلُ فَقَدْ حَدَثَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ عِلْمٌ بِالْفِعْلِ. وَهَذِهِ الْمَرْتَبَةُ عِنْدِي بَاطِلَةٌ لِأَنَّ تِلْكَ التَّفَاصِيلَ إِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةً لَهُ وَجَبَ أَنْ يُمَيِّزَ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهَا عِنْدَ عَيْنِهِ فَيَكُونُ الْعِلْمُ التَّفْصِيلِيُّ حَاصِلًا. وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْعِلْمُ بِهَا حَاصِلًا أَصْلًا بَلْ رُبَّمَا كَانَ الْمَعْلُومُ حَالَةً مِنْ أَحْوَالِهَا لَكِنْ تِلْكَ الْحَالَةُ مَعْلُومَةٌ لَهُ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ فَهُوَ مَعْلُومٌ لَهُ مُنْفَصِلٌ عِنْدَهُ وَالَّذِي لَيْسَ الْمُنْفَصِلُ عِنْدَهُ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَعْلُومًا لَهُ.
The Shaykh [Ibn Sina] established a third rank, which is: having knowledge of a problem, then being heedless of it, then being asked about it, so that some of the answer occurs to his mind. So that is not potential for two reasons: First, because he is knowledgeable at that time of his ability to answer, and that entails knowledge of that answer; the dependent [thing] is a cause, i.e., what I know it. Second, because of the intuitive distinction between his state before hearing that teaching and his state after it, while the potentiality was present before, so actual knowledge has occurred at that time. And this rank, in my view, is invalid; because those details, if they are known to him, he must distinguish each one of them in its specific instance, so detailed knowledge would be obtained. Otherwise, knowledge of them would not be obtained at all; rather, perhaps the known thing is a state among its states, but that state is known to him in detail, so it is known to him as separate at him; and that which is not separate at him was not known to him.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a position held by al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) regarding the division of cognition into potential, actual, and an intermediate third rank. The author opens by stating that al-Shaykh posited a third rank: a person knows a problem, then becomes heedless of it, and when asked, some of the answer comes to mind. The author argues that this state is not potential, but actual, for two reasons. First, the person's ability to answer implies they have knowledge of that answer; the ability itself depends on actual knowledge. Second, there is an intuitive distinction between the state before hearing the teaching and after, and since potentiality was present before, actual knowledge arises at that moment. The author then declares this third rank invalid. He argues that if the details were known to the person, they would be able to distinguish each one distinctly, meaning detailed knowledge is present. If not, then no knowledge exists at all. At best, the person might know some general state, but that state would be known in detail, so it is separate knowledge. Anything not separate is not known. Thus, the alleged intermediate rank collapses into either potential or actual.

Key Terms
المرتبة الثالثة (al-martabah al-thālithah)
التعقل (al-ta'aqqul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why does the person's ability to answer after being asked indicate actual knowledge rather than a third state?
A) Because the ability to answer is itself an actual property.
B) Because the person has detailed knowledge that allows them to distinguish each part of the answer.
C) Because the potentiality remains unchanged before and after learning.
D) Because the question itself provides new knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's critique demonstrates that the supposedly intermediate cognitive state either reduces to detailed actual knowledge or to no knowledge at all, thereby refuting Ibn Sina's three-tier model.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using al-Razi's dichotomy, evaluate the Mu'tazilite concept of 'al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn' (the intermediate state). Does it commit the same error as Ibn Sina's third rank?
?
In a debate, an Ash'ari scholar claims that a believer's faith cannot increase or decrease because it is like knowledge: either present fully or absent. Use al-Razi's argument to support this position.

Parsing the Triad: 'Aql, 'Aqil, Ma'qul
Author discusses whether the intellect and intelligible are identical or distinct, focusing on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three terms 'aql, 'āqil, ma'qūl and trace their logical relations as discussed in the passage.

Core Idea
Intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are not always identical; their relation depends on the object of intellection.

The passage delves into a fundamental epistemological question: Is the act of intellection identical to its object? This is especially complex when considering God's self-knowledge. In this lesson, we will parse the key terms and their logical interplay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key terms
Locate 'aql (intellect), 'āqil (intelligent), and ma'qūl (intelligible) in the Arabic passage. Note that 'aql may refer to the faculty or substance, while 'āqil is the subject possessing that faculty, and ma'qūl is the object known.
2
Understand the composite model
The author presents a model where a composite whole consists of an individual and an essential addition. The intellect is considered the whole, and the intelligible is each of the restrictions. This results in difference (mughāyara).
This is a logical analysis to show how the two can be distinct.
3
Examine the self-intellection problem
The author notes that if the intellect is knowledgeable of each part, it may not be knowledgeable of itself. This highlights the difficulty of identity between subject and object in self-knowledge.
4
Apply to the divine case
When God intellects another, the intelligent and intelligible are distinct. When He intellects Himself, they become identical. This shows the special nature of divine self-knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجموع المركب منها ومن الشخص ذاتي إضافة للما هيئة المشركه كذب العام ولن ي لنا انه لا بد من المغايرة لكن كل شخص فانه لا تدل على ماهيته النوعية التي امتح نفس الصورها من الشركة بقيد ذاته فيكون هناك فيدان ومجتمع منها مجموع ذات واحدة فاذا جعلنا العاقل هو المجموع والمعقول كل واحد من القيدين حصلت المغايرة وبهذا الاعتبار يمنع أن نقول ذاتي وذاتك والحواف عن الأول إنه الإسلام من كون احد القيدين أم من الآخرة اللفظ والعقد معه وجود العام بدون ذلك الخاص في نفس الأمر كما ان فاعل ساغ أن علة إلى أعم من فعلنا انه علة إلى آخر غيره ومع ذلك فلازم صحة كون الله علة لفية وعن الثاني ان ذكر العند عيد في كون ذلك المجموع عالما بكل واحد من جزيه لكنه حينئذ لا يكون عالماً بنفس قيل بكل واحد من حربه فاما كون عالما بنفس فالأشكال يعينه قائم فهذا حتم الغواية هذا الموضع ولا يصفو العلم إلا بالتمام إضافة إلى نفسه من الوجه الواحد وفيه ما فيه من كون الله عقلا و عاقلا و معقولا اما إذا عقد الله غيره فالعاقل ليس هو المعقول بالبداهة فاما إذا غير ذاته فالمعقول هو العاقل معنى ان الذي عقل إن كان معقولاً هو الذي عقل له أن اعلم ان الخير ما لو كان عاقلاً واما نفس كون عاقلاً فهو مغاير لكون معقولا لانه قد يفهم من المقال موجودة الكون معقولاً مع الشك في كونه عاقلاً وبالعكس وذلك بعضي تغاير ان الا ان الان وهل هما وصفات شرقية في الخارج أم لا فيه نظر فان كان عمر هذه العات لئلا يكون منفي ذات العاقل لان المعقول من العقل نعم ان وجود إضافة حاصلة بين ذات العاقل و المعقول و الإضافات عوارض فتكون من المقبل مغايرة لمعرض منافرة عنها وعلى هذا النظر يكون كون العقل عاقلا و معقولا يغيل ان يكون امرا واحد او جدال فاني أقسام
The composite whole consists of it and the individual, essential, as an addition to the shared essence. The universal is false... and we say that difference is necessary, but every individual does not indicate its specific essence which tests the same forms from sharing by the restriction of its essence. So there are two... and from them a composite of a single essence. So if we make the intellect the whole and the intelligible each one of the two restrictions, difference occurs. And by this consideration, we are prevented from saying essential and your essence... and the avoidance of the first... that one of the two restrictions is from the hereafter... the word and contract with it the existence of the universal without that particular in reality. Just as the agent is valid as a cause more general than our action, it is a cause of another besides it, and despite that, the soundness of God being a cause of... is necessary. And regarding the second, the mention of the slave... in that that composite is knowledgeable of each of its parts, but then it is not knowledgeable of itself, it is said of each of its parts. As for being knowledgeable of itself, the problem stands precisely, so this is the end of error in this place. And knowledge is not clear except by completion, an addition to itself from one aspect. And in it is what is in it of God being intellect, intelligent, and intelligible. But if God thinks of another, the intelligent is not the intelligible intuitively. But if He thinks of His essence, the intelligible is the intelligent, meaning that which He intellects, if it is intelligible, it is that which He intellects for Him. Know that the good is if He were intelligent, but the very fact of being intelligent is different from being intelligible, because it may be understood from the article that the existence of being intelligible exists with doubt about being intelligent, and vice versa. And that is some difference... And are they two attributes in the external world or not? There is speculation. For if... these... are such that they are not negative of the essence of the intelligent, because the intelligible is from the intellect. Yes, there is a relation occurring between the essence of the intelligent and the intelligible, and relations are accidents, so they are from the... difference from the... contrary to it. And according to this view, the intellect being intelligent and intelligible would be one thing or a dialectical division.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a dense philosophical discussion about the relationship between the intellect ('aql), the intelligent ('āqil), and the intelligible (ma'qūl), particularly in the context of God's attributes. The author begins by considering a composite whole made of an individual and an essential addition to a shared essence, arguing that difference (mughāyara) is necessary. He then examines the scenario where the intellect is taken as the whole and the intelligible as each of the two restrictions, leading to difference. He critiques the idea that the intellect can be knowledgeable of itself while also being knowledgeable of its parts, noting problems. The discussion then shifts to God: if God intellects another, the intelligent is not identical to the intelligible intuitively; but if He intellects His own essence, the intelligible is the intelligent. The author points out that the very fact of being intelligent is different from being intelligible, as one can conceive of one without the other. He then questions whether these are two attributes in the external world or not, and notes that relations are accidents, leading to a discussion of whether the intellect can be both intelligent and intelligible as one thing or a dialectical division. The passage is highly technical and requires careful parsing.

Key Terms
عاقل (ʿāqil): The intelligent being, the subject of intellection.
معقول (maʿqūl): The intelligible object, that which is intellected.
عقل (ʿaql): The intellect, the faculty of intellection, here considered as a substance or attribute.
مغايرة (mukhāyara): Difference or distinction between two things.
إضافة (iḍāfa): Relation or relative concept, here considered as an accident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between 'āqil and ma'qūl when God intellects His own essence?
A) They are identical.
B) They are distinct.
C) They are both accidents.
D) They are not related.

Key Takeaway
The identity of intelligent and intelligible holds only in the case of self-intellection, particularly for God.
