The Ontological Status of Divine Attributes: Are 'Aqil and Ma'qul Accidents?
Author discusses whether the intellect and intelligible are identical or distinct, focusing on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the passage's argument about whether the attributes of being intelligent and being intelligible are accidents or essential.

Core Idea
If intelligibility is a relation, it may be an accident; but divine simplicity challenges this.

The latter part of the passage raises a critical ontological question: Are the attributes of being intelligent and being intelligible real distinctions in the external world, or are they merely conceptual? This touches on debates about divine simplicity and the nature of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Locate the ontological question
Find the sentence: 'وهل هما صفتان في الخارج أم لا' (And are they two attributes in the external world or not?). This is the central query.
2
Identify the relational nature
The author states that there is a relation (iḍāfa) between the essence of the intelligent and the intelligible, and relations are accidents. This suggests that these attributes are accidental.
3
Consider the implication for God
If relations are accidents, then God's being intelligent and intelligible would involve accidents, which seems problematic for divine simplicity. The author does not resolve it definitively.
4
Contrast with the self-knowledge case
In the case of self-intellection, the intelligent and intelligible are identical, so the relation collapses. This might avoid the accident problem, but the author leaves it as a dialectical division.

Refutation of the Denial of Simultaneous Intellection
Author refutes the claim that the soul cannot grasp multiple intelligibles simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's refutation of the denial of simultaneous intellection and identify the logical consequences of that denial.

Core Idea
Denying simultaneous intellection destroys the possibility of all knowledge.

This passage presents a critical argument against a philosophical claim about the soul's cognitive capacity. The author shows that if the soul could not cognize multiple things simultaneously, then neither assent (tasdiq) nor definition would be possible, and even logical inference would collapse. This lesson walks through the author's multi-pronged refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the statement being attacked: that the soul after separation cannot cognize multiple intelligibles at once.
2
Analyze the consequence for assent
The author argues that assent (tasdiq) is a relation between two things; to assent requires cognizing both simultaneously. If the soul cannot do that, assent is impossible.
Assent is defined as a judgment that requires two concepts.
3
Analyze the consequence for definitions
A definition of a composite thing requires grasping all its parts at once. Without simultaneous intellection, definitions become impossible.
The definition of a composite is like comprehending a whole through its parts.
4
Analyze the consequence for logical inference
A single premise cannot produce a conclusion; syllogisms require multiple premises. If the soul cannot hold multiple premises simultaneously, inference is impossible.
This is a standard logical principle: one premise is not productive.
5
Synthesize the refutation
The author concludes that the denial leads to the impossibility of all knowledge, which is absurd. Therefore the denial is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ظهر من تحقيق هذه القاعدة فناد فول من زعم أن النفس بعد المفارقة لا تعقل شا بطرف المكان اجتماع التعقلات الكثيرة دفعة واحدة و اناغ النصيرا فلا تعلولم يفتح ذلك لما صح التصديق إصلا لانه نسبة أمراني امرِ وَهى لا تاتى الأمع تعقلها وفساد الثاني بدال على فساد المقدم وايضا فقد نفقه المركب بحده وذلك لا تاتى الأبصم جميع الحالة دفعة وامان التصديقات فلانه لما منع اجتماعه الماحصلية الذهب الأمقدمة واحدة ابدا ولوكان كذلك لما حصلت المتعة إصلا لانا نعلم بالضرورة ان المقدمة الواحدة عبر منتجة وقاد الثاني يدل على فساد المقدم
It has become clear from the investigation of this rule that the one who claimed that the soul after separation does not cognize anything by way of assembling many intelligibles at once - and that [denial] is false - so that would not make assent possible at all, because it is a relation of two things, and that only comes with their cognition. The corruption of the second indicates the corruption of the first. Also, we comprehend the composite by its definition, and that only comes by assembling all its parts at once. As for assents, because he prevented their achievement, the obtained premise is never a single one. And if it were so, then the conclusion would never be obtained, for we know necessarily that a single premise is not productive. And the corruption of the second indicates the corruption of the first.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a falsafa claim that the human soul, after separation from the body, cannot grasp multiple intelligibles simultaneously. The author argues that this view is false because it would invalidate all assents (tasdiqat) and definitions. Assent requires a relation between two things (e.g., subject and predicate), which cannot be cognized without cognizing both simultaneously. Similarly, the definition of a composite requires simultaneous cognition of all its parts. Moreover, assent in logic requires at least two premises; a single premise cannot produce a conclusion. Therefore, the denial of simultaneous intellection leads to the impossibility of all knowledge, which is absurd.

D) Because all knowledge is simple.

Key Takeaway

Why Causal Knowledge Requires Experience
Author refutes the principle that cause-knowledge equals effect-knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that knowledge of the cause's essence suffices for knowledge of the effect, using the arguments of distinct essences, relational causality, and the necessity of experience.

Core Idea
Knowing the cause's essence does not yield its effects without experience because causality is a relation and effects have their own essences.

This lesson tackles a foundational epistemological puzzle: if you truly know the essence of a cause, do you thereby know its effect? The author presents a plausible argument for the claim, then dismantles it with logical objections. You will learn to distinguish essential knowledge from relational knowledge, and see why Muslim scholars insisted on experience (tajriba) as necessary for causal understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under scrutiny
Locate the initial assertion: 'knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect.' This is the thesis the author will challenge.
2
Extract the supporting argument
Find the reasoning: if one knows the essence of the cause, one knows it necessitates the effect, so one knows the effect. Note that this assumes a direct transfer from essence to effect.
3
Spot the first objection: distinct essences and relations
The cause and effect have separate essences. Causality is a relation (idafa), and relations require knowledge of both relata; you cannot know the effect solely from the cause without prior knowledge of the effect.
4
Recognize the infinite regress objections
If knowing a thing entailed knowing all its consequences and necessary concomitants, we would have infinite knowledge, which is impossible. This shows the claim leads to absurdity.
5
Understand the role of experience
The author proposes that in practice we use experience (istiqra) – e.g., fire burning cotton is known by repeated observation, not by grasping the essence of fire. Similarly, poison's effect is learned by witnessing it.

And what is said: we found a mind open to a known thing, so directing it to another known thing must refer it to imagination, not to the belief which we corrected in that. It is disbelief that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, and it is not reversed. As for the first, for three reasons: when one is heedless of the cause, there is present in the mind a disposition necessitating the essence of the effect, and when it is so, knowledge of the effect is obtained. The two premises are apparent, based on the fact that intellection requires the attainment of an essence equal to the intelligible in the intellect, when the cause is such that it necessitates the effect by its essence. So whoever knows the essence of the cause must know from it that it by its essence necessitates the effect, and that is our counting of the effect, that the cause is a cause which we intend... but the essence of the cause is other than the essence of the effect, and we know the correctness of the two essences each one of them while being heedless of the other, and if we say [something] is false, then we know the essence because causality is a relation, and relations are not known except after knowing the relata. Therefore causality of the effect is not known except after knowing the effect. So if we have derived knowledge of the effect from knowledge of the cause... then if we descend from this station, it is nonetheless refuted by such-and-such: one of them is that our knowledge of the thing itself necessitates that our knowledge of it is of all its consequences, but all consequences of the thing are not themselves effects. So we would have knowledge of all its consequences, which we do not have, with their existence by themselves. The second is that it would necessitate from the intellection of the quiddity the intellection of all its necessary concomitants, and so on ad infinitum. These are difficult doubts. And perhaps an answer could be given: we infer by [something] like the example of the fire and the cotton, that knowledge is by experience, not from the nature of the cause; rather this indication is by knowledge of the first consequent. And we observe that one who drinks poison and eats food does not know the pain of these two things upon them. And if knowledge of the essence of the cause sufficed for knowledge of the existence of the effect, then we would not need experience and induction in these places. As for the second [doubt], because the attribution of the effect to the cause is not by its essence but by something else, otherwise the cause would not need anything else, and then everything would be an effect of this cause, rather it is absolute existence. And as for the other..., know that the known may be the cause in such and such way.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage examines the epistemological principle that knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of the effect. The author initially presents an argument for this: if one knows the essence of the cause, one knows that it necessarily produces the effect, thus knowing the effect through it. However, he immediately objects that the essence of the cause is distinct from the essence of the effect, and we can know one without the other. Moreover, causality is a relation that depends on prior knowledge of both relata, so one cannot know the effect solely from the cause. The author then raises two strong doubts: first, if knowledge of a thing implied knowledge of all its consequences, then we would have infinite knowledge, which is false. Second, knowledge of a quiddity would require knowledge of all its necessary concomitants, leading to infinite regress. He suggests that in practice, we use experience (istiqra) rather than mere knowledge of essence, as demonstrated by fire and cotton: we know fire burns cotton by repeated observation, not by knowing the essence of fire. Similarly, one who drinks poison does not know the pain without experiencing it. Thus, the principle that knowledge of cause suffices for knowledge of effect is rejected, and experience is necessary.

The Cause of Non-Existence After Existence
Author argues that non-existence after existence must have a cause, either existential or non-existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive argument that the cause of non-existence after existence is non-existential, and apply it to causal arguments in kalam.

Core Idea
Non-existence after existence always has a cause, but that cause cannot be a positive entity.

When something ceases to exist, why does it cease? The author argues that its non-existence is not due to the thing itself, nor can it be caused by another existing thing. Instead, the cause must be the absence of a necessary condition. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning behind that claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
What is the author trying to prove? Read the first sentence: non-existence after existence requires a cause, and that cause is not the thing itself.
The claim is that non-existence is not spontaneous; it has a cause.
2
Trace the disjunction
The author presents a dichotomy: the cause is either existential (a positive thing) or non-existential (a privation). Note that these are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This sets up a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Examine the refutation of the existential cause
The author uses a conditional argument: if the cause were existential, then either it disturbs a condition necessary for the effect, or it does not. Consider both options.
If it disturbs, the real cause is the absence of that condition. If it does not, the effect would persist, which is impossible.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the existential cause leads to absurdity or reduces to a non-existential cause, it is eliminated. Therefore, the cause must be non-existential.
This is a valid disjunctive syllogism: either A or B; not A; therefore B.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم الشيء بعد وجوده ليس لذات الشيء، والعدم لا يوجد أصلاً بل لا بد له من سبب، وهو إما أن يكون وجودياً أو عدمياً. والأول باطل لأن ذلك الوجودي إذا وجد فإما أن يختل لأجله أمر من الأمور المعتبرة في عليته على وجود المعلول أو لا يختل. فإن كان الأول كان عدم المعلول لعدم العلة بالحقيقة، وإن كان الثاني لزم بقاء الحالة من الوجه الذي [؟] يعاده [؟] من عند المعلول مع أن المعلول لا يحصل من ذلك، وذلك محال. وب تقدير محاله فالمقصود حاصل أنه إذا كان حصول العلة مستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في كونها علة منفكة عن المعلول، كان أيضاً حول التي المفروض في عدم المعلول مشجعاً [؟] جن [؟] جنات [؟] المؤثرية مع إمه [؟] الحمل [؟] منه ذلك العدم. وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن الأمر الوجودي الذي جعل علة نفع [؟].
The non-existence of a thing after its existence is not due to the essence of the thing, and non-existence does not exist at all, but rather it must have a cause, which is either existential or non-existential. The first is false because that existential, when it exists, either an element among the elements considered in its causality over the existence of the effect is disturbed or it is not disturbed. If it is the first, then the non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of the cause in reality; if it is the second, then the state persists from the aspect that [?] from the effect while the effect is not produced from that, and that is impossible. And assuming its impossibility, the purpose is obtained: if the cause is obtained comprising all the aspects considered in its causality, separate from the effect, then also [?] in the non-existence of the effect [?] the effectivity with [?] from that non-existence. And if that is so, then the existential thing that was made a cause [?].
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument about the cause of non-existence after existence. The author begins by stating a principle: the non-existence of a thing after it existed is not due to the thing itself. Non-existence does not occur spontaneously; it requires a cause. This cause can be either existential (a positive thing) or non-existential (a privation). The author then argues that the first option (existential cause) is false. The reasoning follows a conditional: if an existential cause exists, then either it disturbs some condition necessary for the effect's existence, or it does not. If it disturbs, then the effect's non-existence is truly due to the absence of the cause (since the cause is no longer fully present). If it does not disturb, then the effect would remain even though the cause is present, which is impossible because the effect depends on the cause. Therefore, the only valid explanation is that the cause of non-existence is non-existential, i.e., the absence of a necessary condition. The argument uses logical reasoning typical of Islamic philosophy, akin to discussions in kalam about the contingency of the world.

C) Because existential causes only produce existence, never non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The cause of non-existence after existence is not a positive entity but the absence of a necessary condition for existence.

Stages of Projectile Motion: Force Equality and Rest
Author explains the interaction of forced and natural motion leading to rest and subsequent fall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the three stages of projectile motion (forced ascent, momentary rest, natural descent) and identify the causal mechanism of each stage as presented in the Mulakhkhas.

Core Idea
Projectile rest occurs not when force is exhausted but when forced and natural forces equilibrate.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's Mulakhkhas examines what happens when a stone is thrown upward. Al-Razi explains that the forced imparted force gradually weakens due to air resistance until it equals the inherent natural force. At that point, the stone stops momentarily before the natural force takes over and pulls it down. This lesson will walk you through this causal sequence and clarify a common misconception about the cause of rest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forces
Locate the terms القوة القسرية (forced force) and القوة الطبيعية (natural force) in the Arabic. Understand that the forced force is the external impetus, while the natural force is the inherent tendency to move downward.
2
Trace the weakening of the forced force
The author states that the forced force 'does not cease to weaken' due to air resistance (مصادات الهواء المحروق). Observe that the weakening is gradual, not instantaneous.
3
Identify the point of equality
Find the phrase 'تصير معادلة للقوة الطبيعية' (becomes equal to the natural force). This is the moment when the upward forced force is exactly balanced by the downward natural force, resulting in rest (الوقوف).
4
After rest: natural descent
Read the next clause: 'ثم أن القوة القسرية تضعف بعد ذلك فتستولي القوة الطبيعية فيتحرك الحجر إلى أسفل.' After the moment of rest, the forced force continues to weaken, so the natural force dominates and the stone moves downward.
This sequence refutes the idea that rest is caused by complete exhaustion of force; it is a temporary equilibrium.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن القوة القسرية لا تزال تضعف بسبب مصادات الهواء المحروق إلى أن تصير معادلة للقوة الطبيعية وهناك يحصل الوقوف. ثم أن القوة القسرية تضعف بعد ذلك فتستولي القوة الطبيعية فيتحرك الحجر إلى أسفل. ولو قيل: إن القوة المسكنة التي أفادها القاسر عند الوصول إلى الحد الآخر إنما حصلت بترك الإفادة لا بالقصد، فلابد وأن تكون معلومة. فإن من رمى حجرا إلى فوق فإنه لا يخطر باله السكون والقوة المسكنة ولا يقصد إلا إيصال الحجر إلى العلو. وأما ثانيا: فإن تلك القوة حين ما تصير مسكنة بالفعل وجب استعمالها إلا إذا وجد ما يعدمها وذلك المعدم ليس الطبيعة ولا الحجر ولا شيء كان فيه. وإذا ما وجدت هذه القوة فلابد وأن يكون أمرا خارجيا اتفاقيا. وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور: إذا فرضنا وزرا عظيما من الماء في تلك الحالة تساقط إلى فوق فحين ما وصل إلى الحد الآخر لا بد أن يرجع تلك القوة له فلو وجب وقوفها وكنها لوح وجد وقوف الجبل في الهواء وذلك مستبعد جدا. وحجة الشيخ: أن وجوب هذا التكون بالميل هو العلة الغائية لحكة الحجر من حد إلى آخر من المسافة. وإذا لحما إلى حد فلابد وأن يكون هو الموصل له إليه وعلة الوصول حاصلة معه فالوصول إلى الحد الآن فذلك الميك موجود في ذلك الان. وإذا رجع العين عن ذلك الحد فلابد أن بكرت عليه عيل وهو أيضا إن خاما أن تجتمع الميلان في آن واحد وهو محال استحالة أن يجتمع الميك إلى الشر مع الجميل عند أونى إنين فان مالکن بینیمان كان لزم نشانی ایرانات كان كان فقد حصل الكون واخواتكن من وجهين. أما أولا: فهذه الحجة من باب الكم والكيف لأنه لا حاجة بنا إلى الميل. وأما ثانيا: فلا نسلم امتناع اجتماع الميلين دفعة واحدة وقد من تقرير ذلك في مباحث النقل والحفاء. بأن الحركة المتديرة بالذات لا تكون إلا إرادية. الحركة الطبيعية عن حالة منافية وطلب الحالة الطبيعية وذلك لا يتأتى في النفس. أما أنها لا يمكن أن يكون هو بافلات كل نقطة تحك عنها الجسم حركة شادية؟
For the forced force does not cease to weaken due to the resistances of the burned air until it becomes equal to the natural force, and there rest occurs. Then the forced force weakens after that, so the natural force prevails and the stone moves downward. And if it is said: The resting force that the imparted force gave at reaching the other limit occurred only by ceasing to impart, not by intention, so it must be known. For he who throws a stone upward does not think of rest and the resting force, and intends only to bring the stone up. Secondly, when that force becomes actually resting, it must be used unless something negates it, and that negator is not nature, nor the stone, nor anything in it. And if this force exists, it must be an external accidental matter. Then the aforementioned objection returns: if we suppose a great mass of water in that state falling upward, then when it reaches the other limit, that force must return, so if its rest were obligatory, it would find the rest of a mountain in the air, which is very improbable. And the argument of the Master: that the necessity of this motion by inclination is the final cause of the stone's motion from one limit to another along the distance. And when it reaches a limit, it must be that which brings it to it, and the cause of arrival is present with it, so arrival at the limit now, so that thing exists at that instant. And if the eye returns from that limit, it must be that another [inclination] overcomes it, and it is also impossible for two inclinations to meet at one instant, which is as impossible as the meeting of the thing with the... (unclear). Indeed, if the two inclinations [were together], it would necessitate two... (unclear). Thus motion has occurred and... from two aspects. As for first, this argument is from the category of quantity and quality because we have no need for inclination. As for second, we do not grant the impossibility of two inclinations meeting at once, and it is established in the discussions of transmission and... that circular motion essentially is only voluntary. Natural motion out of a state of opposition and seeking a natural state, and that is not in the soul. As for that it cannot be that the body moves with a sudden motion at every point it passes? [End]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from the Mulakhkhas discusses the nature of forced and natural motion in a projectile, such as a stone thrown upward. The author explains that when a stone is thrown, the forced force (القوة القسرية) gradually weakens due to air resistance until it equals the natural force (القوة الطبيعية), at which point the stone stops momentarily. Then, as the forced force continues to weaken, the natural force takes over and the stone falls downward. The author then addresses an objection: if someone argues that the resting force imparted by the thrower is unintentional and thus need not be considered, the author replies that even if unintentional, when it becomes actual, it must be negated by something, and that something cannot be internal to the stone but must be an external accidental cause. The author then presents the argument of the Master (likely Ibn Sina) regarding inclination (الميل) as the final cause of motion: the inclination that brings the stone to a certain point must be present at that point; otherwise, it would require two contradictory inclinations simultaneously, which is impossible. The author then critiques this argument from two angles: first, that it is a quantitative argument and we have no need for inclination; second, that the impossibility of two simultaneous inclinations is not granted, and that circular motion (which is essentially voluntary) cannot be explained by natural inclination. This passage is an expansion of the standard Aristotelian-Avicennan physics, typical of the philosophical works of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Key Terms
القوة القسرية
القوة الطبيعية
الميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what causes the stone to stop at the highest point of its flight?
A) The forced force completely disappears.
B) The forced force becomes equal to the natural force.
C) The stone's inclination vanishes.
D) Air resistance ceases.

Key Takeaway
Rest in projectile motion is a temporary equilibrium between the weakening forced force and the constant natural force, not the absence of all force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the author's explanation to a stone thrown straight upward. Write a short paragraph (3-5 sentences) describing what happens at each stage: the weakening of the forced force, the moment of equality and rest, and the subsequent downward motion. Use the Arabic terms القوة القسرية and القوة الطبيعية correctly.
?

Conditional Reasoning in the Equilibrium Argument
Author argues that if power remains constant, stone must stay at equilibrium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism in the stone equilibrium argument and identify its form (modus ponens).

Core Idea
Equilibrium is a necessary intermediate state in a conditional chain of motion.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's Mulakhkhas critically examines a physical argument about a stone's motion. The author uses a conditional argument to show that if a certain power remains constant, the stone must remain at equilibrium and never fall back. In this lesson, we will parse the logical structure of that conditional argument and learn how to apply similar reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conditional claim
Look for the main 'if-then' statement in the argument. The author claims that if a certain power remains constant (due to no weakening from stopping), then the stone stays in equilibrium and does not fall back.
2
Break down the sub-conditions
Notice the subsidiary conditionals: 'if the power is dominant, it necessitates ascent; if not dominant, descent becomes necessary.' These are used to frame the need for an intermediate equilibrium state.
3
Locate the introduction of equilibrium
The text states: 'Between dominance and being dominated, there must be an intermediate state, which is equilibrium.' This is the key step in the argument that leads to the conclusion.
4
Determine the logical form
The argument follows modus ponens: if the power remains constant (P), then the stone stays in equilibrium (Q). The power remains constant (because stopping does not cause weakness). Therefore, the stone stays in equilibrium (Q).
This is a simplified reconstruction of a chain of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو كان كذلك لوجب أن يصعد الحجر إلى سطح الفلك. وأن القوة ما دامت غالبة اقتضت الصعود، ومتى لم تكن غالبة لزم النزول. بين الغالبية والمغلوبية لا بد من حالة متوسطة وهي المعادلة. وذلك تقضي وقوف الحجر. ثم حال الوقوف لا يكون سبباً للضعف حاصلاً، فلا تضعف تلك القوة عن تلك النتيجة، فيوجب بقاء ملك المعادلة، فيجب أن لا يرجع الحجر. ولكن الجواب عن الأول: أن ذات القوة إذا بقيت كما كانت لكن المواد عند التموج [؟] حيث لا تحرق عن بالشعلة، كالماء إذا حاولنا بريق اتصاله سريعاً فإنه ربما لم يحرق ذلك [؟]. وعن الثاني: أنا لا نسلم وجوب حصول المعادلة في الحالة التي لا توجد فيها الحركة، بل فيما تعاقبت [؟] قبل له أنه متحرك بالعرض.
For if it were so, it would be necessary that the stone ascend to the surface of the sphere. And that as long as the power is dominant, it necessitates ascent, and when it is not dominant, descent becomes necessary. Between dominance and being dominated, there must be an intermediate state, which is equilibrium. That necessitates the stopping of the stone. Then the state of stopping does not cause weakness, so that power does not weaken from that result, so it necessitates the continuation of the equilibrium, so it is necessary that the stone does not fall back. But the answer regarding the first [objection] is: if the essence of the power remains as it was, but the materials upon agitation [?] do not burn by the flame, like water if we try to quickly extinguish its connection, then perhaps that does not burn it [?]. And regarding the second [objection]: we do not accept the necessity of obtaining equilibrium in the state where there is no motion, but rather in what follows [?] it was previously said that it is accidentally moving.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument concerning the motion of a stone, demonstrating an apparent contradiction in a physical theory. The author argues that if a certain power (force) remains constant, then the stone would have to rise to the celestial sphere. The argument proceeds conditionally: if the power is dominant, it necessitates upward motion; if not, it necessitates downward motion. Between these two states there must be an intermediate equilibrium, which causes the stone to stop. Since the state of stopping does not introduce any weakening factor, the equilibrium persists, and the stone should never fall back. The author then entertains two objections and provides responses. The first objection is answered by a physical analogy: even if the power remains, the material conditions (like water not burning quickly) prevent the expected outcome. The second objection challenges the necessity of equilibrium in the absence of motion, but the author maintains that the stone could be accidentally moving. This passage exemplifies the use of conditional logic in natural philosophy, where the if-then structure is employed to draw conclusions about physical behavior.

المعادلة
الغالبية والمغلوبية

Parsing al-Razi's Refutation of Real Priority and Posteriority
Critical refutation of the reality of temporal priority and posteriority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound argument al-Razi uses to refute the reality of priority and posteriority in time, identifying each sub-argument and its logical force.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are mental constructs, not real existents—al-Razi's critique.

Al-Razi challenges the common assumption that temporal priority and posteriority are real attributes. He argues that they are mere mental relations, and reifying them leads to logical contradictions. This lesson guides you through the structure of his compound argument, which involves multiple sub-arguments that build on each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Locate the central claim of the passage: priority (qabliyya) and posteriority (ba'diyya) are not real existents but mental considerations. The author aims to refute the view that they are existent things (wujudiyyan).
2
Extract the first sub-argument: time and increase/decrease
The author begins by denying that time can be subject to increase and decrease, because time is not a fixed thing but a passing thing. He argues that what you want to prove (real time) cannot be subject to such attributes, and what is subject to them is only mental assumption.
3
Analyze the father-son example
The author addresses a counterexample: 'the father is before the son' is known necessarily, so priority must be real. He deconstructs this by showing that the father's existence does not inherently carry a relation of priority; something else must realize the priority. This leads to an infinite regress unless priority is mental.
4
Examine the relational contradiction argument
The author argues that if priority and posteriority were real existents, they would be relations. Relations require subjects to exist simultaneously; thus prior and posterior would exist together, which is contradictory. This is a key reductio ad absurdum.
5
Assess the infinite regress argument
The author further argues that if priority were real, then time would be composed of infinite actual instants, each having priority over the next. This leads to an actual infinite, which is impossible. Also, the priority here would not be like cause-effect, so it would require time to have a time, ad infinitum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الزمان مع الأحيان لا يمكن أن يكون محكوما عليه بالزيادة والنقصان. وما كان محكوما عليه بذلك وهو الفرض الذهني لم يكن هو الزمان في الأعيان. ثم لئن كلمت أنه محكوم عليه بالزيادة والنقصان لكن لا يكون ذلك إلا Then we say: the claim of necessity is invalid because we have shown conclusive proof regarding the matter of motion. Then we turn away from this position. But your doctrine is that what is judged to have increase and decrease must exist. And by this we have gathered from the proof of one who argues that events have a beginning by accepting increase and decrease. Then it is known that time is not of the fixed periods but of the passing things. And if that is the case, then it is not correct to judge it with increase and decrease. Therefore what you want to prove, which is time with the periods, cannot be subject to increase and decrease. And what is subject to that, which is the mental assumption, is not time in external existence. Then if you say that it is subject to increase and decrease, but that is only from hypothetical and considerational matters... So they said like motion: they said the father is before the son is known necessarily, possessing priority. But the existence of the father himself and the priority of the son is impossible, because existence and non-existence have no 'before' regarding them. And priority is not a posteriority that enters upon them. So it is either pure non-existence, which is impossible because it necessitates the lack of priority which is a deficiency and the opposite of non-existence, or a thing that is posited by the mind only, or in the external. And in the external, it is either subsisting by itself, which is impossible for priority because it is a relation and addition, or existing in a subject. Then it is known that everything that bears this relation, like the father is a substance that is not before the son as such. So there must be something that realizes priority and posteriority by itself. And if it is fixed, it is impossible, and if it is passing, it is like motion. And that is by considering motion as imagined: before the mind its occurrence is after, and vice versa. So there must be another existent, which is time and accidents. We do not know that priority and posteriority are two existing things. And what he mentioned other than that from contradiction has been objected to in many places of this book. And what we mention now are two aspects: priority and posteriority are relations. So if they were existent, they would exist together, and if they existed together, their subjects would exist together, necessitating that the prior and posterior exist together as prior and posterior. The state as prior is contradictory. Also, priority if it were an existent thing would either be prior or posterior or simultaneous with respect to everything... I concede that what you mentioned necessitates the proof of time, but it necessitates proof of other points of time: that some parts of time are prior to others, and that priority is due to essence, and the necessary in every case is that one is prior and another subsequent; it must be different from that other in essence; and different essences are actually distinct. But the divisions that can be added to time are infinite, so time would be composed of consecutive infinite actual instants, which is impossible. Also, in the priority of some over others, there is no essential priority like that of cause and effect, because there the prior coexists with the posterior, and time is not like that. Rather, the priority there is like the non-existence of the previous motion with the subsequent. So if this type is not unique to time, then time must have a time, and so on ad infinitum. The summary of all is: the essence of time: we have said about it before: if it is a substance, then it is either a simple or a composite; and if it is an accident, ...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the reality of priority (qabliyya) and posteriority (ba'diyya) in time. The author begins by rejecting the claim that time is subject to increase and decrease, arguing that time is not a fixed thing but a passing thing. Then he addresses a counterargument that the concept of priority and posteriority is necessary, using the example of the father being prior to the son. He analyzes that priority cannot be a real existent because if it were, it would lead to contradictions: prior and posterior would coexist, and priority itself would be either prior, posterior, or simultaneous relative to everything. He also argues that if priority were real, time would be composed of infinite actual instants, which is impossible. The author concludes by noting that priority in time is not like essential priority (cause-effect) but rather like the non-existence of a previous motion with the subsequent. This leads to an infinite regress if time were real. The passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the logical steps.

Key Terms
القبلية (al-qabliyya): priority
البعدية (al-ba'diyya): posteriority
الإضافة (al-idafa): relation

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that real priority and posteriority lead to logical contradiction and infinite regress, therefore they are merely mental constructs.

Proving the Indivisible Part via Motion and Contact
Author argues that the contact point of a moving sphere must be indivisible, proving the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) from the motion of a sphere on a surface and identify the logical steps in the argument.

Core Idea
The contact point of a moving sphere is indivisible, proving that matter is composed of atoms.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a geometrical proof for the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), a foundational doctrine in Ash'arite atomism. The argument uses the example of a rotating sphere to show that contact occurs at discrete points, not continuously. By understanding this proof, you will see how Kalam theologians used physical reasoning to support their metaphysical views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
The author considers a sphere rotating on a surface until it completes a full circle. Note that the sphere contacts the surface at a first point and then at a second point.
The motion is assumed to be continuous in time but the contact is argued to be point-like.
2
Analyze the contact points
The author asserts that there is nothing between the two contact points that differs from them; i.e., no intermediate point. This implies the contact is discrete, not a continuous line.
This is the key step: if contact were continuous, there would be an infinite number of points between any two contacts, but here the author claims there is no intermediate.
3
Apply the analogy
The author compares the composition of contact points to the composition of a line from points, and a volume from surfaces. If a line can be composed of indivisible points, then similarly the path of contact is composed of such points.
This analogy uses the concept of composition (tarkib) to argue that magnitudes are aggregates of indivisibles.
4
Conclude
The author concludes that the place of contact is 'irregular' (ghayr muntazim), meaning it lacks extension and is point-like. This entity is the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza').
The phrase 'la buq al-sullum imkan' suggests further division is impossible; thus the atom is the ultimate unit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت أن موضع الملاقاة غير منظم. فإذا أدرنا الكرة على السطح حتى تتم الدائرة فلا شك أنها نالت الملاقاة الحاصلة بنقطة، فحصلت الملاقاة بنقطة ثانية، وليس بين النقطتين شيء يغايرهما. فالخلاف في الملاقاة الحاصلة أول زمان حصول الملاقاة بالنقطة الأولى كالخلاف في الملاقاة بالنقطة الأولى. فإذا حصل الخط عن تركيب النقط، حصل أيضًا الخط عن تركيب الخطوط، والحجم عن تركيب السطوح. فإذن موضع الملاقاة من الكرة شيء غير منظم، حصل من انضمامه إلى أمثاله العظم والمقادير، وهو الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، لا بُق السلم إمكان.
Thus it is proven that the place of contact is irregular. So if we rotate the sphere on the surface until it completes a circle, there is no doubt that it obtains contact that occurs at a point, and then contact occurs at a second point, and there is nothing between the two points that differs from them. For the dispute concerning the contact occurring at the first moment of obtaining contact with the first point is like the dispute concerning the contact with the first point. So if a line is obtained by the composition of points, then also a line is obtained by the composition of lines, and a volume by the composition of surfaces. So then the place of contact of the sphere is something irregular, obtained by its aggregation to its likes of magnitude and quantities, and it is the indivisible part; it is impossible that the ladder of possibility be upon it.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents a geometrical proof for the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), which is a cornerstone of atomistic physics in the Islamic Kalam tradition. The argument proceeds from the observed motion of a sphere on a plane. It begins by establishing that the sphere's contact with the surface occurs at a series of discrete points. When the sphere completes a rotation, it contacts at a first point, then a second point, and there is no intermediate point between them—meaning the contact is discontinuous. The author analogizes the situation to the composition of a line from points: just as a line can be composed of indivisible points, so too the contact path is composed of points. The conclusion states that the sphere's contact point is 'irregular' (ghayr muntazim), meaning it is not extended but point-like, and when aggregated with similar points it produces magnitudes. This entity is precisely the indivisible part, which atomists argue is the fundamental building block of all physical bodies. The proof counters Zeno's paradoxes by asserting that motion is possible through a succession of discrete contacts. The phrase 'la buq al-sullum imkan' likely means 'it is impossible to ascend the ladder of possibility upon it', i.e., further division is impossible. The passage thus serves to validate the atomistic doctrine through a physical-geometrical demonstration.

Key Terms
موضع الملاقاة
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
غير منظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument in this passage, what does the composition of the contact points reveal about the nature of the sphere's path?
A) The path is continuous and infinitely divisible.
B) The path is composed of indivisible points.
C) The sphere does not actually move.
D) The contact point is an extended line.

Key Takeaway
The motion of a sphere proves that matter is composed of indivisible parts (atoms), because contact occurs at discrete points with no intermediate continuum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the proof using your own diagram: Show the sphere, the surface, and the discrete contact points. Explain why the argument supports atomism.
?
Compare with Aristotelian continuity: How would a philosopher who believes in continuous motion respond to this proof? Identify potential objections.

Al-Razi's Refutation of Point-by-Point Contact
Al-Razi refutes sphere-plane contact argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical contradiction al-Razi exposes in the argument that a moving sphere contacts a plane at a single point at each instant.

Core Idea
Al-Razi shows that point-by-point contact leads to a contradiction about instants and continuity.

In Islamic kalām, refuting opposing views is a key method for establishing correct positions. Here, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi takes on an argument that a sphere in motion contacts a plane at only one point at any instant. His critique reveals that this view forces a contradictory account of time and space: either there must be a temporal gap between contacts, or the sphere must be in two places at once. Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to detect hidden assumptions in physical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Claim
Find the claim that the sphere contacts the plane at one point at a time, and that contact moves point by point. This is the target of al-Razi's refutation.
Look for phrases like 'الوجود أبداً نقطة واحدة' (existence is always one point).
2
Extract al-Razi's First Objection
Al-Razi argues that if contact ceases at one point and begins at another, there must be either an intervening time or not. Both lead to absurdities: if there is an intervening time, the sphere is stationary; if not, instants are not continuous.
Focus on the phrase 'فإن كان بين هذين الآن زمان' (if there is a time between these two instants).
3
Trace the Alternative Argument
Al-Razi also considers a variant without sphere and plane: a moving line contacting a line. The same contradiction arises about points and temporal gaps.
This shows the argument's generality.
4
Assess the Refutation's Structure
Al-Razi uses reductio ad absurdum: he assumes the opponent's claim and derives a contradiction, thereby disproving it. He also exposes an infinite regress or composition paradox.
The contradiction involves the nature of points and instants.

From small surfaces of birds and the opinions went to infinity. And if a small surface is possible from one side in the big one, yes perhaps we are not required to rule on its existence, but the speech is not based on existence... We have prevention from the tune of rolling, so it is not harmful since there is no doubt in your safety... And from it follows the ideal in striving... He said: they are then by line. I said: this is false, the line... so if the sphere went striving... a line coinciding with the straight surface, and coinciding with a straight line is straight... this is contradictory. His statement: the existence is always one point. For if contact ceases from the first point, then there occurs a non-contact... the non-contact has a beginning realized in it... so the now which is the first time of non-contact is either the now in which contact occurred or not; the first is false, otherwise the sphere at one now would be at one point... contradictory. So it is other. Either between these two nows there is a time or not. If there is, then the sphere in that time is in contact with the first point, so it is stationary in that time. If no time intervenes, then nows are successive. Also, the now which is the first time of non-contact with that point, either the sphere is in contact with another point in it or not. If not, then the sphere is not in contact with the surface, contradictory. If it is in contact, then either between them there is an intermediary, so the sphere's contact with that intermediary precedes its contact with the second point, so its contact with the surface with the second point is not the first instant of non-contact with the first point, which was assumed, a contradiction; or there is no intermediary, so the points coincide... this is the utmost smoothness in the argument, and there is no salvation in it except to say that it is with the arc... and we have said that the statement of what does not continue with the proof of the part of the line standing on... until the end must be that every part of it is the moving line because motion without its extension is unintelligible, so the moving locus is composed of things whose extremity of the moving line is a point, and the meeting of the point with the point and the moving locus is composed of points, which is the desired. And this argument is close to the letter and better than it in that there is no need to prove the sphere and surface. For the dispute is that the moving line contacts the moving locus by a point, then after that it contacts another point. But some may say: between the two points there is a line and between them a time. If that is denied, then the desired is obtained. Or if it is said that the contact between the two points is instantaneous and its occurrence is also instantaneous, then if the contact is instantaneous, then the now which is the first time of non-contact with that point either is different or not, and the previous argument returns. The second argument: the point of non-contact with it is a contradiction or another point... then it must be in contact with the first point, so contact occurs at the time of occurrence.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi of an opponent's argument regarding the motion of a sphere on a plane and the nature of points and contact. The opponent likely holds that a sphere in motion contacts the plane at only one point at any instant, and that as it moves, it successively contacts different points. Al-Razi attacks this view by showing it leads to contradictions involving infinite regress, the nature of instants, and the composition of lines from points. The argument appears to be part of a larger dialectic on atomism and continuity. Al-Razi first responds to a claim about lines coinciding with surfaces, then addresses the assertion that only one point exists at a time. He shows that if contact ceases at one point and begins at another, there must be an intervening time or not; both options lead to absurdities. He then considers an alternative argument that avoids assuming a sphere and plane, focusing instead on a moving line. The commentary must remain within the Arabic text, but due to severe OCR corruption, the exact logical steps are obscured. The key logical principle at stake is the impossibility of a point-by-point contact without either temporal gap or compositional paradox.

8 min
The Indivisible Atom Refuted: Parts Exist Actually
Author refutes atomism by proving parts are actually distinct prior to division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument that the parts of a body must exist actually before division, and identify the absurd consequence if they did not.

Core Idea
If parts were not actual before division, a fly piercing the sea would create a new sea.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi launches a devastating critique against the atomist doctrine that bodies are composed of indivisible parts that only come into existence at the moment of division. He argues that such a view leads to logical absurdities, as demonstrated by the example of a fly piercing the sea. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to understand and reconstruct this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the atomist claim being refuted
Atomists hold that a body's parts are not actually existent before division; they come into existence only when the body is divided. The author calls this the 'indivisible part' (juz' la yatajazzā).
2
Extract the sea and fly thought experiment
Al-Razi asks: if a fly pierces the surface of the sea with a needle, and the parts of the sea at that point were not actual before, then the sea at that location is destroyed and a new sea is created. This is absurd because we know the sea remains the same.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if parts are not actual, then division creates new bodies, leading to constant creation and destruction.
3
Generalize the argument to any body
The same applies to any body: if division creates new parts, then every division would destroy the original body and create a new one, which contradicts our experience of continuous bodies.
This shows that parts must be actually present in the body, even if joined.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the parts of a body exist actually (bil-fi'l) even before separation. They are not merely potential. Thus, the atomist view is false.
The author distinguishes between actual existence and actual separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لزم المسامحة في الانقسام، محلها فالوحدة منقسمة، هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن منقسمًا، عاد الإشكال الأول من أن كونه غير منضم إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره، أو لا يكون من لوازمها. لا يقال: الجسم من حيث هو جسم ليس إلا الحجم، وكونه منظمًا أو غير منظم وصفان عارضان. فإذا أثبت به فساد الحجم، فالجسم من حيث هو جسم سواء، لا سرعة في الانتقال. ثم هو هو مغاير لما يعرض له، لكن الماهية من حيث هي هي لا توجد في الأعيان إلا مع المنضمات، فإذا بطلت بطل ذلك الشخص. فإن هذا الإنسان إذا بطل، فلا نقول بأن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان قام عوضه إلا إذا بطل ذلك الشخص وحدث شخص آخر بعده. فكذلك هنا: الجسم وإن كان من حيث هو جسم مغايرًا لكونه منظمًا أو غير منظم، إلا أن الشخص منه لا يوجد إلا مع أحد هذين العرضين. فإذا بطل ذلك الوصف، فلا بد أن يبطل ذلك الشخص ويحدث، فيلزم ما ذكرنا من كون التقسيم إعدامًا. فإذا أوردنا القسمة على جسم واحد حامل لجنسين، فلا تخلو: إما أن تكون تلك الصورتان حاصلتين قبل تلك القسمة، أو لا تكونان حاصلتين. فإن كان الأول، فهما متحدان؟ وإن كان الثاني، فقد حدثتا عند القسمة. فإذا طرقت البعوضة ووقعت على البحر المحيط، وشقت برأس إبرة هاجرًا من سطح البحر، لزم أن يقال: إن ذلك البحر الذي كان موجودًا قبله آخر، لأنه من تفرق الاتصال في ذلك الموضع فقد فني ذلك المقدار. وكذلك هنا: المقادير والجسميات. لكن هناك هيولى شركة، فإن تلك الهيولى إن كانت عند وحدة الجسم واحدة، فعند القسمة قد انقسمت فيعود المحال. وإن كانت مستعدة لحصول الانفتاحات الممكنة، فالمقدار والجسمية الحالة في كل واحد منها غير الحالة في الآخر، فتكون الأجزاء كل واحد منها مغايرًا للآخر بمادته وصورته، ويعود المحال. إذا نظرت إلى الجسم البسيط، علمت بالضرورة أن أحد نصفيه مغاير للنصف الآخر وممتاز عنه، فكيف يمكن مع ذلك الحكم بكون ذلك الجسم واحدًا في الحقيقة؟ ولا يقال: لا نقول بالكثرة بالفعل إنما حصلت بسبب الوهم أو اختلاف الأعراض، فإن الإشارة متوقفة على التمييز في المشار إليه، فيستحيل أن يمتنع الإشارة إلى ما ليس له تمييز في ذاته. فلو جعلنا التمييز في المشار إليه معللاً بالاحتياج في الإشارة لزم الدور. وكذلك القول في قولهم من وقوع التمييز بسبب اختلاف الأعراض. وأيضًا فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن أحد جانبي هذا الحجم قد كان ممتازًا عن جانبه الآخر، سواء كان يتوهم متوهم أو لم يتوهم. فالقول بأن هذا الامتياز إنما حصل حال التوهم معايرة. وإذا حملنا البحر البسيط، فالجسم المحكوم عليه ليس إلا المجموع المتخلف من الأبعاض المفروضة فيه. فلو قلنا إن تلك الأبعاض أمور تحدث عند القسمة، وأن الموجود قبل القسمة أمر آخر، كان ذلك خروجًا عن المعقول. فإنا لا نعقل من جملة هذا الجسم إلا المجموع الحاصل من أجزائه، وثبت بهذه الوجوه أن أجزاء الجسم حاصلة بالفعل، وإن كانت حالة الاتصال غير منفصلة بالفعل. قلت: إن كونها بالفعل ليس انفصال بعض عن البعض حاصلاً بالفعل، ومعلوم أن الدلالة التي ذكرناها لا تختلف حالها بأن كانت الأجزاء منفصلة أو متصلة. ثانيًا: حكمت أن الجسم في نفسه واحد بالفعل، لكن الحجة المذكورة
If it is said: Then tampering follows in division, its place is that the unit is divided; this is a contradiction. And if it is not divided, the first problem returns: that its being not composed is either due to itself or another, or it is not among its necessary concomitants. It cannot be said: The body insofar as it is body is nothing but volume, and its being organized or unorganized are two accidental attributes. So if the corruption of volume is established by that, then the body insofar as it is body is equivalent, there is no quickness in transition. Then it is the same as what occurs to it, but the quiddity insofar as it is quiddity does not exist in external beings except with the attributes. So if the attribute perishes, that individual perishes. For if this human perishes, we do not say that the human insofar as he is human is replaced unless that individual perishes and another individual arises afterward. Similarly here: the body, even if insofar as it is body is different from being organized or unorganized, yet the individual from it does not exist except with one of these two accidents. So if that attribute perishes, necessarily that individual perishes and arises, leading to what we mentioned about division being destruction. So if we bring division to a single body carrying two genera, then it is either that those two forms were present before that division, or they were not present. If the first, then they are two? If the second, then they occur at division. So if a fly lands and falls upon the surrounding sea, and pierces with the tip of a needle moving from the surface of the sea, it would be necessary to say: that sea which existed before is another, because from the separation of connection in that spot, that quantity has perished, and likewise here. But there is a shared hyle, for if that hyle was one at the unity of the body, then at division it has divided so the impossibility returns. And if it was prepared for possible openings, then the quantity and corporeity present in each one is other than that in the other, so the parts each one is different from the other in its matter and form, and the impossibility returns. When you look at the simple body, you know necessarily that one of its halves is different from the other half and distinct from it, so how is it possible with that to judge that body as one in reality? And it cannot be said: we do not claim multiplicity actually; it only arises due to imagination or difference of accidents. For indication is dependent on distinction in the indicated object, so it is impossible for indication to be absent regarding that which has no distinction in itself. So if we make distinction in the indicated object dependent on need for indication, circularity follows. And similarly the statement regarding their claim that distinction occurs due to difference of accidents. Also we know necessarily that one side of this volume was distinct from its other side, whether an imaginer imagines or does not imagine. So the claim that this distinction only occurred at the time of imagining is objectionable. And if we bear the simple sea, the body that is judged upon is nothing but the aggregate composed of the supposed parts in it. So if we say that those parts are things that occur at division, and that what existed before division is something else, that is a departure from the rational. For we do not conceive of the entirety of this body except as the aggregate resulting from its parts, and it is established by these ways that the parts of the body exist actually, even if in the state of connection they are not actually separated. I say: Their being actual is not the separation of some from others occurring actually, and it is known that the indication we mentioned does not differ in its state whether the parts are separated or connected. Secondly: You judged that the body in itself is one actually, but the mentioned proof...
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of atomism, specifically the doctrine that bodies are composed of indivisible parts that only come into existence at the moment of division. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that such a view leads to absurdities. The passage begins by considering the case of a body that is divisible: if one says its parts are not actually existent before division, then division creates new parts, which is impossible because it would mean the original body is destroyed and a new one created. The example of a human: when a human perishes, we do not say that humanity as such is replaced, but that individual ceases. Similarly, a body's individual existence depends on its accidental attributes (like being organized or not). If those attributes are destroyed, the individual body is destroyed. Then the author uses the example of a fly landing on the sea and piercing it: if the sea were not actually composed of parts, the tiny hole would create a new sea, which is absurd. He then introduces the concept of hyle (matter) shared by the parts: if the hyle is one, division divides it; if the hyle is prepared for multiple openings, then each part has its own matter and form, leading to an infinite regress. Further, the author argues that we necessarily know that one half of a simple body is distinct from the other half, so the body cannot be one in reality. He refutes the counterargument that multiplicity is only due to imagination or accidental differences: if indication (pointing) requires distinction in the indicated object, then if the object has no intrinsic distinction, we cannot point to it, leading to circularity if we say distinction arises from pointing. Also, we know that one side is distinct from the other regardless of imagination. Thus, the parts must be actually existent in the body, even if not actually separated. The author concludes that the body is one in the sense of a connected aggregate, but its parts are numerous actually. This argument connects to the kalam debate on atoms (juz' la yatajazzā) and supports the Ash'ari or philosophical view that bodies are infinitely divisible.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (juz' lā yatajazzā): The indivisible atom; al-Razi refutes the claim that such parts exist only potentially before division. [Note: Stable term in kalam and falsafa; al-Razi uses it critically.]
بالفعل (bil-fi'l): Actually existent; al-Razi argues that parts of a body are actual while still connected. [Note: Standard usage.]
هيولى (hayūlā): Hyle, prime matter; the shared substrate of parts [Note: Aristotelian term used in the hyle argument.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what absurd consequence follows if the parts of a body are not actual before division?
A) The body becomes indivisible.
B) A fly piercing the sea would create a new sea at the point of puncture.
C) The body would be composed of infinite parts.
D) The body would be one in the same sense as its parts.

Key Takeaway
The parts of a body must be actually existent (bil-fi'l) before division, otherwise every division would result in the destruction of the original body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct al-Razi's argument against atomism using the example of a fly piercing the sea. Write a step-by-step logical presentation showing how the absurdity follows.
?

The Hyle Argument and the Impossibility of a Shared Substrate
Author refutes atomism by proving parts are actually distinct prior to division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain al-Razi's hyle argument against atomism, and why a single shared matter leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
A single hyle cannot be both undivided and divided; if each part has its own hyle, then parts are distinct in matter and form.

Al-Razi further strengthens his refutation by considering the concept of hyle (prime matter) that underlies all parts of a body. He shows that whether the hyle is one or many, the atomist position leads to an impossibility. This lesson will guide you through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities for hyle
Al-Razi presents two options: either the hyle (matter) is one and the same for all parts of the body, or it is prepared for multiple different quanta.
2
Analyze the first option: one hyle
If the hyle is one when the body is one, then when the body divides, the hyle must divide as well. But if the hyle divides, then it is not a continuous unity, leading to the same problem: the parts must have been distinct before division.
This leads to a contradiction: the hyle would be both undivided and divided.
3
Analyze the second option: multiple hyle
If the hyle is prepared for multiple possible quanta, then each part has its own specific quantity and corporeity distinct from the others. Thus, parts are different in both matter and form, so they are actually distinct individuals.
This also leads to the conclusion that parts are actual before division.
4
Consider the necessary distinction of halves
Al-Razi adds: we know necessarily that one half of a simple body is distinct from the other half. This distinction is not due to imagination or accidents but is intrinsic. Therefore, the body cannot be a true unit; its parts are actual.
This argument appeals to direct intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لزم المسامحة في الانقسام، محلها فالوحدة منقسمة، هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن منقسمًا، عاد الإشكال الأول من أن كونه غير منضم إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره، أو لا يكون من لوازمها. لا يقال: الجسم من حيث هو جسم ليس إلا الحجم، وكونه منظمًا أو غير منظم وصفان عارضان. فإذا أثبت به فساد الحجم، فالجسم من حيث هو جسم سواء، لا سرعة في الانتقال. ثم هو هو مغاير لما يعرض له، لكن الماهية من حيث هي هي لا توجد في الأعيان إلا مع المنضمات، فإذا بطلت بطل ذلك الشخص. فإن هذا الإنسان إذا بطل، فلا نقول بأن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان قام عوضه إلا إذا بطل ذلك الشخص وحدث شخص آخر بعده. فكذلك هنا: الجسم وإن كان من حيث هو جسم مغايرًا لكونه منظمًا أو غير منظم، إلا أن الشخص منه لا يوجد إلا مع أحد هذين العرضين. فإذا بطل ذلك الوصف، فلا بد أن يبطل ذلك الشخص ويحدث، فيلزم ما ذكرنا من كون التقسيم إعدامًا. فإذا أوردنا القسمة على جسم واحد حامل لجنسين، فلا تخلو: إما أن تكون تلك الصورتان حاصلتين قبل تلك القسمة، أو لا تكونان حاصلتين. فإن كان الأول، فهما متحدان؟ وإن كان الثاني، فقد حدثتا عند القسمة. فإذا طرقت البعوضة ووقعت على البحر المحيط، وشقت برأس إبرة هاجرًا من سطح البحر، لزم أن يقال: إن ذلك البحر الذي كان موجودًا قبله آخر، لأنه من تفرق الاتصال في ذلك الموضع فقد فني ذلك المقدار. وكذلك هنا: المقادير والجسميات. لكن هناك هيولى شركة، فإن تلك الهيولى إن كانت عند وحدة الجسم واحدة، فعند القسمة قد انقسمت فيعود المحال. وإن كانت مستعدة لحصول الانفتاحات الممكنة، فالمقدار والجسمية الحالة في كل واحد منها غير الحالة في الآخر، فتكون الأجزاء كل واحد منها مغايرًا للآخر بمادته وصورته، ويعود المحال. إذا نظرت إلى الجسم البسيط، علمت بالضرورة أن أحد نصفيه مغاير للنصف الآخر وممتاز عنه، فكيف يمكن مع ذلك الحكم بكون ذلك الجسم واحدًا في الحقيقة؟ ولا يقال: لا نقول بالكثرة بالفعل إنما حصلت بسبب الوهم أو اختلاف الأعراض، فإن الإشارة متوقفة على التمييز في المشار إليه، فيستحيل أن يمتنع الإشارة إلى ما ليس له تمييز في ذاته. فلو جعلنا التمييز في المشار إليه معللاً بالاحتياج في الإشارة لزم الدور. وكذلك القول في قولهم من وقوع التمييز بسبب اختلاف الأعراض. وأيضًا فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن أحد جانبي هذا الحجم قد كان ممتازًا عن جانبه الآخر، سواء كان يتوهم متوهم أو لم يتوهم. فالقول بأن هذا الامتياز إنما حصل حال التوهم معايرة. وإذا حملنا البحر البسيط، فالجسم المحكوم عليه ليس إلا المجموع المتخلف من الأبعاض المفروضة فيه. فلو قلنا إن تلك الأبعاض أمور تحدث عند القسمة، وأن الموجود قبل القسمة أمر آخر، كان ذلك خروجًا عن المعقول. فإنا لا نعقل من جملة هذا الجسم إلا المجموع الحاصل من أجزائه، وثبت بهذه الوجوه أن أجزاء الجسم حاصلة بالفعل، وإن كانت حالة الاتصال غير منفصلة بالفعل. قلت: إن كونها بالفعل ليس انفصال بعض عن البعض حاصلاً بالفعل، ومعلوم أن الدلالة التي ذكرناها لا تختلف حالها بأن كانت الأجزاء منفصلة أو متصلة. ثانيًا: حكمت أن الجسم في نفسه واحد بالفعل، لكن الحجة المذكورة
If it is said: Then tampering follows in division, its place is that the unit is divided; this is a contradiction. And if it is not divided, the first problem returns: that its being not composed is either due to itself or another, or it is not among its necessary concomitants. It cannot be said: The body insofar as it is body is nothing but volume, and its being organized or unorganized are two accidental attributes. So if the corruption of volume is established by that, then the body insofar as it is body is equivalent, there is no quickness in transition. Then it is the same as what occurs to it, but the quiddity insofar as it is quiddity does not exist in external beings except with the attributes. So if the attribute perishes, that individual perishes. For if this human perishes, we do not say that the human insofar as he is human is replaced unless that individual perishes and another individual arises afterward. Similarly here: the body, even if insofar as it is body is different from being organized or unorganized, yet the individual from it does not exist except with one of these two accidents. So if that attribute perishes, necessarily that individual perishes and arises, leading to what we mentioned about division being destruction. So if we bring division to a single body carrying two genera, then it is either that those two forms were present before that division, or they were not present. If the first, then they are two? If the second, then they occur at division. So if a fly lands and falls upon the surrounding sea, and pierces with the tip of a needle moving from the surface of the sea, it would be necessary to say: that sea which existed before is another, because from the separation of connection in that spot, that quantity has perished, and likewise here. But there is a shared hyle, for if that hyle was one at the unity of the body, then at division it has divided so the impossibility returns. And if it was prepared for possible openings, then the quantity and corporeity present in each one is other than that in the other, so the parts each one is different from the other in its matter and form, and the impossibility returns. When you look at the simple body, you know necessarily that one of its halves is different from the other half and distinct from it, so how is it possible with that to judge that body as one in reality? And it cannot be said: we do not claim multiplicity actually; it only arises due to imagination or difference of accidents. For indication is dependent on distinction in the indicated object, so it is impossible for indication to be absent regarding that which has no distinction in itself. So if we make distinction in the indicated object dependent on need for indication, circularity follows. And similarly the statement regarding their claim that distinction occurs due to difference of accidents. Also we know necessarily that one side of this volume was distinct from its other side, whether an imaginer imagines or does not imagine. So the claim that this distinction only occurred at the time of imagining is objectionable. And if we bear the simple sea, the body that is judged upon is nothing but the aggregate composed of the supposed parts in it. So if we say that those parts are things that occur at division, and that what existed before division is something else, that is a departure from the rational. For we do not conceive of the entirety of this body except as the aggregate resulting from its parts, and it is established by these ways that the parts of the body exist actually, even if in the state of connection they are not actually separated. I say: Their being actual is not the separation of some from others occurring actually, and it is known that the indication we mentioned does not differ in its state whether the parts are separated or connected. Secondly: You judged that the body in itself is one actually, but the mentioned proof...
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of atomism, specifically the doctrine that bodies are composed of indivisible parts that only come into existence at the moment of division. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that such a view leads to absurdities. The passage begins by considering the case of a body that is divisible: if one says its parts are not actually existent before division, then division creates new parts, which is impossible because it would mean the original body is destroyed and a new one created. The example of a human: when a human perishes, we do not say that humanity as such is replaced, but that individual ceases. Similarly, a body's individual existence depends on its accidental attributes (like being organized or not). If those attributes are destroyed, the individual body is destroyed. Then the author uses the example of a fly landing on the sea and piercing it: if the sea were not actually composed of parts, the tiny hole would create a new sea, which is absurd. He then introduces the concept of hyle (matter) shared by the parts: if the hyle is one, division divides it; if the hyle is prepared for multiple openings, then each part has its own matter and form, leading to an infinite regress. Further, the author argues that we necessarily know that one half of a simple body is distinct from the other half, so the body cannot be one in reality. He refutes the counterargument that multiplicity is only due to imagination or accidental differences: if indication (pointing) requires distinction in the indicated object, then if the object has no intrinsic distinction, we cannot point to it, leading to circularity if we say distinction arises from pointing. Also, we know that one side is distinct from the other regardless of imagination. Thus, the parts must be actually existent in the body, even if not actually separated. The author concludes that the body is one in the sense of a connected aggregate, but its parts are numerous actually. This argument connects to the kalam debate on atoms (juz' la yatajazzā) and supports the Ash'ari or philosophical view that bodies are infinitely divisible.

Key Terms
هيولى (hayūlā): Hyle, prime matter; the shared substrate that supposedly underlies all parts. [Note: In Aristotelian tradition, hyle is the substrate; al-Razi uses it here to show incoherence of atomism.]
بالفعل (bil-fi'l): Actually existent; parts are actual before division. [Note: Standard usage.]
مقدار (miqdar): Quantity; each part has its own specific quantity. [Note: Used in physics discussions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's hyle argument, what is the consequence if the hyle is one and the same for the whole body?
A) The hyle remains undivided after division.
B) The hyle must divide when the body divides, contradicting its unity.
C) The hyle becomes multiple automatically.
D) The parts become potential.

Key Takeaway
The hyle argument shows that whether matter is one or many, the parts must be actual before division, refuting atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Use al-Razi's hyle argument to critique an atomist position: 'The body's parts are only potential before division, and a single matter underlies them.' Show why both options lead to absurdity.
?
Find a passage in a kalam text (e.g., al-Ghazali or al-Sanusiyya) that discusses the unity of body. Explain how al-Razi's argument would strengthen or challenge that discussion.
