Analyzing a Critique of Infinite Divisibility
Author presents and critiques the Peripatetic view on infinite separative division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a logical objection in a dialectical argument and evaluate its force using the critique_dissector.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility is debated: the objection that parts may differ in essence challenges the assumption of homogeneity.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the Peripatetic argument for infinite separative division and then immediately raises a powerful objection. Understanding how objections are constructed is essential in the Islamic dialectical tradition (adab al-bahth). We will analyze the logical moves the author makes, focusing on the critique structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Peripatetic claim
Find the sentence where the Peripatetic position is stated: that separative division proceeds to infinity. Note the two reasons given.
The claim is the target of the later objection.
2
Extract the two reasons for infinite divisibility
Look for the phrases 'li-wajhayn' or similar. The first reason involves every body having two ends distinguished by awareness; the second is less clear due to OCR but relates to essence or concomitants.
The text says 'awwalan' and 'thaniyan' implicitly.
3
Locate the objection and its basis
The objection begins with 'wa li-qā'il an yaqūl' or similar. Identify the question: 'Why is it not permissible that each of the parts be different in essence from the other part?'
This challenges the assumption that all parts are homogeneous.
4
Assess the force of the objection
Consider whether the objection refutes the Peripatetic argument or merely weakens it. The author notes the alternative is improbable but not impossible without evidence.
This is a dialectical move: the burden of proof is on the Peripatetics.
5
Determine what would be required to refute it
Think about what evidence would be needed to show that parts cannot differ in essence. This relates to metaphysical principles about the nature of bodies.
The author leaves the question open, indicating the debate continues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفق المشاؤون على أن القسمة الانفكاكية حاصلة إلى غير النهاية لوجنين: أما أولا فلأن كل جسم في ذاته يعرض فيه طرفان يتميز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر بالوعي، فالتلاصق النصفي عن الاتصال الذي يتم زواله إن كان لنفس الماهية أو لوازمها وجد أن معضل ذلك بين الجزءين كان عمل من نصف الجزء الواحد من الانفصال، إما حصل بين الجزءين الاتحاد ماهيات تلك الأجزاء أو وجوب الاشتراك في اللوازم، وإن لم تكن كذلك فتح تبدل الاتصال بالانفصال بالعكس، وهو المطلوب. ولقائل أن يقول: لم لا يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من الأجزاء مخالفا في الماهية للجزء الآخر؟ وهذا وإن كان مستبعدا لكن لا بد في إبطاله من الدلالة.
The Peripatetics agreed that separative division proceeds to infinity for two reasons: First, because every body in itself has two ends, each distinguished from the other by awareness, so the half-adhesion from the connection whose removal occurs—if it belongs to the essence itself or its concomitants—then the difficulty between the two parts would be like the action of half of one part from separation; either the union of the essences of those parts occurs between the two parts, or the necessity of sharing in the concomitants; and if not so, then the change of connection to separation is reversed, which is what is desired. And one may say: Why is it not permissible that each of the parts be different in essence from the other part? And this, though unlikely, requires evidence for its refutation.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents a critical argument: the Peripatetic view that bodies are infinitely divisible is stated and then an objection is raised. The Peripatetics give two reasons for infinite divisibility, but the objection questions the assumption that all parts are homogeneous in essence. The author notes that even if the alternative—parts differing in essence—is improbable, it cannot be rejected without proof. This reflects the dialectical method: stating a claim, supporting it, then raising a counter-claim that requires further evidence.

Key Terms
القسمة الانفكاكية
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection to the Peripatetic argument for infinite separative division?
A) Parts are too small to be divided infinitely.
B) Parts of a body may differ in essence, which would prevent infinite division.
C) Only intellectual division is possible, not physical.
D) The Peripatetics did not provide enough reasons.

Key Takeaway
The objection that parts may differ in essence shows that an argument for infinite divisibility depends on an unproven assumption of homogeneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar objection against the claim that 'every created thing has a cause.' What assumption would you challenge? (Hint: think about the nature of the cause or the possibility of uncaused things.)
?
Identify the assumption about homogeneity in the Peripatetic argument and explain how the objection targets it. Then suggest one piece of evidence the Peripatetics might use to defend their position.

Reconstructing the Categorical Syllogism for Matter in All Bodies
Author argues that every body has matter, responding to the objection about celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves every body requires matter, and identify its middle term.

Core Idea
Every body has potency; potency needs a substrate; therefore every body needs matter.

In this passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas, he argues that all bodies—including celestial spheres—are composed of matter and form. The argument hinges on the concept of potency: a body, as such, has potential for change, and that potential requires a receptive substrate, which is matter. We will extract the underlying categorical syllogism and test its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The conclusion is that every body is composed of matter (hayulā). Look for the phrase 'جثومية الفلك إلى الهيولى' and the final 'فكذلك في جميع الأجسام'.
2
Extract the minor premise
Find where the author states that every body has potency. The line 'كل جسم من حيث هو جسم موجود بالفعل، ومن حيث هو مستعد لاستعداد ما هو بالقوة' shows that every body has potential (quwwa).
3
Extract the major premise
The author asserts that potency requires a substrate: 'مبدأ له القوة وهو الهيولى'. This is the general principle that whatever has potency needs matter.
4
Form the categorical syllogism
Combine the premises: Major: Everything with potency is composed of matter. Minor: Every body has potency. Conclusion: Every body is composed of matter. This is a first-figure AAA (Barbara) syllogism.
5
Address the objection about celestial spheres
The objector claims spheres are not divisible, so they cannot have matter. The author replies that matter is needed for their bodyness (juthūmiyya) not just divisibility. This shows that the syllogism's minor premise applies to all bodies without exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا الفصل والقابل للانفصال إما أن يكون هو الاتصال، وهو محال، لأن القابل يساوق المقبول، والاتصال لا يساوق الانفصال. أو غير الاتصال، كان قابلا للاتصال حال وجوده ثم صار قابلا للانفصال بعد ذلك، وهو الهيولى. لا يقال: الأفلاك غير قابلة للانفصال، فكيف تكون مركبة من الجثومية والهيولى؟ لأنا نقول: القابلة للانفصال حاجة جثوميتها إلى الصورة لا تنفي كونها مركبة من الهيولى، لأن الطبيعة الواحدة لا تتخلف مقتضاها، فكل جسم من حيث هو جسم موجود بالفعل، ومن حيث هو مستعد لاستعداد ما هو بالقوة. وقد ثبت أن الواحد الصادر عنه لا يصدر إلا الواحد، فإذا الجسم مركب من مبدأ له القوة وهو الهيولى، ومبدأ له الفعل وهو الصورة. وإذا ثبت افتقار جثومية الفلك إلى الهيولى، فكذلك في جميع الأجسام.
When division occurs, that which receives division is either the connection itself—that is impossible, because the receiver accompanies the received, and connection does not accompany disconnection—or something other than connection, which was receptive of connection at the time of its existence then became receptive of disconnection afterwards, and that is matter. It cannot be said: the celestial spheres are not receptive to disconnection, so how are they composed of bodyness and matter? For we say: the receptivity of their bodyness to form does not negate their composition from matter, because a single nature does not fail to necessitate its implication. So every body, insofar as it is body, exists in act, and insofar as it has a certain disposition, is in potency. And it has been established that from the one, only one issues. So the body is composed of a principle that has potency—which is matter—and a principle that has act—which is form. And if it is established that the bodyness of the sphere is in need of matter, then likewise in all bodies.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that all bodies, including celestial spheres, are composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). The author begins by arguing that when a body is divided, the substrate that receives division cannot be the connection (ittisal) itself, because a receiver must be distinct from what it receives; connection and disconnection are opposites. Therefore, the receiver must be something that first had connection and later becomes receptive to disconnection—this is matter. An objection is raised: celestial spheres are not divisible, so how can they have matter? The author responds by stating that whether or not a body is actually divisible, its bodyness (juthumiyya) inherently involves potency (quwwa) and act (fi'l). Every body, as body, exists in act, but it also has a potential for states (like division). The principle of this potential is matter, and the principle of actual form is form. Since from a single principle only one effect issues, the body must have two distinct principles: one for potential (matter) and one for actuality (form). This reasoning applies to all bodies, including celestial ones. The argument uses categorical syllogisms implicitly: every body has potency; everything with potency requires a substrate for that potency—matter; therefore every body has matter. The author then extends this to all bodies universally.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
هيولى
صورة
قابل
جثومية
فلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why cannot the connection itself be the receiver of division?
A) Because connection is a quality, not a substance.
B) Because the receiver must accompany the received, but connection does not accompany disconnection.
C) Because the connection is eternal and cannot change.
D) Because the connection is a concept, not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The essential logical point is that all bodies, including celestial spheres, must be composed of matter because their bodyness involves potentiality, which requires a substrate distinct from actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism hidden in the argument from 'every body has potency' to 'every body requires matter'. Write out the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion in standard form.
?
Identify which term serves as the middle term (hadd al-awsat) in the syllogism you reconstructed.

Refutation of Natural Place and Divine Takhsis
Author refutes natural place and asserts divine assignment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key theological claim in this passage and distinguish it from the naturalist position.

Core Idea
Natural attributes are not inherent; they are divinely assigned.

This passage marks a critical turn in the text: the author dismisses naturalist explanations and reaffirms the Islamic theological position. Understanding the key terms 'free agent' (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) and 'specification' (takhsis) is essential for grasping the Ash'ari response to Aristotelian natural place theory. The doxology signals the religious stakes of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of refutation
Find the phrase that indicates what the author is rejecting. The passage begins by stating that falling into the darkness of doubts is due to turning away from the manifest truth. The target is the theory of natural place as an inherent cause.
The 'darkness of these doubts' refers to the previous discussion on natural place.
2
Extract the positive theological claim
Locate where the author states the true explanation: 'these bodies are only distinguished by the kinds of attributes through the specification of the free Agent.' This is the core of the passage.
The Arabic 'تخصيص الفاعل المختار' is the key phrase.
3
Examine the key terms
Break down 'al-fa'il al-mukhtar' (the free agent) and 'takhsis' (specification). The free agent acts by will, not by nature. Takhsis means the divine assignment of specific properties to each body.
Contrast with natural agent (al-fa'il al-tabi'i) who acts by necessity.
4
Analyze the doxological conclusion
The author ends with 'Blessed be Allah... exalted be He above what the wrongdoers say.' This emphasizes that the naturalist view is a form of wrongdoing (zulm) against divine omnipotence.
The doxology serves as a rhetorical closure but also a theological verdict.

واعلم ان الوقوع ظلمات هذه الشبهات للعدول عاهو الحق المبين والدين المتين وهو ان هذه الاجسام انما اختصت بالعا من الصفات بتخصيص الفاعل المختار الذي له الخلق والامر تبارك الله رب العالمين وتعالى عما يقول الظالمين
And know that falling into the darkness of these doubts is due to turning away from the manifest truth and the firm religion, and it is that these bodies are only distinguished by the kinds of attributes through the specification of the free Agent, to Whom belongs creation and command. Blessed be Allah, Lord of the worlds, and exalted be He above what the wrongdoers say.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation. The author states that the doubts about natural place arise from abandoning the clear truth and the firm religion. The true explanation is that bodies are distinguished by their attributes solely through the specification (takhsis) of the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), i.e., Allah. He concludes with a doxology, emphasizing divine transcendence above the false claims of the wrongdoers. The author does not engage in detailed logical argument here; instead, he dismisses the naturalist position as a deviation from the truth revealed in the Islamic tradition.

A) Their inherent natures and natural places

Key Takeaway
The passage affirms that all specific attributes of bodies are due to the specification of the free agent, not inherent natures.

?

Critique of Natural Place Theory
Refutation of natural place and assertion of divine choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of natural place theory and explain why he attributes body-place specification to divine choice.

Core Idea
Bodies have no inherent natural place; their location is due to Allah's choice.

Have you ever wondered why fire rises and water falls? Ancient philosophers said it's because each element has a 'natural place' it seeks by its own nature. The author here challenges that assumption head-on, showing the logical problems it leads to and offering a deeper explanation rooted in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Read the opening lines: 'that the specification of a particular body to a particular place requires a cause.' What is the theory being attacked?
Natural place theory: each element gravitates toward its own sphere.
2
Extract the refutation
If specification requires a cause, then the cause itself would require another cause, leading to infinite regress. Does the author accept this objection?
The author uses this to undermine the need for a natural place.
3
Find the alternative
Locate the phrase 'the free agent who has creation and command'. What is the author's positive position?
He asserts that all qualities are by divine choice, not nature.
4
Assess the shift
Compare this with the Aristotelian view. How does the author's position change the way we understand motion and rest?
This is a key theological move: from natural necessity to voluntary divine action.

الاقرب لإن الحركات الطبيعية تشبة عند القرب من اجازها وبالعكس وانا ان كان كل واحد منهما احدهما غالبالغ القوة والمقدار فهناك قابش يحفظ ذلك الامتزاج ولاشكر اخبار المركب إلى مكانه واما ان تركب من ثلية فان غلب احدهما بالكيفية والكمية حصل المركبة كانه ان تشاوف فان كانت من ثلثة متجاورة حصل المركبية حتى الوسط وكذا ان كانت متباينة مثل الارض والماء و الناد لتساوى الجذب من الجانبين ولاشتراك الأرض والمارغ فاذن النار
To the first method: that the specification of a particular body to a particular place requires a cause, and it is opposed by its specification due to that specification... And know that falling into the darkness of these doubts leads away from the clear truth and the firm religion, which is that these bodies are specified with qualities only by the specification of the free agent who has creation and command, blessed be Allah, Lord of the worlds, and exalted above what the wrongdoers say. ... The simple body has one nature, so it does not necessitate two contradictory places... Composite from two elements: if one is dominant, it prevents the other, etc. ... Then fire is stationary with the center?
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the theory that bodies have natural places by inherent nature. The author begins by presenting an objection to the natural place argument: if a body's specific place requires a cause, then the cause itself would also require a cause, leading to infinite regress. The author then declares that the true position is that all specific properties of bodies are due to the choice of the Creator, not to inherent natural tendencies. The passage then discusses the impossibility of one simple body having two natural places, listing three aspects: if it seeks one, it does not seek the other; if it seeks both simultaneously, that is impossible; etc. It then analyzes composite bodies made of two, three, or four elements, discussing how the dominant element determines the place of the composite. The example of fire and earth illustrates opposite tendencies. Finally, the author argues against the idea that a body can move without a specific cause, concluding that fire tends to the sphere? The passage is dense and requires careful reading of the corrected Arabic.

A) Due to their inherent nature
B) Due to the choice of the Creator

D) Due to chance

Key Takeaway
The author replaces the Aristotelian concept of natural place with the Islamic doctrine of divine choice, using a logical critique to clear the ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a modern scientific claim (e.g., 'water naturally flows downhill') and critique it using the author's method: does the 'natural' explanation require an infinite regress of causes? Then propose an alternative explanation consistent with Tawhid.

8 min
One Simple Body Cannot Have Two Natural Places
Three aspects proving a simple body cannot have two natural places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that a simple body cannot have more than one natural place.

Core Idea
A simple body has one nature, hence it cannot seek two different places.

If a simple element like earth had two natural places, it would be pulled in contradictory directions. The author systematically demolishes this possibility through three logical aspects. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The author states: 'The simple body has one nature, so it does not necessitate two contradictory places.' What does 'one nature' imply?
A simple body (like earth) is not composed of other elements; it has a single essential quality.
2
First aspect: if one place is actual
If a simple body is in one place, would it seek the other? If yes, then the current place would not be natural; if no, then the second is not natural.
This shows inconsistency.
3
Second aspect: if outside both places
If the body is outside both supposed natural places, it cannot be equally inclined to both. It would either go to both (impossible) or to one, negating the other as natural.
Fails the test of equal inclination.
4
Third aspect: parts sharing the nature
Each part of a simple body shares the same nature, so they should all seek the same place. If they seek different places, that contradicts uniformity.
This reinforces the impossibility.

12 min
Composition of Elements and Determination of Place
Analysis of composite body's natural place based on elemental composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the author determines the natural place of a composite body based on its dominant element.

Core Idea
A composite body's place is determined by its dominant element, preventing it from disintegrating.

What happens when you mix two elements with opposite tendencies? The author presents a nuanced analysis: the composite body ends up in the place of the dominant element, thanks to a 'preserver' that maintains the mixture. Let's explore his reasoning for composites of two, three, and four elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of composites
The author distinguishes composites of two, three, and four elements. Locate these in the passage.
Two elements: either equal or one dominant; three and four similarly.
2
Analyze the two-element case
If one element is dominant in power and quantity, it 'prevents' the other and the composite moves to the dominant's place. If they are equal, the author says they would separate unless there is a 'preserver' (qabish). What is that preserver?
The preserver is likely the divine power that holds the mixture together; this echoes kalam atomism.
3
Examine three and four elements
For three elements, if one is dominant, the composite goes to its place; if they are contiguous, it goes to the middle. For four equal elements, it rests in the center. Why the center?
Because equal forces from opposite directions cancel out, resulting in equilibrium.
4
Connect to the larger argument
How does this analysis support the author's rejection of natural place theory?
The determination of place by dominance shows that it is not a simple inherent nature but a result of composition and external factors.

And know that falling into the darkness of these doubts leads away from the clear truth and the firm religion, which is that these bodies are specified with qualities only by the specification of the free agent who has creation and command, blessed be Allah, Lord of the worlds, and exalted above what the wrongdoers say. ... The simple body has one nature, so it does not necessitate two contradictory places... Composite from two elements: if one is dominant, it prevents the other, etc. ... Then fire is stationary with the center?
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the theory that bodies have natural places by inherent nature. The author begins by presenting an objection to the natural place argument: if a body's specific place requires a cause, then the cause itself would also require a cause, leading to infinite regress. The author then declares that the true position is that all specific properties of bodies are due to the choice of the Creator, not to inherent natural tendencies. The passage then discusses the impossibility of one simple body having two natural places, listing three aspects: if it seeks one, it does not seek the other; if it seeks both simultaneously, that is impossible; etc. It then analyzes composite bodies made of two, three, or four elements, discussing how the dominant element determines the place of the composite. The example of fire and earth illustrates opposite tendencies. Finally, the author argues against the idea that a body can move without a specific cause, concluding that fire tends to the sphere? The passage is dense and requires careful reading of the corrected Arabic.

Why Simple Bodies Are Spherical
Author argues that every simple body has a spherical natural shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument from simplicity to sphericity and apply it to identify natural shapes in Islamic cosmology.

Core Idea
Simplicity demands uniformity—only the sphere satisfies that for natural bodies.

The author of the Mulakhkhas argues that every simple body (basit) must have a spherical natural shape. This is not an empirical claim but a logical deduction from the nature of simplicity. Understanding this argument helps us see how classical Islamic cosmologists reasoned about the heavens and the elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Find where the author states his conclusion. It is: 'Therefore the shape of simple [bodies] is the sphere.' This is the main thesis.
The conclusion is in the middle of the passage.
2
Extract the premises
The author gives two premises: (1) A simple body has one nature and one matter produces only one action. (2) Any shape other than the sphere has different actions (line, angle, point) from its sides.
Premise (2) assumes geometric analysis of shapes.
3
Follow the logical move
From (1), a simple body must have uniform action. From (2), only the sphere yields uniform action from all sides. Therefore, the simple body must be spherical.
This is a deductive argument: if a shape has multiple actions, it cannot belong to a simple body.
4
Consider the objection
The author anticipates an objection: What about the celestial spheres (aflak) or perfect spheres? They have convex and concave surfaces, which seem to be different. The text is cut off, but the objection challenges the uniformity of the sphere's action.
In classical cosmology, the celestial spheres are also simple and spherical, so the author must resolve this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تع ساكن مع الوسط في إن لكلحم بشكل طبعا وهو الكرة البسيطله طبيعة واحدة وهي لا تفعل المادة الواحدة الأفعلاً واحدا وكل شكل سوى الكرة ففيه افعال مختلفة فانه يكون جانب منه خطا وآخر ناوية وآخر نقطة. فاذن شكل البسط الكرة ولقائل أن ينقض ذلك بالمتممات فانها مع تباطتها مخلفة التخز و بالافلاك المكوكية فانها ع لبساطتها مستقرة في موضع دون موضع ولان الكرات المحطة لها سطحات محدب و مفقر وهما بعد اخلاقها
...stationary with the center? For every body has a natural shape, which is the sphere. The simple body has one nature, and one matter does not produce except one action. Every shape other than the sphere contains different actions, because one side is a line, another is an angle, another is a point. Therefore the shape of simple [bodies] is the sphere. And one may object with the perfect [spheres]? For they, despite their simplicity, different [aspects]? And with the planetary spheres, for they, despite their simplicity, are stationary in one place not another. And because the spheres have convex and concave surfaces, which are [different]?
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage defines the natural shape of simple bodies as the sphere. The author argues that a simple body has a single nature, and a single nature produces only one type of action. The sphere is the only shape that is uniform from all sides; any other shape (like a cube) has different features (edges, angles, points) that would produce different effects, which contradicts the simplicity of the body. Therefore, the natural shape of every simple body (earth, water, air, fire) is spherical. The author then anticipates an objection: what about the celestial spheres (afak) or the perfect spheres? They have convex and concave surfaces, which are different, so they might seem to have multiple actions. The text is cut off, but the argument likely continues to address this objection.

Analyzing a Conditional Argument in Celestial Physics
Author argues whether celestial sphere's nature can cause extreme heat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional premise and the objection-response structure in a natural philosophical argument about celestial heat.

Core Idea
A simple nature can produce multiple effects; the objection misapplies the principle of simplicity.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange concerning whether the celestial sphere's nature can cause extreme heat. The author defends the possibility against an objection that a simple nature cannot produce opposite effects. We will parse the conditional argument and map the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and objection
First, locate the claim being defended: that the celestial sphere's nature can produce heat, but not necessarily extreme. Then find the objection: a conditional argument that if it were hot, it would be extremely hot.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The objection's conditional: 'If the celestial spheres were hot, they would be extremely hot.' This is based on the principle that a simple body without impediment achieves perfect heat.
3
Analyze the objection's reasoning
The objection concludes that since celestial spheres are not extremely hot, they cannot be hot at all. This is a modus tollens inference. Identify the logical structure.
4
Examine the author's response
The author responds by questioning the conditional: the celestial nature might produce another quality preventing extreme heat, or extreme heat might be different in essence. The response has multiple aspects; identify the key counter-argument.

Bodies as considered in their actualization: the agent is also considered the recipient. And it is possible that the celestial atmosphere is not a recipient for that... similar with heat and [?]. If the celestial spheres were hot, they would be extremely hot, but the second is false, so the premise is similar. Explanation of the conditional: if heat along with [?] for simple bodies free from impediment, then the perfection of heat must occur, because when the agent finds the recipient free from impediment, the effect achieves perfection. Then [?] also aids the nature to produce heat, so it must increase... that necessarily the higher air would be hotter than the air adjacent to the earth; rather it would be necessary that all elements burn, because the [?] like a drop in the sea. And it would be necessary that the effect of the weak agent does not appear when compared to the strong agent. And someone may say: their proof of the conditional by the [?] of air and the [؟] of fire according to their doctrine, for each of them is simple, even though the nature of air does not [؟] the [؟] and the nature of fire does not [؟] the dryness that [؟] from air, because its nature requires moisture and heat, and moisture hinders the perfection of heat. The answer to it is from five aspects: if that is [؟], then it is possible that the simple celestial nature, with its requirement of heat, brings forth another quality preventing the perfection of [heat]... what they mentioned that the simplicity of the body prevents that the two things [i.e., opposite effects] from it, since if one of them is from the effect, then the effect is a sign of it. So if moisture were to hinder heat, then heat would also hinder it, so it would follow that air's moisture is not extreme, and that is false according to them, because the natural air of bodies according to them is moisture... air returns to thinness and subtlety. And to [؟] that thinness and subtlety are hindrances to the perfection of heat? Indeed, the nature that prevents heat is that which has the meaning of density, and it does not exist in air. You made moisture [؟] from extreme heat and did not make it prevent the original production. So extreme heat must be different in essence from original heat. And if that is so, then it is possible that the celestial nature requires one of the two types, which is the original, not the second which is perfection. For if there were opposition between moisture and dryness, then the single nature would do two opposite acts, which is impossible. For if it is said: [؟] moisture opposes the perfection of heat, rather we say: the nature makes the matter limited in preparedness. We say: the united nature of air either has in its power the requirement of extreme heat or not. If the first, then it necessitates moisture, and the implication is realized. If the second, then [؟] from the existence of the united nature results extreme heat, which is what is sought. And it also needs [؟] about the [؟] of fire, that the extremity of its density prevents the perfection of [؟], and it is weak for the reasons known. And what we intend here is that they made the extremity of air's moisture a preventer from the perfection of its heat. And if that is so, then it would be necessary that the extremity of heat is opposite to moisture, because opposition is from both sides. That requires that the extremity of heat be opposite to the extremity of moisture, unless it is said that the extremity of heat is opposite to the extremity of moisture and [؟] and opposite to moderation. But if that is so, then it is also not permissible that the celestial nature be opposite to the extremity of heat and dryness, opposite to moderate heat, and requiring it— and that is what is sought. We grant the [؟] from the [؟], but what is the proof for the conditional? For extreme heat may differ in kind from moderate heat, and the signs from being such that in itself it has a requirement of a quiddity, that it may have another quiddity different in kind, and it does not follow from the nature producing moderate moisture that it would prevent extreme heat. Indeed, the celestial nature is not beyond producing moderate heat, but motion is also in that?
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a critical dialectical argument concerning whether the celestial sphere's nature can produce extreme heat. The author presents an objection to a previous claim, then gives a detailed response. The discussion begins with a general principle: in the actualization of bodies, the agent and the recipient are both considered. It is possible that the celestial atmosphere (or sphere) is not a recipient for heat. The objection argues that if celestial spheres were hot, they would be extremely hot, which is false, so they are not hot. The author then explains a conditional: if heat were to affect simple bodies free from impediments, perfect heat would result, because an agent finding a receptive material without impediment achieves full effect. This leads to a consequence: the upper air would be hotter than the lower, and the elements would burn. Moreover, the weak agent's effect would not appear in the presence of a strong one. Someone then points out that according to their own doctrine, air and fire are simple, yet air does not become extremely hot, and fire does not lose its dryness. The answer has five aspects. The first is that the simple celestial nature, despite requiring heat, might produce another quality preventing perfect heat. They mention that simplicity does not prevent two opposite effects; if one effect is from a cause, that cause is indicated by it. If moisture hindered heat, then heat would also hinder moisture, so air's moisture would not be extreme—but they claim air is naturally moist. Thinness and subtlety are not preventing perfect heat; rather, density prevents, and density is absent in air. They made moisture a hindrance to extreme heat but not to original heat production. Hence extreme heat must be different in essence from original heat. If so, the celestial nature can require one type (original) but not the other (perfection). If moisture and dryness were opposed, one nature cannot produce both opposites. If it is said that nature limits matter's preparedness, the author replies: the unified nature of air either has the power to require extreme heat or not. If it does, it necessitates moisture (so the implication stands). If it does not, then from the existence of that nature extreme heat results—which is the required conclusion. Next, regarding fire, the claim that its density prevents perfection is weak. The main point: they made extreme moisture of air prevent perfect heat, so extreme heat must oppose moisture. This implies that extreme heat opposes extreme moisture and moderate heat. Thus the celestial nature would oppose extreme heat and dryness and require moderate heat—which is what was to be shown. The author grants the supposition but asks for proof of the conditional: extreme heat may differ in kind from moderate heat, and no necessity that a nature producing moderate moisture prevents extreme heat. The celestial nature can produce moderate heat, and motion also plays a role.

رطوبة
حرارة
طبيعة فلكية

Key Takeaway
The conditional argument about celestial heat is rebutted by showing that a simple nature can produce multiple effects and that extreme heat may differ in essence from moderate heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a conditional argument against the claim that the celestial sphere causes only moderate heat, modeling it on the objector's reasoning; then formulate a rebuttal using the author's strategy of distinguishing types of heat.

The Dilemma of Independent Causes
Author refutes the claim that celestial motion generates heat and defends celestial matter's unique nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's reductio argument against celestial motion causing heat and apply the principle that a single effect cannot have two independent causes.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot produce one effect – a key principle in Kalam causality.

In this passage, al-Razi attacks the claim that celestial motion generates heat. He constructs a dilemma: if both celestial nature and motion independently necessitate heat, then either both are effective together (impossible) or neither is effective (also impossible). This forces the conclusion that the opponent's position is false. This lesson dissects that logical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that celestial motion is a cause of heat. Find where in the Arabic this claim is implied.
Look for phrases like 'الحركة الفلكية توجب السخونة'.
2
Extract the two potential causes
Al-Razi identifies two candidates: the celestial nature (الطبيعة الفلكية) and the celestial motion (الحركة). Note how he frames them as independent.
3
Apply the principle of causal independence
The principle: a single effect cannot have two independent causes. Al-Razi uses this to test the scenario where both causes would be effective together.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic theology used to prove the oneness of God.
4
Construct the dilemma
If both nature and motion are effective together, it's impossible. If neither is effective, the effect is uncaused (also impossible). Thus the opponent's assumption that motion is a cause must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude that celestial matter is receptive to heat
Al-Razi concludes that since the opponent's argument leads to impossibility, the opposite is true: celestial matter can accept all types of heat.
The conclusion is stated: 'وثبت أن المادة الفلكية قابلة لجميع أنواع السخونة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السخونة أيضًا محنة لكل مادة بلاقيها، فإذا وجدت تجر المادة الخارجة عنها فالمادة الملاقية لها أولى. وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يحدث عن الحركة ويعني تلك السخونة، لا من حيث هي زائدة بل من حيث هي محفوفة. فإذا تلك الزيادة محفوفة لا إلى آخر، كأن معنى آخر لو انضاف إليه لأن يفيد سخونة أخرى. لانا نقول: المعلول كما لعترة بحة قد حصول العلة الفاعلة فيه، أيضًا حصول العلة القابلة. فلم لا يبطلون أن يقال: المادة الفلكية لكن قوتها لا تقبل ذلك النوع من السخونة، كما أنه ليس لقوات المذهبة إلا قبول القدر الخاص من المقدار؟ وإذا كان ذلك محتملاً سقط قولكم كالقول بأنه ليس في طباع تلك المادة إلا قبول ذلك النوع المعتدل من الحرارة يفضي إلى المحال. لأن الطبيعة الفلكية مستقلة باقتضاء ذلك القدر من السخونة، وأحركة التي للفلك متنقلة باقتضائه، فقد اجتمع على الأثر الواحد علمان منتقلتان، فليس إحداهما بالاقتضاء أولى من الأخرى. فإما أن يكونا مؤثرين فيه معًا، وهو محال استحالة استناد المعلول الشخصي إلى علتين مستقلين، أو لا إلى واحدة منهما، فإما أن تحصل من غير سبب وهو محال، أو كالعدم أصلًا وهو محال أيضًا، لأنه يلزم تعدد المعلول عند قوة عليه، ولأنه يحصل عطل قولنا من كون الفلك غير محتمل، كما ذكر عنه يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة فيكون باطلاً. وثبت أن المادة الفلكية قابلة لجميع أنواع السخونة. لانا نقول: للمبطلون أن يقال: الحركة الفلكية غير موثرة في السخونة، ولم يبطل ذلك بما شاهد من كون الحركة محرقة، لأن الحركة الفلكية مخالفة بالنوع للحركات التي تلها، ولا يلزم من كون نوع من جنس موجبًا لشيء أن يكون جميع أنواع ذلك الجنس كذلك. سلمنا أن الحركة محرقة، فما لنا ساوية للطبيعة الفلكية في الاستقلال بإفادة ذلك القدر من السخونة؟ لكن لم المبطلون أن يقولوا: الطبيعة أولى بإفادته من الحركة، لكون الطبيعة متقدمة على الحركة تقدمًا بالطبع. فسلم أن الحركة أولى، لكن لم لا يبطلون أن يقال: الطبيعة الفلكية لا تقتضي السخونة أصلًا، لكن الحركة السريعة التي له تقتضي تلك السخونة؟ وعلى هذا الاجتماع لا يلزم علتين على المعلول الواحد. قوله: السخونة الصادرة عن تلك القوة يجب أن تفعل سخونة أخرى، قلنا: هذا بعيد جدًا، ولا الزمان تصدر عن كل متحركة سخونة أخرى لا إلى غاية، فيكون في المحل سخونات غير متناهية. قوله: تلك السخونة تخل تحت ما يلاقي محلها، فذلك تحن نعمل أولى، ضعيف، احتمال أن يكون شرط إفادتها أن تكون الأثر في محل آخر. ثم نؤكد هذا الاحتمال: أنه لو أفادت سخونة أخرى في محلها لزم اجتماع السخونتين على الشطبة، لكن مفضوخكم: اما تحقق لو كانت سخونة الفلك من طبيعته، فإنا لو حصلت من الفاعل المختار، فذلك غير مانع من سخونة ملازم سكون، كما ذكرتم مخالف، لكنه المنع من كون الشمس ذاتها حارة، لكنها لغاية بعدها عنا لا يصل إلينا التأثير العظيم من قادتها. وكذا القول في سائر الكواكب: النار شفافة والكواكب ليست شفافة، فالكواكب لبن مرآتا؟ وتقابل أن قوات الاشتراك في اللزوم لا يقتضي الاشتراك في تمام الماهية، فلم لا يبطلون أن تكون الكواكب والنار مشتركين في غاية السخونة، لكن الكواكب مخالفة في ماهياتها لماهية النار، فلأجل ذلك كانت النار التي عندنا شفافة وتلك الكواكب غير شفافة. و أن الملك ليس باردًا، لو كان باردًا إلى غاية البرودة فكانت تستولي البرودة على المجموع من العناصر، فما كانت تكون الحيوانات. لا يقال: لم لا يمكن أن تنكسر البرودة بمعاودة النار؟ لأنا نقول: لو كان كذلك لكانت المادة الفلكية قابلة للسخونة، ولو كان كذلك لوجب أن يصير غاية السخونة بسبب الحركة التي لها. والكلام هنا كالإعلام بالإمارات في أن الفلك
Heat is also a trial for every matter that meets it. If it is found to drag the matter out from it, then the meeting matter is more fitting. And if so, then it is necessary that motion produces heat, not insofar as it is excessive but insofar as it is surrounded. For that excess is surrounded not to another, as if another meaning if added to it would produce another heat. For we say: the effect as if it has the cause in it, and also has the receptive cause. Why do they not invalidate the claim: celestial matter, but its power does not accept that type of heat, just as the powers of the sphere only accept a specific quantity? If that is possible, your statement falls, like the claim that it is not in the nature of that matter except to accept that moderate type of heat, which leads to impossibility. For the celestial nature is independent in necessitating that amount of heat, and the motion of the sphere is moving in necessitating it, so two moving causes have come together on one effect, but one of them by necessitation is not prior to the other. Either they are both effective together – which is impossible because attribution of a singular effect to two independent causes is impossible – or not to one of them, so either it comes from no cause – which is impossible – or it does not exist at all – which is also impossible, for it would necessitate multiplicity of the effect and cause our statement of celestial matter being improbable. Thus their statement leads to false divisions and is false. And it is established that celestial matter is receptive to all types of heat. For we say: the refuters can say that celestial motion does not affect heat, and that is not invalidated by what we observe of motion causing heat, because celestial motion is different in species from the motions below it, and it does not follow from one species of a genus causing something that all species of that genus are thus. Granted that motion causes heat, then we are equal to celestial nature in independence in effecting that amount of heat? But why do the refuters not say: nature is more fitting than motion because nature precedes motion naturally. Granted that motion is more fitting, but why do they not invalidate the claim that celestial nature does not necessitate heat at all, but the fast motion of it necessitates that heat? In this way, two causes do not meet on one effect. His statement: the heat emanating from that power must produce another heat. We say: this is very far-fetched, and time, from every moving thing, another heat would be produced ad infinitum, leading to infinite heats in one location. His statement: that heat is included under what meets its location, so we should work accordingly, is weak. There is a possibility that the condition of its effect is that the effect be in another location. Then we emphasize this possibility: if it caused another heat in its location, the two heats would combine on one spot, but your refutation: if the heat of the sphere were from its nature, then if it came from a free agent, that does not prevent heat accompanying rest, as you mentioned. But it prevents the sun itself from being hot, but due to its distance from us, the great effect of its powers does not reach us. Similarly for other stars: fire is transparent, stars are not transparent, so stars are like mirrors. And it is said that the commonality in necessity does not require commonality in essence, so why do they not refute that stars and fire share in extreme heat, but stars differ in essence from the essence of fire, so for that reason the fire with us is transparent and those stars are not transparent. And that the sphere is not cold; if it were extremely cold, then cold would overpower the elements, and animals would not exist. It cannot be said: why cannot cold be broken by the onset of fire? For we say: if it were so, then celestial matter would be receptive to heat, and if so, then it would become extremely hot due to its motion. And the discussion here is like an indication of the signs concerning the sphere.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of an opponent's claim that celestial motion causes heat. The author begins by stating that heat affects any matter it meets, and if it drags matter out (presumably from a state), then the matter meeting it is more likely to be affected. He argues that if so, motion should produce heat, but not because heat is excessive but because it is surrounded by conditions. He then questions why the opponent does not consider that celestial matter might not accept that type of heat, just as the powers of the sphere only accept a specific quantity. If that possibility stands, the opponent's claim falls. The author then presents a dilemma: if both celestial nature and motion independently require heat, then either both are effective together (impossible for a single effect to have two independent causes), or neither is effective (leading to uncaused effects or non-existence), both impossible. Therefore, celestial matter must be receptive to all types of heat. He then rejects the absolute claim that motion is ineffective in heat, noting that celestial motion differs in species from sublunary motions, so analogy fails. He also argues that granting motion causes heat, why should it be equal to celestial nature? Possibly nature is prior. He then counters the objection that the heat from the power would produce infinite heats, calling it far-fetched. He also addresses the possibility of the effect being in another location. Finally, he discusses the transparence of stars and the nature of the sphere's temperature, concluding that stars are not transparent like fire despite sharing heat, because their essences differ. The passage uses logical arguments with categorical syllogisms and dialectical objections.

الحركة
الفلك
العلة المستقلة
محال

Checkpoint
What logical principle does al-Razi use to refute the claim that celestial motion causes heat?
A) Every effect must have a cause.
B) A single effect cannot have two independent causes.
C) Motion always produces heat.
D) Celestial matter is receptive to heat.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the causal claim by showing that its assumptions violate the logical principle of causal independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Using the principle that a single effect cannot have two independent causes, construct a reductio argument against the claim that both the sun and the moon independently cause the tides (assume they do). Show that this leads to an impossibility.
?
2) In the context of tawhid, explain why the existence of two independent gods would lead to the same dilemma al-Razi presents. Write a brief syllogism.

advanced
12 min
Defending Celestial Matter's Unique Nature
Author refutes the claim that celestial motion generates heat and defends celestial matter's unique nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate al-Razi's dialectical arguments against objections based on species difference, priority of cause, and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Analogies from sublunary motion fail because celestial motion is a different species.

After establishing his main argument, al-Razi addresses several objections an opponent might raise. He argues that celestial motion differs in species from sublunary motion, so analogy fails. He also counters that nature is prior to motion and rejects the infinite heat regress. This lesson examines these rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical objection
The opponent might argue: since sublunary motion causes heat, celestial motion should too. Find where al-Razi addresses this.
Look for the phrase 'الحركة الفلكية مخالفة بالنوع للحركات التي تلها'.
2
Understand the species distinction
Al-Razi states that celestial motion is different in species from motions below. Therefore, the property of one species does not transfer to another.
This is a key principle: a genus includes species with different properties.
3
Examine the priority argument
Al-Razi considers the objection that nature is prior to motion and thus more deserving to cause heat. He even grants that motion might be more fitting, but then shifts to another objection.
He uses a concessive strategy to test the opponent's position.
4
Analyze the infinite heat regress
The opponent claims that heat from a power would produce another heat ad infinitum. Al-Razi calls this far-fetched and offers the alternative that the effect might be in a different location.
This avoids the infinite regress by denying the premise.
5
Reflect on the transparency difference
Al-Razi notes that stars are not transparent like fire, yet both are extremely hot. This supports that celestial matter differs in essence.
This counters the objection that celestial matter must be like fire.

Why does al-Razi reject the analogy from sublunary motion to celestial motion?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) In a debate about whether angels can be seen, a participant argues 'humans cannot be seen as they are spirits, so angels (also spirits) cannot be seen.' Identify the analogical argument and critique it using al-Razi's species distinction principle.

Analyzing a Dialectical Objection on Planetary Motion
Dialectical exchange on planetary motion via a hypothetical ring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection and refutation in a dialectical exchange about celestial motion and evaluate the strength of the refutation.

Core Idea
A transparent ring model fails to disprove sphere motion because it is ad hoc and unobserved.

In Islamic astronomy and kalam, dialectical reasoning is used to defend or challenge models of celestial motion. Here, an objector proposes a clever alternative: suppose a dense ring attaches to the sphere and moves with the planet, while the sphere stays still. The author refutes this by pointing out that the ring is unobserved and the assumption does not actually undermine the original argument. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of this objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Claim
Read the objection carefully. The objector is challenging the idea that planets move by the motion of their spheres. What alternative mechanism do they propose? Look for the key term 'حلقة' (ring).
2
Extract the Objection's Reasoning
The objection assumes a dense ring attached to the sphere, equal in diameter to the planet. When the planet moves, the ring moves with it, while the sphere remains stationary. The objector claims this is not observed because the ring is transparent.
Notice the objector uses a hypothetical scenario to try to show that planet motion does not necessitate sphere motion.
3
Analyze the Author's Refutation
The author responds by stating that even under this assumption, the planet's independent motion does not necessitate sphere motion. The refutation implies the objection is irrelevant: the original argument is not disproven by an ad hoc, unobserved entity.
The author does not directly reject the existence of the ring but questions its necessity and observability.
4
Evaluate the Dialectical Outcome
In dialectical terms, the objector has not successfully refuted the original claim because the proposed model is speculative and lacks evidence. The author's response reinforces the original position by showing the objection fails to change the conclusion.
This exchange demonstrates how a valid objection must be based on observed phenomena, not mere possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمت ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال أنه ينفصل عن بعض الفلك جسم شديد معلقة ويكون قطر ذلك مساويًا لقطر الكواكب، فإذا تحرك الكوكب اعتمدت تلك الحلقة وتتحول الحلقة والكوكب معًا، ويكون الفلك واقفًا من غير أن يعرض له الحاق إصلاثم؟ إنا لا نشاهد حركة تلك الحلقة لكونها شفافة مشاكلة لجرم التمار، ومع هذا التقدير لا يلزم من استقلال الكوكب بالحركة الحاق الفلك.
You conceded that, but why is it not permissible to say that from some sphere separates a suspended dense body, its diameter equal to the diameter of the planets, so when the planet moves, that ring is relied upon and the ring and the planet move together, while the sphere remains stationary without being affected? Then we do not observe the motion of that ring because it is transparent and similar to the body of the [؟]. And with this assumption, the independence of the planet in motion does not necessitate the motion of the sphere.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection followed by an implicit refutation. The objector proposes an alternative to the view that planets move by the motion of their spheres. They suggest that a dense ring (حلقة) could be attached to the sphere, with a diameter equal to the planet's. When the planet moves, the ring moves with it, while the sphere remains stationary. The objector then notes that we do not observe this ring's motion because it is transparent and similar in substance to the celestial body. The author counters that even under this assumption, the planet's independent motion would not necessitate the sphere's motion—meaning the objection fails because it does not undermine the original argument that the sphere moves. The author likely implies that such a ring is ad hoc and unnecessary, especially since the observed motions are more simply explained by the sphere's motion. The lesson guards against overcomplicating explanatory models without empirical evidence.

Refuting Theories via Logical Inconsistency
Author critiques two theories that explain Earth's stability via celestial attraction or repulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaws in the attraction and push theories for Earth's stationarity as presented by the author and apply the same method of critique to other claims.

Core Idea
The easiest way to refute a theory is to show it leads to absurd consequences.

In this passage, the Fakhr al-Din al-Razi critiques two theories proposed by philosophers to explain why the Earth remains stationary despite being spherical. He does not attack the spherical Earth itself, but rather the mechanisms offered—attraction and repulsion by the celestial sphere. By drawing out the logical and empirical consequences of each theory, he shows they are untenable. This approach is a powerful tool for evaluating any claim in the Islamic sciences, whether in kalam, fiqh, or usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Theory
Find the core claim of the theory being critiqued. Here, the first theory says the celestial sphere attracts the Earth from all sides, keeping it balanced at the center.
2
State the Consequence
Work out what logically follows from the theory. The author argues that if the sphere attracts, the smaller and closer an object is, the faster it would be drawn—so Earth would not be stable.
3
Classify the Objection
Determine whether the objection is empirical (contradicts observation) or logical (leads to contradiction). The author's objections to the push theory include both: we would feel a constant push like wind (empirical) and objects would fall westward (logical).
4
Evaluate the Objection
Consider whether the objection is sound given the assumptions. The author assumes that if the sphere pushes equally from all sides, then there should be a perceptible effect, which we do not experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage critiques two theories proposed by philosophers to explain why the Earth remains stationary despite being spherical. The first theory claims the celestial sphere attracts the Earth from all sides, keeping it balanced at the center. The author refutes this, arguing that the closer and smaller the body, the faster it would be drawn—hence Earth would not be stable. The second theory claims the sphere pushes the Earth from all sides. The author gives multiple objections: we would feel a constant push like strong winds; we would tend to fall westward (due to the direction of push); and the motion of a falling object would be accelerated near the sphere, which contradicts observation. These refutations demonstrate logical inconsistencies in the physical models.

A theory can be refuted by showing it leads to absurd empirical or logical consequences, even without direct empirical evidence.

Refuting the Half-Up Half-Down Earth Theory
Author refutes a theory about Earth's halves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two arguments the author uses to refute the theory of Earth's halves moving opposite each other and explain why the theory contradicts the simplicity and uniform gravitational orientation of the Earth.

Core Idea
A simple body cannot have parts with opposite natural motions.

In this passage, the author takes on a specific cosmological claim: that Earth is split into two halves, one ascending and one descending, and they balance in the middle. This might sound plausible if you think of balancing forces, but the author shows it is false on two grounds. We'll parse the Arabic carefully and see how logical reasoning from physical principles dismantles the theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the part of the Arabic that states the theory: 'النصف الأسفل من الأرض صاعد والنصف الآخر هابط'. Note that 'زعم' (claimed) signals this is someone else's view.
The author first states the opponent's position before refuting it.
2
Extract the first argument: simplicity of Earth
The author says 'وهذا باطل لأنها بسيطة'. 'بسيطة' means simple, not composite. A simple body cannot have parts with contrary natural motions. This is a direct contradiction: the theory implies two opposite tendencies in one simple substance.
The term 'بسيطة' is a key concept in natural philosophy.
3
Extract the second argument: uniform feet alignment
The author gives an example: people standing on the eastern and western edges of the inhabited world have their feet 'محاذية' (aligned). That is, both feet point downward toward the Earth's center, not in opposite directions. This shows that the direction of 'down' is uniform globally, contradicting any half-up half-down notion.
The Arabic is broken but the sense is clear from context.
4
Assess the strength of each argument
The first argument is a priori: from the definition of a simple body. The second is empirical: from observation of consistent gravitational orientation. Both are independent and jointly refute the theory.
Consider whether the theory could be salvaged by distinguishing different senses of 'up' and 'down'.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific theory about Earth's composition: that its lower half naturally ascends and upper half descends, and they balance at the center. The author gives two reasons for its falsehood. First, Earth is a simple body (basīṭa), not composed of two parts with opposite tendencies. Second, if this were true, people standing on opposite sides of the Earth (east and west) would have their feet pointing in opposite directions relative to the center, but in reality, they are oriented similarly—both feet point downward toward the Earth's center. This indicates a uniform gravitational direction, contradicting the idea of halves moving oppositely. The last line is cut off but illustrates the point about uniform nature.

Debate on whether water or earth has greater coldness
Author presents opposing views on water and earth coldness intensity with objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the opposing positions and the logical objection in a dialectical argument about natural qualities, and apply the principle that equal effects can have diverse causes.

Core Idea
An observed effect does not prove a single cause — equal results can arise from different causes.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas presents a dialectical exchange concerning the relative coldness of water and earth. You will learn to dissect the arguments and counterarguments, and recognize the logical principle that correlation does not entail causation. Understanding this principle is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, especially when determining effective causes in legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and its basis
The passage begins with an empirical observation: dates absorb cold from water more than from earth, leading to the claim that water's coldness is stronger. Identify this claim and its supporting evidence.
The initial claim relies on sensory experience (dates absorbing cold).
2
Identify the counterclaim and its basis
A later scholar argues that earth is colder because it is denser, and density is an effect of coldness. Locate this counterclaim and the reasoning behind it.
The counterclaim uses a theoretical principle linking density to coldness.
3
Find the objection to the counterclaim
The objection denies that density necessarily causes greater coldness. It states that two equally cold substances can have different causes, so density alone does not prove earth is colder. Pinpoint this objection.
This is the logical core of the passage: equal effects can have diverse causes.
4
Assess the logical structure
Evaluate the strength of the objection. It correctly identifies that correlation (density and coldness) does not imply causation, and that a single effect can have multiple causes. Consider whether the initial claim remains unchallenged.
The objection does not refute the initial claim, only the counterclaim's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange regarding the relative coldness of water and earth. The initial claim states that water is colder than earth, based on empirical observation: dates (possibly a test substance) absorb coldness from water more than from earth. A later scholar counters that earth is actually colder because its higher density (which is an effect of coldness) makes it less receptive to heat. The objection then refutes the link between density and coldness, arguing that two equally cold substances can have different causes for their coldness, so density does not necessarily imply greater coldness. This illustrates the logical principle that correlation does not entail causation, and equal effects can have diverse causes.

Distinguishing Sensory Perceptions: Water's Color and Taste
Author questions the existence of color and taste in water using reflection and sensory arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological uncertainty about sensory definitions of water's properties and apply the principle of tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) when evidence is insufficient.

Core Idea
Certainty about what we sense requires distinguishing between different perceptual faculties.

This passage from the Mulakhkhas al-Razi examines whether water has color and taste. The author uses optical reasoning to argue that water does have a color, but when it comes to taste, he suspends judgment because we cannot definitively attribute the sensation to the sense of taste or touch. This illustrates a fundamental logical principle: before defining a property, we must establish which sense is perceiving it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two properties examined
The passage separately discusses color and taste of water. Note the author's approach to each: for color he gives an argument by reflection; for taste he suspends judgment.
2
Analyze the argument for color
The author uses reflection: water reflects light like a crystal, implying it has some optical property (color or transparency). What is the underlying assumption?
The corrupted text mentions reflection from a colored substance and a flask; the inference is that if water had no color, it would not reflect.
3
Examine the suspension for taste
The author says 'it is a place of suspension' because we do not know if the sensation from drinking mango-flavored water is due to touch or taste. Why is this a logical problem?
If a property can be perceived by two senses, we lack a clear definition unless we specify which sense is authoritative.
4
Generalize the epistemological principle
Before defining something as having a property, we must ensure we are using the correct sense and have clarity on the definition of the sense itself. Otherwise, the definition is uncertain.
This relates to the concept of tawaqquf in Islamic logic: suspending judgment when evidence does not permit certainty.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage examines the properties of water: color and taste. The author begins with a question: does water have a color? This shows the logical need to distinguish between different faculties of perception.

Deducing Fire's Shape from an Axiom
Author defends spherical fire from axiom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the axiomatic principle used to deduce the spherical shape of fire.

Core Idea
Fire's shape follows from the axiom that all simple elements are spherical.

Despite the garbled text, we can extract a clear logical move: a universal principle about simple bodies is applied to fire. Al-Razi reports the popular view that fire is spherical, and the commentary frames it as an expansion of the axiom. This lesson will help you see how axioms function in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about fire
The passage states that the famous view is that fire has the shape of a sphere. Find the phrase 'شكلها المشهور أنه الكرة'.
This is the conclusion to be justified.
2
Find the justifying principle
The text says 'لأن شكل البسيط الكرة' – the shape of the simple is the sphere. This is the universal axiom.
Here 'simple' refers to elementary bodies like fire, air, water, earth.
3
Check the logical form
The argument is a straightforward deduction: All simple bodies are spherical (axiom). Fire is a simple body (implied). Therefore, fire is spherical.
The minor premise (fire is simple) is taken from the classification of elements.
4
Consider the objection
The passage then mentions a counterargument about slow motion near the pole, which might challenge the application of the axiom. Even with corruption, the move shows the author is aware of empirical challenges.
This step shows that axioms are not always blindly applied; observations can complicate deductions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage discusses the shape of fire, which is traditionally considered spherical based on the principle that simple elements have spherical shapes. Some argued that fire moves in accordance with the celestial sphere, thus it is spherical. The text then involves a discussion of how air and water interact with fire, but the corruption obscures the details. This reflects the logical use of axiomatic principles (simple elements are spherical) and empirical observations about motion to derive conclusions about natural forms.

Do Elements Retain Their Natures in Mixtures?
Author debates whether elemental natures persist in mixtures, presenting two schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main positions on elemental natures in mixtures and reconstruct the author's argument for the persistence of natures.

Core Idea
Elemental natures persist in mixtures; only qualities change.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a dialectical exchange about what happens to the fundamental elements when they combine to form a compound. Do they retain their identities, or are they completely transformed? This question is not just physical: it shapes how Islamic philosophers understand change, causation, and the structure of the natural world. By carefully dissecting the positions, proofs, and objections, you will learn to analyze a philosophical debate with the rigour of the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing doctrines
Find in the passage the Peripatetic view (mashshāʾīn) and the view of some later philosophers. What does each claim about the fate of elemental natures?
The Peripatetics say natures persist; later philosophers say they are nullified.
2
Locate the author's preferred position
The author favors the 'madhhab al-maththāliyyīn'. Determine what this doctrine holds regarding natures in mixtures.
The author argues that natures persist, but only qualities change.
3
Extract the author's two proofs
Read the two reasons the author gives for the truth of his preferred doctrine. The first involves placing the compound in an oven; the second involves bodies of similar nature.
The proofs are: 1) heating a compound yields a homogeneous substance; 2) like-natured bodies produce only one effect from a single agent.
4
Analyze the objection and response
Identify the objection raised by the opponents (e.g., 'pure fire would not remain fiery when mixed with contraries') and the author's reply. How does the author defend his position?
The author argues that the same problem applies to the opponent's view, and that change only occurs in composite state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا بحث آخر وهو أن تلك العناصر هل يبقى على طبائعها حال كونها جزءا من المركب أم لا؟ مذهب المشائين على بقاء تلك الطبائع والمفاعل إنما يحصل في الكيفيات. ومن المتأخرين من زعم أن تلك الطبائع تبطل وتحصل للمركب طبيعة واحدة. وهذا البحث متفرع على أن طبائع هذه العناصر ليست نفس هذه الكيفيات. والحق مذهب المثاليين لوجهين: ما إذا وضعنا المركب في الفرن والانيق حصل لنا منه جوهر مادي واحد هواريكل [؟] ولولا اختلاف الجوهر المركب لكان كذلك. وأن الأجسام المتشابهة الطباع لا يفعل الفاعل الواحد منها إلا فعلا واحدا. فالكأس لا بد من بقائه حال حصول الانكسار. فلك الطبائع باقية حال انكسار تلك الكيفيات. واختار الأخرون: ابقاء الناري مع بقاء طبيعة النارية. لو لم يبق لانقلبت الصورة الحاصلة عارضا حتى تنتھي حرارته إلى ذلك الحد الذي حصل له عند كونه جزءا من المركب. وجوابهم: أن النار الصرفة لو بقيت على طبيعتها النارية عند إخلاطها بالأضداد لاتصلحت الشعل. والجواب عن الأول: أنه يلزم عليكم أيضا: أن ذلك العرض الحاصل للناري حين أخلط بغير من العناصر عرض له ما أخرجه عن الصورة النارية وكسبه الصورة اللحية في حكم أن تجوزوا عروض ذلك العارض من النار بالبسيطة. فلا يلزم عندنا أنه من المحتمل أن لا يعقب ذلك العارض للنار إلا عند التركيب. وهو جوابنا. ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه إنما يعتبر في تكون المركبات النسبية التي بين بساطتها، فلذلك يعتبر كذلك أيضا لكل واحد منها. وقد خصص الكلام.
And here is another investigation: whether those elements remain in their natures when they are part of a compound or not? The doctrine of the Peripatetics is the preservation of those natures, and the [effect] happens only in the qualities. Some later [philosophers] claimed that those natures are nullified and a single nature occurs for the compound. This investigation depends on whether the natures of these elements are not themselves these qualities. The truth is the doctrine of the [exemplars?] for two reasons: firstly, if we place the compound in the oven and the anvil, we obtain from it a single material substance, [homogeneous?] [؟]; and if the compound substance were not different, it would be so. And [secondly], bodies of similar nature do not receive from a single agent except a single effect. Thus the cup must remain at the moment of breakage. Therefore the natures remain at the moment of breakage of those qualities. And the others chose: the persistence of the fiery [nature] with the persistence of the fiery nature. If it did not persist, the generated form would transform into an accident until its heat reaches that limit which it obtained when it was part of the compound. Their answer: pure fire, if it remained on its fiery nature when mixed with contraries, the flame would not subsist. The answer to the first: it is also incumbent upon you: that that accident occurring to the fiery [element] when it was mixed with another element, there occurred to it something that removed it from the fiery form and it acquired the igneous form in the judgment that you permit the occurrence of that accident from simple fire. So it does not necessarily follow for us that it is possible that that accident does not follow fire except in composition. And that is our answer. From this it appears that only the relative compositions between their simplicities are considered in the formation of compounds, so for that reason also each of them is considered likewise. And the speech has been specified.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the persistence of elemental natures in mixtures. The author presents two competing doctrines: the Peripatetic view that elements retain their natures (with change only in qualities) and the view of some later philosophers that elements transform entirely into a new single nature. The author favors the so-called 'madhhab al-maththāliyyīn' (perhaps meaning the exemplars or a particular school) and provides two proofs. First, if a compound is subjected to heat in an oven, it yields a homogeneous substance, which would not happen if the elemental natures were different. Second, like-natured bodies produce only one effect from a single agent, so if natures were lost, the reaction would not occur. The author then deals with objections: an opponent argues that pure fire would not remain fiery when mixed with contraries, to which the author replies that the same problem afflicts the opponent's view, and that the change only occurs in the composite state. This clarifies that the concept of mixture considers the relative proportions of the simple natures. The discussion is rooted in Aristotelian natural philosophy as interpreted by Islamic philosophers, using examples from physical change (like heating metals) rather than from logic proper.

Key Terms
عناصر
مزاج
طبائع
مشائين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Elements are annihilated and a new single nature emerges.
B) Elements retain their natures; change occurs only in qualities.
C) Elements become non-existent and only qualities remain.
D) Elements transform completely into the dominant element.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debates, the persistence or annihilation of elemental natures affects how we understand causation and change in the physical world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
