Classification and Causes of Atmospheric Phenomena
Classification and causal explanation of atmospheric phenomena

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the six atmospheric phenomena mentioned and explain the role of rising vapor and the cold layer in their formation.

Core Idea
Rising vapor meeting cold creates atmospheric phenomena; freezing turns dew into frost.

In this passage, the author classifies atmospheric phenomena into those above and below the earth, focusing on the above-ground group: vapor, rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost. He then explains their common cause: ascending vapor that reaches the cold layer of the atmosphere. The intensity of cold determines the specific outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
The passage first divides phenomena into two categories: above earth and below. Focus on the first category, which is the subject of this lesson.
2
List the phenomena
Extract the six phenomena named: بخار (vapor), مطر (rain), ثلج (snow), برد (hail), طل (dew), صقيع (frost).
Some of these are water states; others are frozen or condensed forms.
3
Trace the causal chain
The primary cause is rising vapor (بخار). It ascends and encounters the cold layer (طبقة باردة). The strength of the cold decides the specific phenomenon.
This is a material cause (vapor) and an efficient cause (cold).
4
Distinguish dew and frost
If the vapor does not freeze upon meeting the cold layer, it becomes dew (طل); if it freezes, it becomes frost (صقيع). This distinction hinges on whether the cold is intense enough to cause freezing.
Dew and frost are both formed from vapor, but the freezing point differentiates them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي اما ان تحدث فوق الارض او تحتها. القسم الاول فيما يتكون في الجو وهو بخار ومطر وثلج وبرد وطل وصقيع. السبب الاكثرى لها قيام البخار الصاعد. ثم اذا كان قليلا و كان في الهواء از تخلله المحدث منه التهاب كان كان كثيرا وان قل لم يوجد الحلك فانا ان تلخ في صعوده إلى الطبعة البارحة من القرية اولا بلغ فان بلغ فانا ان يكون البرد هنال قويا ولا يكون فان لم يتوالي و تحائف، ذلك البخار بذلك القدر من البرد فان لم ينحمد كان طلا فان النجد كان مقيدا مكنسبة الصقيع.
They are either generated above the earth or below it. The first division is what forms in the atmosphere, and it is vapor, rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost. The most common cause of them is the rising vapor. Then if it is little and in the air, [something]... then if it rises to the cold layer... if it reaches it, then either the cold there is strong or not. If not, and that vapor is not [something] by that amount of cold, then if it does not freeze it becomes dew, and if it freezes it becomes frost.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and causal explanation of atmospheric phenomena. The author begins by dividing such phenomena into those that occur above the earth and those below, then focuses on the first division—things formed in the atmosphere: vapor, rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost. He states that the primary cause for all these is ascending vapor. He then explains that the vapor rises and encounters the cold layer of the atmosphere. If the cold is not intense enough, the vapor may simply condense into dew if it does not freeze, or into frost if it does. The text continues with further details about conditions for rain and snow, which are partly corrupted. The key point is that the varying intensity of cold and the amount of vapor determine which phenomenon results. For example, in fiqh, the formation of rain (مطر) is relevant for determining the beginning of the rainy season for prayer times or agricultural rulings. The author's method here is empirical and causal, typical of the physical sciences within the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
بخار (bukhar): Vapor that rises from the earth, considered the material cause of atmospheric phenomena. [Note: In later physics, بخار may be distinguished from دخان (smoke); here it is generic.]
طبقة باردة (tabaqa barida): The cold layer of the atmosphere that causes condensation of vapor. [Note: No significant shift; standard in medieval Islamic meteorology.]
طل (tall): Dew, formed when vapor does not freeze. [Note: Stable term across texts.]
صقيع (saqi‘): Frost, formed when vapor freezes. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether the vapor is little or much
B) Whether the cold layer is strong or not, and whether the vapor freezes
C) Whether the wind blows
D) Whether the vapor rises above mountains

Key Takeaway
Atmospheric phenomena share a common material cause (vapor) and efficient cause (cold layer), with freezing determining dew vs frost.

The Prohibition of Saying 'Qaws Quzah'
Author reports hadith forbidding the term 'qaws quzah' for the rainbow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the reason for the prohibition of the term 'qaws quzah' and apply the principle of avoiding names associated with falsehood in Islamic speech.

Core Idea
Words matter: using a devil's name for a natural phenomenon is forbidden.

This brief hadith from Ibn Abbas illustrates a core Islamic principle: the words we use must not carry associations with falsehood or shirk. The rainbow is a natural sign of Allah's power, but the common term 'qaws quzah' contains a name linked to devils. The Prophet explicitly forbade its use. In this lesson, we'll examine the hadith and understand why linguistic choices are part of religious practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Read the hadith carefully. The Prophet says 'لا تقولوا قوس قزح' – 'Do not say qaws quzah.' This is an explicit prohibition (nahy).
The prohibition is on the expression, not the phenomenon.
2
Extract the reason
The hadith continues: 'فإن قزح من أسماء الشياطين' – 'for indeed quzah is one of the names of the devils.' The rationale is that the word 'quzah' belongs to a devil.
The reason is textual and specific to this term.
3
Clarify the scope
The prohibition is about the name, not the rainbow itself. Some mistakenly think the hadith forbids looking at or appreciating the rainbow. Distinguish between the natural phenomenon and the terminology.
Common misunderstanding: the prohibition is linguistic, not ontological.
4
Apply the principle
The broader principle is that Muslims should avoid any term that originates from pagan myths, polytheistic concepts, or glorifies falsehood. For example, avoid using names of pre-Islamic deities for months or days.
This principle is part of Islamic etiquette (adab) and maintaining tawheed in speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن ابن عباس أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: لا تقولوا قوس قزح فإن قزح من أسماء الشياطين.
From Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Do not say 'qaws quzah', for indeed quzah is one of the names of the devils.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith in which the Prophet Muhammad prohibits using the term 'qaws quzah' (rainbow) because 'quzah' is a name of a devil. The passage is a brief narration from Ibn Abbas. It functions as a critical lesson: the Muslim should avoid superstitious or pagan terminology for natural phenomena. The hadith is often cited in the context of Islamic etiquette and linguistics, reminding believers that names should not carry associations with falsehood or shirk.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because quzah is a name of a devil.
C) Because it is a non-Arabic word.
D) Because it is a pagan ritual.

Key Takeaway
The term 'qaws quzah' is prohibited because 'quzah' is a devil's name; Muslims must avoid words linked to shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting a Weak Causal Claim in Natural Philosophy
Razi refutes an astronomical-climatological claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two-pronged refutation strategy used by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the claim that the coldest places are near the winter tropic.

Core Idea
A weak causal link cannot survive when distance is negligible and motion is complex.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi critiques a natural philosophical claim about the coldest regions on Earth. He uses both an astronomical argument about negligible distance variation and a dynamic argument about solar motion and apogee. By examining his refutation, you will learn how to evaluate causal arguments in Islamic science, particularly how to test the strength of a proposed cause (ta'thir).

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Locate the statement: 'Among people are those who hold that the coldest places are near the tropic of winter because the sun is closer there.' This is the target claim.
The claimant asserts that proximity to the sun causes extreme cold, which contradicts common sense.
2
Extract the first counterargument
Razi argues that the distance between the center of the world (Earth) and the center of the sun's sphere is very small, so any variation in distance due to the tropic is negligible. Thus, proximity cannot be a significant cause.
This attacks the causal efficacy of the proposed factor.
3
Extract the second counterargument
Razi points out that the sun's motion and its apogee (point farthest from Earth) cause the hottest regions to shift throughout the year. Therefore, no fixed place is always the coldest.
This shows that the effect (extreme cold) is not constant, undermining the universal claim.
4
Evaluate the refutation's logic
Razi's critique is sound: if the proposed cause is negligible or if the effect is not uniquely tied to the cause, the causal claim fails. This demonstrates the principle that a weak 'illa (cause) cannot necessitate a hukm (ruling).
This mirrors the logical principle used in usul al-fiqh to test causal links.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخط الاستواء لم يخل قبل ذلك في جميع السنة من مثل هذا التخين أو ما هو أقوى منه بكثير. أما مثل هذا التخين فذلك عند كون الشمس في غاية الميل من الجانب الآخر، وأما الأقوى فذلك عند كونها في غاية البعد عن سمت رؤوس سكان ضعف الميل. فأسباب البرد الشديد كانت موجودة لهم في كل السنة. فالشتاء عند كون الشمس في غاية الميل الشمالي تكون الليالي طويلة فيكون البرد العظيم. وقد بينا أن حكم التاثير يفيد المادة استعدادًا لقبول الأثر. ومن الناس من يرى أن أشد المواضع برودة بالقرب من مدار المنقلب الشتوي لأن الشمس هناك أقرب. وهو أيضًا ضعيف لأن البعد بين مركز العالم ومركز الفلك الحامل للشمس قليل. وأيضًا فحركة الشمس متحركة وهي الآن في أوجها فإذا وصلت إلى الميزان كان الحنيف في الحمل فيكون حيد خط الاستواء في غاية التخونة فإذا وصل إلى الجدي كان مكان المتقلب الصيفي غاية التحرية فالاعتبار الذي ذاك لا يقضى تعين موضع ليكون في غاية البرودة.
The equator is not devoid before that in the entire year of such heat or something much stronger than it. As for such heat, that is when the sun is at the extreme obliquity on the other side. As for the stronger, that is when it is at the extreme distance from the zenith of the inhabitants of the region of double the obliquity. So the causes of severe cold were present for them throughout the year. For winter, when the sun is at the extreme northern obliquity, the nights become long, so there is great cold. And we have shown that the ruling of influence gives the matter a readiness to receive the effect. And among people are those who hold that the coldest places are near the tropic of winter because the sun is closer there. This is also weak because the distance between the center of the world and the center of the sphere carrying the sun is small. Also, the motion of the sun is moving, and it is now at its apogee. So when it reaches Libra, the heat is in Aries, so the region of the equator is in extreme heat. When it reaches Capricorn, the place of the summer tropic is in extreme heat. So the consideration mentioned does not necessitate determining a place to be at extreme cold.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a particular view about the coldest places on Earth. The author first establishes that the equator experiences moderate temperatures because the sun passes quickly overhead, and that regions at latitude twice the obliquity experience severe cold due to long nights. He then addresses the claim that the coldest places are near the winter tropic (Capricorn) because the sun is closer (in terms of distance). The author gives two counterarguments: first, the distance variation between the center of the world and the center of the sun's sphere is negligible; second, the sun's motion and its apogee/perigee affect which regions are hottest at different times. Therefore, says the author, the claimed consideration does not determine a particular place as the coldest.

Key Terms
خط الاستواء
المدار
الميل
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sun is furthest from the winter tropic.
B) The distance between the center of the world and the center of the sun's sphere is small.
C) The winter tropic receives direct sunlight.
D) The sun is at its apogee during winter.

Key Takeaway
A causal claim in natural philosophy can be refuted by showing that the proposed cause is negligible in magnitude or that other factors dominate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Unity of Bodily Faculties
Author refutes unity of bodily faculties using observation and dialectical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's refutation of the claim that bodily faculties are one power, and distinguish between weak and strong arguments in a critical philosophical context.

Core Idea
Observed mutual weakening of actions proves distinct faculties.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles a natural philosophical claim: that the four bodily faculties (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) are actually one power differing only by relation. He criticizes the standard argument against this claim and offers a stronger one based on observation. He then engages in a dialectical refutation of a possible counter-argument. Let's analyse his method step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Target Claim
Find the claim that the author is trying to refute: that the four bodily faculties are one power by nature.
The claim is stated in the lesson title and context.
2
Find the Weak Argument
The author mentions a 'well-known argument' that a single power cannot produce different actions. Note that he calls it weak.
This argument is weak because it assumes uniformity without empirical support.
3
Extract the Stronger Argument
The author presents his stronger argument: we observe that a single organ weakens in one action and strengthens in another, implying multiple powers.
This is an empirical observation, not a priori reasoning.
4
Analyze the Dialectical Exchange
The author anticipates an opponent's objection using a conditional dilemma: either the places for nutrition exist before food penetration or after. Identify each horn and the author's refutation.
The opponent tries to defend unity by arguing that faculties are distinguished by location, but the author shows contradictions in both possibilities.
5
Determine the Outcome
The author concludes that the opponent's dilemma is unsuccessful, thus the claim that the faculties are one power is refuted.
The refutation uses both empirical observation and logical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة المشهودة في ابطال هذا الأعمال ان القوة الواحدة لا تكون بدا الافعال المخلفة وهي ضعيفة. والاجود ان يقال: نرى العضو الواحد ضعيفان احد هذه الأفعال فوتاغ الافعال الباقي. ولو لا تعاني ها لاستحالة الكمال [؟]. فالاعتذار حاصل بظاهر الجسم والعمة [؟] والأغذية النافذة عمق المغتذي. فاما ان يقال تلك المواضع كانت موجودة عمق المغتذي قبل نفوذ الغذاء فيها او ما كانت. فان كانت الأول لزم ان يكون المعتدى مجموع اجزاء صغيرة بالغة كل واحد منها في الصغر الى حيث لا تحتريه مع ان شيئا [؟] بالاقى صاحبه فلا يكون هناك عضو واحد متصل بل اجزاء متناثرة. وان كانت الثانى نزم أن تكون الاعضاء الغذائية مفتقة لاتصال العضو لكن ذلك بالفعل بنا بر مجلة الأعضاء [؟]. ولا موجب للزوم فوجب ان يكون للألم [؟] ساريا في كل الأعضاء [؟].
The well-known argument in refuting this opinion is that a single power does not produce different actions, and it is weak. And the better is to say: we see that a single organ weakens in one of these actions and strengthens the remaining actions. If not for the mutual opposition [؟], perfection would be impossible [؟]. So the excuse is obtained by the apparent body and the general [؟] and the nutrients penetrating the depth of the nourished. Then either one says those places were present in the depth of the nourished before the penetration of the food or they were not. If the first, then it follows that the nourished is a collection of small parts each reaching such smallness that it does not contain it, while something [؟] adjacent to its companion, so there is not a single connected organ but scattered parts. And if the second, it follows that the nutritive organs are split for the connection of the organ, but that is actually by [؟] the totality of organs [؟]. And there is no necessity for the implication, so it must be that the pain [؟] is permeating all organs [؟].
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage presents a critical refutation of the claim that the four bodily faculties (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) are one power in essence, differing only in relation. The author first states the well-known argument against this: a single power cannot produce diverse actions. But this argument is considered weak. A stronger argument is then proposed: we observe that when one organ is weak in one action, it becomes strong in another; mutual opposition prevents perfection. The author then delves into a dialectical exchange. An opponent might argue that the attractive power is superficial while the nutritive power penetrates deeply, and that the places for nutrition exist either before or after food penetration. If they existed before, then the nourished body would be a collection of particles so small as to be imperceptible, not a continuous organ. If after, then the nutritive organs must separate to allow connection, which is not the case. Thus, the original claim is refuted. This passage exemplifies the use of logical reasoning (hujja) and dialectical method (jadal) in natural philosophy to disprove a philosophical position.

Key Terms
قوة
حجة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It relies on empirical observation that is not reliable.
B) It assumes that a single power cannot produce different actions without justification.
C) It is based on a logical fallacy.
D) It does not address the opponent's dilemma.

Key Takeaway
A single power cannot produce diverse actions is a weak argument; observation of mutual weakening provides a stronger refutation.

Eliminative Induction: Determining Causes in Physical and Juridical Reasoning
Physical explanation of why smooth surfaces cause light reflection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of elimination to isolate a cause in a given argument.

Core Idea
Elimination is a sound method for identifying causes when only one plausible factor remains.

In this lesson, we explore how an optical problem – the cause of reflection from a mirror – is solved by eliminating impossible factors until only smoothness remains. This method, called al-sabr wa al-taqsim in Usul al-Fiqh, is used to identify the effective cause ('illah) of a juristic ruling. By understanding this logical tool, you will see how Islamic scholars reason about causes in both natural and legal domains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The author asks: What causes a mirror to reflect light? He considers three candidates: solidity, smoothness, and 'difference'.
The question is causal – which property of the mirror produces reflection?
2
List the proposed causes
Three possible causes are named: صلابة (solidity), ملاستها (smoothness), and اختلافها (difference).
3
Test each cause through elimination
The author rejects solidity because water reflects despite not being solid. He rejects 'difference' on similar grounds (water also has difference? but it is not the cause). Only smoothness remains as the probable cause.
This is the core of eliminative induction – each counterexample eliminates a candidate.
4
Verify the remaining cause
The author then explains that rough surfaces, composed of many small angled surfaces, scatter light, whereas a smooth continuous surface reflects it in a focused manner. This confirms smoothness as the cause.
Verification involves showing how the cause produces the effect.
5
Apply the method to a new case
In Islamic jurisprudence, the same method is used to determine the 'illah (effective cause) of a ruling. For example, to find the 'illah for prohibiting riba, one eliminates possibilities like increase or deferment until the true cause is isolated.
This connects the physical example to Usul al-Fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Disjunctive Elimination of Causes for Reflection
Author disproves alternative causes of mirror reflection and concludes smoothness as the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a disjunctive syllogism used to eliminate alternative causes in a physical example from Islamic optics.

Core Idea
Reflection's cause is determined by disjunctive elimination, not mere observation.

In this passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas, the author seeks the true cause of mirror reflection. He lists three possible causes—hardness, smoothness, and an unclear third—and eliminates two, leaving smoothness. This is a textbook application of disjunctive syllogism in Islamic natural philosophy. By dissecting his reasoning, you will learn how to apply this logical tool to any scientific or juristic question where multiple causes are possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Find where the author explicitly lists the possible causes of reflection. The text says reflection is 'due to its hardness or its smoothness or its [?]'. This is a disjunction of three alternatives.
The third cause is unclear due to OCR corruption, but the structure remains.
2
Eliminate the false causes
The author refutes hardness by pointing to water: water reflects but is not hard. Similarly, he refutes the third cause. This elimination is based on empirical counterexamples.
Elimination requires that the disjunct be false; here it is shown by counterexample.
3
Conclude the remaining cause
After eliminating two alternatives, the author concludes that smoothness must be the cause. This is the valid inference of disjunctive syllogism: if a disjunction is true and all but one disjunct are false, the remaining one is true.
This step assumes the original disjunction was exhaustive.
4
Recognize the logical form
The argument is a disjunctive syllogism (qiyās munfaṣil) of the exclusive type (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw). Formally: either A, B, or C; not A; not C; therefore B.
This form is widely used in Islamic jurisprudence and kalam to determine causes and rulings.
5
Examine the elaboration
The author then explains how smooth surfaces cause reflection by considering the fineness of the light ray and the continuity of the surface. This shows that the conclusion is consistent with a deeper physical explanation.
The elaboration is not part of the syllogism but confirms its plausibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by elimination (tamyīz) to determine the cause of reflection from a mirror. The author lists three possible causes: hardness (ṣalāba), smoothness (malāsa), and a third unclear one. He refutes the first cause by pointing out that water is not hard yet reflects; similarly, the third cause is refuted. Therefore, the only remaining cause is smoothness. The author then elaborates that the incident ray from the eye is very fine and strikes the surface. If the surface is large and continuous (as in mirrors), the ray is reflected. He mentions crushed salt and other substances that have fine particles, indicating that even rough bodies can reflect if their surfaces are composed of many small smooth facets. The argument uses a disjunctive syllogism (qiyās munfaṣil) to eliminate false causes and establish the true cause.

Using Conditional Reasoning to Refute the Imprinting Theory of Vision
Author critiques imprinting theory using experimental objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional refutation of the imprinting theory and evaluate its validity using the experimental observation described.

Core Idea
The imprinting theory fails because it predicts double vision when focusing on a single object.

The author of this passage aims to refute the imprinting theory of vision, which claimed that the form of an object imprints on the crystalline humor of each eye and then merges in the common sense to produce single vision. The refutation proceeds by conditional reasoning: if the theory were true, certain consequences would follow, but experiment shows these consequences do not hold. This lesson guides you through the logical structure of that refutation, showing how empirical observation can be combined with logical inference to falsify a theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponents' claim
The imprinting theory states that the image first imprints on the crystalline humor in each eye, then through the hollow optic nerve a cone of rays carries it to the common sense where the two images merge into one. This is the thesis under attack.
Look at the beginning of the passage where the author reports the theory.
2
Extract the conditional argument
The author argues: if the imprinting theory were true, then when we look at a single object we would see it as one, because the images merge. But experience shows that when we focus on a near object, the far object appears double. This counterexample shows that the merging does not happen universally.
The conditional is: If imprinting theory, then all objects appear single when focusing on one. The observation of a far object appearing double (while focusing on a near object) contradicts the consequent, thus the antecedent is false.
3
Recognize the logical form
This is a modus tollens argument: If P (imprinting theory is true), then Q (a far object will appear single when focusing on a near object). Not Q (it appears double). Therefore, not P (the theory is false).
Modus tollens is a valid form of conditional reasoning.
4
Assess the validity
The reasoning is logically valid. The empirical observation (Q being false) is the crucial premise. If the observation is accurate, the conclusion follows necessarily. This demonstrates how a logical tool can undermine a scientific theory.
The author invites the reader to test the experiment themselves ('Try it yourself to ascertain it').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the imprinting (inti'ā') theory of vision. According to this theory, the image from the external object first imprints on the crystalline humor (jalidiyya) in each eye, then a cone of rays carries it through the hollow optic nerve to the common sense, where the two images merge into one. The author argues against this, showing that if imprinting occurred, then when looking at one object, we would see it double (as with the two hands). The proponents try to explain single vision by the meeting of cones, but the author raises an experimental objection: when you look at a near object, the far object appears double, and vice versa. This demonstrates that the imprints cannot be single, and thus the theory fails. The passage is a classic example of a logical refutation using empirical observation.

Epistemology of Visual Perception: Colour and Light
Epistemological investigation of visual perception objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument that colour and light are not perceived instantaneously and identify the rotating disc evidence.

Core Idea
Colour and light require time to be perceived; the eye blends rapid inputs into a composite sensation.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas examines the objects of sight. He argues that we perceive colour as a general quality before specific colours, and that colour as such is not instantaneous. He uses the example of a rotating multicoloured disc to show that perceived colour can be a temporal blend. Understanding these epistemological claims is crucial for later discussions in Islamic optics and the theory of perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven investigations
The author mentions 'seven investigations' (المباحث السبعة) which structure the inquiry. While not all are enumerated here, note that the passage focuses on colour, light, points, lines, surfaces, magnitude, and distance.
This structure is specific to this work.
2
Analyse the primacy of general colour
Al-Razi claims that we first perceive colour as a general quality before specific colours (e.g., we see coloured before seeing it is black). Why? Because the more general is more known.
This is an epistemological principle: the general is prior in knowledge.
3
Examine the rotating disc argument
The author describes a disc with many coloured lines rotating quickly. We see a single composite colour. He explains it is because each point stimulates the same retinal spot over a perceptible time, mixing the colours.
This shows colour perception is not instantaneous but requires duration.
4
Understand the visibility of extremities
He argues that points, lines, and surfaces are visible because we can compare sizes (e.g., a date and a bean). But this requires contrast and is not perceived in dense bodies without gaps.
This relates to the concept of 'accidental' vs 'essential' visibility.
5
Synthesize the epistemological stance
Al-Razi's analysis demonstrates that sensory perception involves temporal and cognitive processing, not mere passive reception. This sets the stage for later debates in kalam about the nature of perception and the reliability of the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هنا مباحث سبعة. النظر في الجنس كالضوء واللون، أو في النوع كالسواد والبياض. إدراك اللون بما هو لون يقدم على إدراك اللون المخصوص؛ لأن الأعم أعرف، ولأن الحجم الملون بالألوان المظلمة كالكمالي والأغبر يدرك لونه الخاص بعد إدراك اللون المطلق. وأما الثاني فلأن امتيازه عن غيره بحكم يدركه العقل بعد إبصار ذلك اللون. واللون بما هو لون والضوء بما هو ضوء لا يكونان في آنٍ زمانٍ، لأن التواتر إذا خرج من مركزها إلى محيطها خطوط كثيرة بألوان مختلفة ثم أديرت بسرعة شديدة، فات الناظر حالة تدرك له لونا واحدا مخالفا لون الخطوط، وكان لونا مركبا من جميع تلك الألوان. وذلك لأن كل نقطة منها تقع في موضع واحد زمانا محسوسا، فلا يتاح الإحساس بها موضعا خاصة، بل تختلط تلك الألوان، ولولا الزمان المقدر لعتبة الابصار لما كان الأمر كذلك. وقيل: إدراك اللون بما هو لون والضوء بما هو ضوء لا يكون في آن زمان، لأن الكرة إذا فتحت، نرى الضوء الآن الذي هو أول زمان فتحها، وتوفى الألوان قبل ذلك، لكن لا يزول الثاني عن النقطة والخط، وهما لا يقبلان الضوء. والضوء والكون المحسوس بما هو محسوس إنما يدركان من زمان فتحا. وذلك يوجد في المشهودات. القوة الباصرة تتعلق بالشيء من حيث إنها بالآفة المحلية، وفيه إشكال: أنا إذا نظرنا إلى شيئين مطلقا، فإنه يشتبه أخذها بالآخر، والاشتباه يكون لعدم الشعور به، مخالف أحدهما لآخر، لكن كل واحد منهما مخالف صاحبه من حيث هو هو، فالقوة الباصرة متعلقة به من حيث هو، لا من حيث وقع. ثم اظهر أن الأطراف وهي النقط والخطوط والسطوح مرئية، لأنا ندرك الفرقة بالحنين العظم والصغر، وما ذاك إلا الإحساس بأن سطح أحدهما أعظم من سطح الآخر، ولكن الإحساس بها مشروط بالإحساسين التمر والفول، ولذلك لا نرى هذه الأشياء في الأجسام الكثيفة. وأما المنكرون لوجودها فانكارهم لكونها عينية أولى جب. أما ما عدا الثلثة المذكورة أعني الصور واللون والطلاق، في لحنها لغير مرئي. أما المقدار فلات المحور لنا من الحجم، إما التعلم الواحد أو التطوخ المحطة به. وأما المقدار الذي هو حشو ما بين السطوح فلا شك أنه غير مرئي، ولذلك لا تحس على كون مجوفا أو مصمتا. وأما البعد فقلت لك أيضا لا نعرفه إلا بتوسط المتباعدين كما ملونا، ولا نعرف تباعدهما إلا بذلك، ولذلك علمنا بهذه الطريقة الكواكب.
Then here are seven investigations. Consideration in genus like light and colour, or in species like black and white. The perception of colour as colour precedes the perception of particular colour, because the more general is more known, and because a volume coloured with dark colours like liver-colour and dust-colour is perceived in its specific colour after perceiving absolute colour. As for the second, because its distinction from others by a ruling that the intellect perceives after seeing that colour. Colour as colour and light as light do not exist in an instant of time, because when radiation goes out from its center to its circumference with many lines of different colours and then is rotated very quickly, the observer misses the state that perceives for it a single colour different from the colour of the lines, and it becomes a colour composed of all those colours. That is because each point of it falls in a single place in a perceptible time, so it is not possible to sense it in a special place, but those colours mix, and were it not for the time measured by the sight threshold, the matter would not be so. And it is said: the perception of colour as colour and light as light does not occur in an instant of time, because when the sphere is opened, we see the light now which is the first time of its opening, and colours are presented before that, but the second does not cease from the point and line, and they do not receive light. Light and the sensible being as sensible are only perceived from the time of opening. And that is found in observed things. The visual faculty is attached to a thing insofar as it is by local defect, and there is an objection: when we look at two things absolutely, it is ambiguous taking one for the other, and ambiguity arises from lack of awareness of it, each one is different from its companion in its essence, so the visual faculty is attached to it as it is, not as it occurs. Then it is evident that the extremities, i.e., points, lines, and surfaces, are visible, because we perceive the difference by the longing for greatness and smallness, and that is only sensation that the surface of one is greater than the surface of the other, but sensation of them is conditional on the two sensations of the date and the bean, and therefore we do not see these things in dense bodies. As for those who deny their existence, their denial because they are accidental is more appropriate. As for other than the three mentioned, i.e., images, colour, and smoothness, in their nuance they are not visible. As for magnitude, we do not have axis from volume, either single learning or the peripheral contact. And as for magnitude which is the fill between surfaces, there is no doubt it is not visible, and therefore you do not sense whether it is hollow or solid. And as for distance, I told you also that we only know it by mediation of two distant things as coloured, and we do not know their distance except by that, and therefore we know the stars by this method.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the objects of visual perception. The author begins by mentioning seven investigations (المباحث السبعة) which are likely the themes under which the visual faculty is examined. He then focuses on the perception of colour and light, making a key claim: we first perceive colour as a general quality before we perceive specific colours like black or white. This is because the more general is more knowable. For example, we see a coloured volume as coloured before discerning it is liver-colour or dust-colour. The second point is that distinction of a colour from others is an intellectual judgement after seeing. Then he argues that colour as colour and light as light are not perceived instantaneously; rather, they require duration. He illustrates with a rotating multicoloured disc: when it spins fast, we see a single blended colour, not the individual colours, because each point affects the same retinal spot over a perceptible time, mixing the colours. If the rotation were faster than the eye's temporal resolution, we would not see the blend. He also mentions a spherical object: when opened, we first see light, then colours appear, but the second (the distinct colour) does not vanish instantly because points and lines do not receive light directly. Then he discusses the visibility of extremities: points, lines, and surfaces. We perceive differences in size by comparing surfaces; but such perception requires contrast, like comparing a date and a bean. Dense bodies do not allow seeing these extremities. Deniers of their existence might argue they are accidental. Then he lists three visible qualities: images (الصور), colour (اللون), and smoothness (الطلاق). Other qualities like magnitude in the sense of volume axis or the space between surfaces are not visible; we cannot see if a body is hollow or solid. Distance is known only by mediating between two coloured distant objects; thus we estimate distances of stars.

Key Terms
اللون
الضوء
المباحث السبعة

Key Takeaway
Visual perception of colour and light is temporally extended; the mind blends rapid sensory inputs into a unified experience.

Refutation of the claim that the intellect is limited to finite acts
Author refutes an objection that the intellect, being material, cannot have infinite acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical structure of a dialectical refutation in Islamic epistemology, identifying the two meanings of 'infinite acts' and evaluating the author's argument against the materiality of the intellect.

Core Idea
The intellect's ability to have infinite acts does not prove it is material; material powers act only while they exist, but the intellect persists.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's refutation of the claim that the intellect, being material, cannot have infinite acts. He dissects the objection into two interpretations and shows that even material powers can act after any limit as long as they exist, so the intellect's persistence implies it is immaterial. This is a classic example of dialectical reasoning in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
Find the initial claim that the author is attacking. The objector asserts that the rational power (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) cannot have infinite acts because it is material.
The objection is based on the idea that material powers are limited.
2
Extract the author's initial rejection
The author begins by stating that no proof is provided for the premise that the intellect is limited. He then argues that intellectual forms, when realized in a subject, become particularized by accidents, so they are not universal as the objector claims.
This counters the assumption that universal forms are inherently abstract.
3
Examine the two meanings of 'infinite acts'
The author distinguishes two interpretations: (1) performing many acts simultaneously – which he refutes by noting human difficulty in attending to many objects at once; (2) being able to act after any limit – which he shows is also true for material powers like the nature of earth.
This shows that the second meaning does not distinguish the intellect from matter.
4
Understand the key distinction: persistence vs. cessation
The author argues that material powers act only while they exist, but they eventually cease. The intellect, being immaterial, persists. Therefore, its ability to have infinite acts (in the serial sense) does not make it material; rather, it is evidence of immateriality.
The crux is that material powers are temporary, while the intellect is enduring.
5
Evaluate the refutation's structure
Identify the dialectical moves: the objector's claim is dissected into two senses, each refuted, and a decisive distinction is drawn. This structure is common in jadal (debate) and strengthens the author's position.
The refutation is successful because it addresses the ambiguity in the objector's terms.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا ما لا تدليك على هذه المقدمة ثم أنها منقوصة عا من بكميات الحجم الملون المفتى تكون سطحه موقوفا باللون والصودُون عقد مع أن التطرف فقد اسفل الطرف هنا بالقابلية واذا جان ذلك قلم الحون مثله ع مسلنا ملتاعة الشابة فالراحة أصدر عالما لفا لانسار ان للعقول بحب حصول ما هيت من العاقل على ما مر سلمنا ذلك لكنا نقول الصحة الكلية اذا حصلت في الفر التي ستونها لا تكون كلبة ولا مجردة عن العوارض لانها صورة شخصية موصوفة بعوارض شخصية من حلولها نے تلک الضر وحدوثهاغ وقت معين وعدم قيامها بنفسها إلى غير ذلك من المشخصات فبطل قولكم العمر العقلية كلية الفاك المعنى بكونها مجردة إن تلك الصورة نظرا إلى كا هيتنا مع قطع النظر عن العوارض التي ذاعتها مجلة فالقول قلم /المجون أن تكون تلك المتحدة حالة الجسم ثم انها تكون مجردة عا معنى إنها بالنظر إلى حقيقتها مع قطع النظر عن العوارض العارضة لها بسبب حصولها فى الجسم مُحتر عن جميع العوارض ثم التحقيق ما ذكرناه في باب العقل انا احية الا انه مانا انصار عليه الانسلم ان القوة العاملة تقوى والفعل اصلا فضلا عن أن يقال انها تقوية الافعال غير متناهية لان التحق عبارة عن قبول الفير الصورة العقلية وهذا الفعال لانعك والانفعالات الغير المتناهية هائزة على الجمانيات لمكان النفوس الفلكية وهيولى الاجواء العنصرية سلمناورتها الفعل لكن الذي تريدون بقوكم القوة العاقلة لقوى الفعال غير متناهية أن عنيتم انها تقوى على أن فعل الوقت الواحد افعا العرفاهية منو باطل انحن بعد من النا إنه يصعب علينا توجيه الذهب عن معلمات كثيرة دفعة واحد وان علم انها لا تنتمى الى حد الا وتكون قادرة بعد ذلك على الفعل فالامر في القوى الجمانية ايضا لذلك فإن القوة الخائية انتمى في نفقة الاشمال الى حد الا وهي تقوى على نصت اشكال اخرى وكنا طبيعة الارض لا تنتمى في التكين الى وقت الأوهى تقوى على التكين بعده والجواب لكم عن ذلك الأفق لكم ان هذه القوى متى كانت باقية كانت قوية على الأفعال لكة بعد انتهاءها إلى العدم لا محالة.
We said: You have no proof for this premise. Moreover, it is deficient in that the volume of the colored body [؟] its surface is dependent on color and [؟] while the extremity is here a potential extremity. If that is the case, [؟] we accept [؟] but we say: The universal form when it exists in a particular subject is neither universal nor free from accidents, because it is a particular image described by particular accidents from its occurrence in that subject, its temporally specific origination, and its lack of self-subsistence, and other individuating attributes. Thus your claim that the intellectual forms are universal because they are abstract is false; rather, that form, considered in terms of its essence without regard to the accidents that attend it, is [؟]. So the statement that [؟] it is abstract means that by its essence, disregarding the accidents that befall it due to its existence in the body, it is purified of all accidents. Then the truth is what we mentioned in the chapter on the intellect: [؟] except that [؟] we concede that the active power [؟] acts at all, let alone that it has infinite acts, because intellection is the reception of the intellectual form, and this [؟] and infinite passivities occur in material entities due to celestial souls and the matter of elemental [؟]. We grant that [؟] but what do you mean by saying 'the rational power has infinite acts'? If you mean that it is able to perform in a single moment acts that are [؟], that is false because we find it difficult to direct the mind to many objects at once. And if you mean that it does not reach a limit without being able to act thereafter, then the same holds for material powers: the [؟] power does not reach a limit in [؟] without being able to produce other forms, and the nature of earth does not reach a time in [؟] without being able to generate afterward. The answer to you about that: [؟] that these powers, as long as they persist, are able to act, but after they cease to exist they cannot; and if that is so, it is not true that the rational power can have infinite acts in the sense that it is able to act as long as it persists, even if its termination to non-existence is conceded, then it would follow that it can perform infinite acts; but it is not necessary that a power that is capable of infinite acts be material.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of an objection regarding the nature of the intellect. The author begins by rejecting a premise of the objector, stating that no proof is provided. Then he argues against the claim that intellectual forms when realized in a particular subject would still be universal and abstract; rather, they become particularized by accidents. The objector had claimed that the intellect as a material power cannot have infinite acts. The author distinguishes two meanings: (1) performing many acts simultaneously—which is false, as we cannot attend to many things at once; (2) being able to act after any limit—this is also true for material powers, e.g., the power of earth to generate after each period. The answer is that material powers only act while they exist, but they eventually cease; the intellect, being immaterial, persists. Thus, the claim that the intellect is material because it could have infinite acts is invalid. The author uses an example from natural philosophy (nature of earth) to illustrate the point.

Key Terms
القوة العاقلة
الصورة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the intellect's ability to have infinite acts not prove it is material?
A) Because material powers cannot have infinite acts at all.
B) Because material powers can only act while they exist, but cease to exist, while the intellect persists.
C) Because the intellect is identical to the body.
D) Because the intellect's acts are finite in number.

Key Takeaway
The intellect's capacity for infinite acts is not evidence of materiality because material powers also have potential for serial acts, but only the intellect persists immaterially.

Intellectual Pain and the Afterlife: An Epistemological Critique
Author reports and critiques a philosophical argument about intellectual misery in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the analogical argument for intellectual pain being greater than bodily pain and identify the epistemic condition (awareness of loss) required for such pain to occur.

Core Idea
Pain requires awareness of loss; ignorance of ignorance may spare one from eternal torment.

The author presents a claim that unfulfilled intellectual desire causes the greatest pain, even beyond physical suffering. But he subjects this claim to a subtle critique: the pain of ignorance can only occur if one knows one is ignorant. This lesson unpacks both the argument and its underlying epistemological condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being made
Find the initial assertion about the person who desires knowledge but never attains it. What is the outcome according to the philosophers?
Look for the term 'shaqāwa' (misery) and the characterization of the unfulfilled seeker.
2
Extract the analogical argument
How do the philosophers compare intellectual and bodily pain? They use an analogy based on pleasures. Find the key sentence that states the ratio.
The analogy is: as the two pleasures relate, so the two pains relate.
3
Find the hidden premise
What condition must hold for pain to arise from unmet desire? The author introduces a distinction: the desirer must know they have not reached the object. Locate the example of Zayd.
The example shows that if you mistakenly believe you've reached what you desire, you feel no pain.
4
Apply the condition to the afterlife
Consider an ignorant person who holds false beliefs but thinks they are true. After death, if that false belief persists, does the person feel pain? Why or why not?
The author argues that pain requires awareness of loss, which is absent if the person believes they already possess knowledge.
5
Assess the author's conclusion
What is the final verdict? Can we be certain of eternal intellectual torment? The author says 'it is not possible to be certain of pain.' How does he justify this?
He suggests that false beliefs might even transform into knowledge after death, but that's not necessary for his point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فزعموا انه صاحب الشقاوة العظيمة لانه يشتاق إلى معرفة الأشياء وغير واصل اليها ولا مشغول بعدها وكل من كان كذلك كان معذبا يا لضرورة لان الشوق إلى العلم مع عدم التمكن من تحصيله وعدم الاشتغال بغير حتة ينهل عنه يوجب الالم. واحتجوا على ان هذا الالم اقوى من الالم الجثماني بان نسبة الالمين كنسبة اللذتين. فلما كانت اللذة العقلية اقوى كان الالم العقلي اقوى. وانما يتصور المشتاق الى شيء غير واصل اليه انما يكون معلوما لو علم انه غير واصل اليه. اما اذا جرى بكونه واصلا اليها لكن معتباقات المشتاق إلى زيد مع انه لا يعرفه بصورته اذا وصل الى إنسان يعتقده زيد بلند بوصوله إليه. فهذا الجاهل قبلان كاتت عنده اعتقادات هي جهالات لكنه كان يعتقد فيها كونها معلومة فيكون ذلك الاعتقاد ايضا جهلا. فاذا مات فان يبقى اعتماد من كون تلك الاعتقادات معلومة ولا بقى فان بقى لم تتصد بفقدان العلوم لما ينا ان فقد المطلوب لنا يوجب الالم لو حصل الشعور بفقده. وهذا ما حصل الشعور بفقده لان الشعور بفقد العلوم لا يحصل مع اعتماد حصول العلوم. وانما بق ذلك الاعتقالي فقد انقلب ذكر اعمك بعد الموت علما. واذا جاز ذلك في البعض ان ايضاف الباغ حتى للجميع بلك احتمالات بالعلوم ومع هذا التون لايمكن القطع بالألم.
They claimed that he is the possessor of great misery because he desires to know things but does not reach them nor is occupied with other things. And everyone who is like that is necessarily tormented, because the desire for knowledge with inability to attain it and not being occupied with anything else necessitates pain. And they argued that this pain is stronger than bodily pain by saying that the ratio of the two pains is like the ratio of the two pleasures. Since intellectual pleasure is stronger, intellectual pain is stronger. The desirer of something not reaching it is only conceivable if he knows that he does not reach it. But if he imagines that he has reached it, like the desire for Zayd while he does not know his form when he reaches a person he believes is Zayd but is not reaching him. So this ignorant person had beliefs that are ignorances, but he believed them to be known, so that belief is also ignorance. When he dies, then the belief that those beliefs are known either remains or does not. If it remains, he does not perceive the loss of knowledge because the perception of loss of knowledge does not occur with the belief of possessing knowledge. And if that belief remains, then those ignorances become knowledge after death. And if that is possible in some, it is possible in all, so with this it is not possible to be certain of pain.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is an argument about intellectual pain and its relation to the afterlife. The author reports a claim: some philosophers assert that a person who desires knowledge but never attains it, and is not occupied with anything else, is in great misery. This is because unfulfilled desire causes pain. They further argue that intellectual pain is greater than bodily pain, since the ratio of pains corresponds to the ratio of pleasures; intellectual pleasure is greater, so intellectual pain is greater. The author then raises a subtle point: for the pain of not reaching a desired object to occur, the person must know that they have not reached it. But if they mistakenly believe they have reached it, like someone who desires to meet Zayd but meets another person they think is Zayd, the pain does not occur. Similarly, an ignorant person who holds false beliefs but believes them to be true does not feel the pain of ignorance. After death, if that false belief persists, they may not feel any pain. In fact, the false beliefs might even become knowledge if they persist. Therefore, it is not certain that such a person suffers pain after death. This critique undermines the claim that intellectual frustration necessarily causes eternal torment.

Key Terms
شقاوة (shaqāwa): Misery or wretchedness, especially in the afterlife due to lack of intellectual fulfillment.
علم (ʿilm): Knowledge, especially certain knowledge of intelligible things.
ألم عقلي (alam ʿaqlī): Intellectual pain, arising from unfulfilled intellectual desire.
لذة عقلية (ladhdha ʿaqliyya): Intellectual pleasure, the highest form of pleasure.
نفس (nafs): Soul, which after death retains its beliefs and desires. [Note: In this context, the soul is rational and survives the body.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why might an ignorant person not feel intellectual pain after death?
A) Because they no longer have desires.
B) Because they still believe their false beliefs are true.
C) Because the body is gone.
D) Because they attain knowledge after death.

Key Takeaway
The pain of ignorance requires awareness of ignorance; without that awareness, eternal intellectual torment is not guaranteed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an analogical argument using an Islamic example: compare the pleasure of a person who prays with devotion to the pleasure of a person who eats delicious food. Then argue which loss is more painful, and check whether the pain requires awareness of the loss (e.g., if the devotee is distracted, they may not feel the pain of missing prayer).
?
Identify the epistemic condition in the following scenario: A student believes he has mastered a subject but actually is ignorant. According to the lesson, will he feel the pain of ignorance after death? Explain.
